Conditions of a Sound Definition: Universal, Converse, Clear, Not Remote
Al-Bajuri presents the four conditions of definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the four conditions of a definition (mutarid, mun'akis, zahir, la ab'ad) and apply them to a given definition.

Core Idea
A definition must cover all instances and only those instances, be clearer than the definiendum, and not be more obscure.

The matn states four conditions that apply to every definition: it must be universal (mutarid), converse (mun'akis), clear (zahir), and not remote (la ab'ad). Al-Bajuri begins by noting that his teacher considers these conditions applicable to verbal definitions as well. In this lesson, we unpack each condition and see why they are essential for a definition to be useful.

Guided Path
1
Understand mutarid (universal)
The definition must include all instances of the definiendum. For example, if you define 'horse' as 'a four-legged animal', it includes all horses but also other animals, so it is universal but not converse.
2
Understand mun'akis (converse)
The definition must apply only to instances of the definiendum. It must exclude everything else. Using the same example, 'a four-legged animal' is not converse because it includes cows.
3
Understand zahir (clear) and la ab'ad (not remote)
The definition must be more evident and well-known than the definiendum. It should not introduce an unfamiliar or ambiguous term.
The text says 'clear, not remote'.
4
Apply the conditions to a fiqh example
Consider defining 'ghusl' as 'pouring water over the entire body with the intention of purification'. Check if it covers all instances (when is ghusl required?) and if it excludes non-instances (e.g., ordinary bathing).

Source Text

(٤٤) وَمَا بِـ «لَفْظِيّ» لَدَيْهِمْ شُهِرَا تَبْدِيلُ لَفْظُ بِرَدِيفٍ أَشْـهَــرَا
الباجوري قوله: (وما بـ «لفظي»... إلخ) أَيْ : وَالَّذِي شُهِرَ عِنْدَهُمْ بِـ المُعَرِّفِ اللَّفْظِيّ... إلخ»، «مَا اسْمٌ مَوْصُولٌ ، وَشُهِرَ) صِلَتُهَا، وَلَدَيْهِمْ بِمَعْنَى : عِنْدَهُمْ ، ظَرْفٌ لِتِلْكَ الصَّلَةِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (تَبْدِيلُ ... إلخ) فِيهِ تَسَامُح ; لِأَنَّ المُعَرِّفَ اللَّفْظِيَّ لَيْسَ نَفْسَ التَّبْدِيلِ، بَلِ اللَّفْظُ الَّذِي أَتَى بِهِ بَدَلاً ; إِذِ التَّعَارِيفُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ الأَلْفَاظِ (۳) ، وَذَلِكَ كَأَنْ يُقَالَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ «البُرِّ»: «هُوَ : القَمْحُ».
(٤٥) وَشَرْطُ كُلِّ: أَنْ يُرَى مُطَرِدَا» «مُنْعَكِساً»، و«ظَاهِراً» لَا أَبْعَدَا
وَقَوْلُهُ : (بِرَدِيفٍ) أَيْ : بِمُرَادِفٍ، فَهُوَ فَعِيلٌ ; بِمَعْنَى : مُفَاعِلٍ . وَقَوْلُهُ: (أَشْهَرًا) أَيْ: عِنْدَ السَّامِعِ ، وَاحْتَرَزَ بِذَلِكَ عَنِ : الرَّدِيفِ الأَخْفَى، أَوِ المُسَاوِي، كَمَا هُوَ ظَاهِرٌ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَشَرْطُ كُلِّ ... إلخ) ظَاهِرُ كَلَامِهِ : اعْتِبَارُ مَا ذَكَرَهُ مِنَ الشُّرُوطِ فِي اللَّفْظِيّ كَغَيْرِهِ. وَتَعَقَّبَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ : بِأَنَّهُ لَا مَعْنَى لِاشْتِرَاطِ هَذِهِ الأُمُورِ فِيهِ; لأَنَّهُ لَا يُعْقَلُ تَخَلَّفُ شَيْءٍ مِنْهَا عَنْهُ; إِذْ لَا يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ لَفْظُ الرَّدِيفِ الأَشْهَرِ غَيْرَ جَامِعِ وَلَا غَيْرَ مَانِعِ ; لِأَنَّ مَدْلُولَهُ عَيْنُ مَدْلُولِ اللَّفْظِ غَيْرِ الأَشْهَرِ، وَلَا يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ دُونَ المُعَرَّفِ وَلَا مُسَاوِياً ; لِأَنَّ الفَرَضَ أَنَّهُ أَشْهَرُ مِنْهُ، وَلَا مَجَازاً ; لأَنَّ المَجَازَ وَالحَقِيقَةَ لَيْسَا مُتَرَادِفَيْنِ، وَلَا يُمْكِنُ أَيْضاً دُخُولُ الدَّورِ فِيهِ، كَمَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ ابْنُ قَاسِمِ فِي الآيَاتِ، وَهَكَذَا البَاقِي. اهـ وَهُوَ وَجِيه. لَكِنْ نَاقَشَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَهَكَذَا البَاقِي» بـ : أَنَّهُ يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ اللَّفْظُ الأَشْهَرُ مُشْتَرَكاً بَيْنَ مَعْنَى رَدِيفِهِ غَيْرِ الأَشْهَرِ وَبَيْنَ مَعْنى آخَرَ (۱) ، وَبِهَذَا يُعْلَمُ مَا فِي قَوْلِهِ : «لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُعْقَلُ تَخَلُّفُ شَيْءٍ مِنْهَا عَنْهُ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
الأنبابي :
قوله : (لَكِنْ نَاقَشَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ ... إلخ) فيه نظر؛ لأنَّه إذا لم يكن الآخر مشتركاً لم يكن رديفاً، وعلى تسليم أنَّه رديف إذا كان المشترك خالياً مِنَ القرينة كان غير ظاهر، وهو خلاف الفرض، وإن كان كلٌّ منهما مشتركاً بين معنيين؛ إلَّا أنَّ أحدهما أشهر فيهما ، والآخر خفيًا فيهما ، لم يمتنع التعريف بالمشترك؛ لأنَّ محلَّ منعه إذا لم يرد به جميع معانيه، وهنا قد أريد به معنياه معاً، وإن كان أحدهما أشهر في معنى من معنيه، والآخر خفيًا فيهما ، كانتِ الشُّهرة في هذا المعنى قرينة على إرادة تعريف الآخر بالنسبة لأحد معنييه، وإن كان كل منهما مشتهراً في معنى غير ما اشتهر فيه الآخر، لم يصح التّعريف; لعدم الشُّهرة مِنَ الجهة التي قصد بها التعريف، فتأمل.
(1) الشرشيمي : قوله : (وَبَيْنَ مَعْنى آخَرَ ) أي : كما إذا وضع لفظ «القمح» مثلاً على ابن زيد» مثلاً، ونظر فيه بعضهم: بأنَّه لا ترادف; إذ المتبادر منه التّساوي من كل الوجوه وقوله فيما سبق : (وَلَا يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ دُونَ المُعَرَّفِ) أي : دونه الظهور، وهو المعبّر عنه في المتن بقوله: «لَا أَبْعَدًا».
(44) And that which is known among them as 'verbal' is the replacement of a word by a more famous synonym. Al-Bajuri: His words: 'And that which... etc.' Meaning: that which is well-known among them as the verbal definer... etc. 'Ma' is a relative noun, 'shuhira' is its relative clause, and 'ladayhim' means 'among them', an adverb for that clause. His words: 'replacement... etc.' contains looseness, because the verbal definer is not the replacement itself, but the word that is brought as a substitute, since definitions are of the type of words. And that is like when it is said in defining 'al-burr': 'It is: al-qamh'. (45) And the condition of every (definition) is that it be seen as universal, converse, and clear, not remote. And his words: 'by a synonym' i.e., by a synonymous word, so it is fa'il in the sense of mufa'il. And his words: 'more famous' i.e., to the hearer, and by that he excludes the less famous or equal synonym, as is apparent. His words: 'and the condition of every... etc.' The apparent meaning of his speech is the consideration of what he mentioned of the conditions in the verbal (definition) as in other (definitions). And some objected to him: that there is no meaning to stipulating these matters in it, because it is inconceivable that any of them be absent from it; since it is impossible for the word of the more famous synonym to be non-universal or non-exclusive, because its meaning is identical to the meaning of the less famous word; and it is impossible for it to be less than the definiendum or equal, because the assumption is that it is more famous than it; and it is impossible for it to be metaphorical, because the metaphorical and the literal are not synonyms; and it is also impossible for circularity to enter into it, as Ibn Qasim stated in (his commentary on) al-Ayat, and likewise the rest. End. And it is sound. However, some of the verifiers criticized him regarding his statement 'and likewise the rest', saying that it is possible for the more famous word to be polysemous between the meaning of its less famous synonym and another meaning. And by this it is known what is in his statement 'because it is inconceivable that any of them be absent from it', so let it be considered. Al-Anbabi: His words: 'However, some verifiers criticized... etc.' There is a consideration in it; because if the other is not polysemous, it is not a synonym. And granting that it is a synonym, if the polysemous word is free from a contextual indicator, it is not clear, contrary to the assumption. And if each of them is polysemous between two meanings, except that one of them is more famous in both, and the other is obscure in both, definition by the polysemous is not prevented, because the place of its prevention is when not all its meanings are intended; and here both meanings are intended together. And if one of them is more famous in one of its two meanings and the other is obscure in both, the fame in that meaning is an indicator of the intention to define the other with respect to one of its two meanings. And if each of them is famous in a meaning different from what the other is famous for, the definition is not valid, due to lack of fame from the aspect intended by the definition. So reflect. (1) Al-Shirshimi: His words: 'and another meaning' i.e., as if the word 'al-qamh' were placed on 'Zayd's son' for example. And some considered it, saying that there is no synonymy, since the immediate understanding is equality from all aspects; and his statement earlier 'and it is impossible for it to be less than the definiendum' means 'less in clarity', which is expressed in the text by 'not remote'.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage defines the verbal definition (al-ta'rif al-lafzi) and discusses its conditions. The matn first states that a verbal definition is replacing a word with a more famous synonym. Al-Bajuri explains that the definer is not the act of replacement but the substitute word itself, as definitions are linguistic. He gives an example: defining 'al-burr' (wheat) as 'al-qamh' (another word for wheat). The matn then lists conditions for all definitions: being universal (mutarid), converse (mun'akis), clear (zahir), and not remote (la ab'ad). Al-Bajuri notes that some scholars objected that these conditions are automatically satisfied for verbal definitions because the synonym has the exact same meaning, so it cannot lack universality or exclusivity, cannot be less famous or equal, cannot be metaphorical, and cannot involve circularity. However, a verifier raised a nuanced objection: the more famous synonym might be polysemous (mushtarak), having another meaning besides the intended one. In that case, the definition would be ambiguous and not clear. Al-Anbabi then provides a detailed analysis of various scenarios of polysemy and fame. He argues that if the polysemous word has no contextual indicator, it lacks clarity. If both words are polysemous and one is more famous in both meanings, defining by it is permissible if both meanings are intended. If one is more famous in one meaning only, that fame can serve as a contextual indicator. But if each is famous in a different meaning, the definition fails due to lack of relevant fame. This refined analysis shows that the conditions are not automatically satisfied in verbal definitions and require careful consideration.

Key Terms
مطرد
منعكس
ظاهر
لا أبعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which condition ensures that a definition applies only to the definiendum and not to anything else?
A) mutarid
B) mun'akis
C) zahir
D) la ab'ad

Key Takeaway
A sound definition must satisfy four conditions: universality, exclusivity, clarity, and non-obscurity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of 'zakat' as 'a specified amount of wealth given to specific recipients under specific conditions'. Check whether it is mutarid and mun'akis. If not, adjust it.
?
Give an example of a definition that is clear but not remote, and one that is remote (e.g., defining 'shajarah' by its Latin botanical name).

Do the Conditions Automatically Apply to Verbal Definitions? A Critical Refinement
Al-Bajuri presents an objection to stipulating conditions for verbal definitions and its refined refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the argument that conditions are automatically met for verbal definitions and identify the polysemy counterexample.

Core Idea
Verbal definitions may fail clarity if the synonym is polysemous, so the conditions are not automatic.

Some scholars argued that the four conditions of definitions are automatically satisfied in a verbal definition because the synonym has the exact same meaning. They claimed it cannot lack universality, exclusivity, or clarity, nor be circular. However, a verifier pointed out that a more famous synonym might be polysemous, thus violating the clarity condition. Al-Anbabi then analyzed various scenarios of polysemy and fame, showing that the issue is nuanced. This lesson unpacks that debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Some scholars said that for verbal definitions, the conditions are automatically met because the synonym has the same meaning as the definiendum in all respects, so it is impossible for it to be non-universal or unclear.
Ibn Qasim is cited as holding this view.
2
Identify the polysemy counterexample
A verifier responded: it is possible that the more famous synonym has another meaning besides the intended one. In that case, the definition is ambiguous and not clear.
Example: if 'qamh' also means 'Zayd's son', then defining 'burr' as 'qamh' is unclear.
3
Analyze Al-Anbabi's refinement
Al-Anbabi distinguishes several cases: (a) if the polysemous word has no context indicator, it fails clarity; (b) if both words are polysemous and one is more famous in both meanings, definition is possible if both meanings are intended; (c) if one word is famous in one meaning, that fame can act as a context indicator; (d) if each is famous in different meanings, the definition fails due to lack of relevant fame.
4
Assess the original objection
After this refinement, the original claim that conditions are automatically satisfied is weakened. The conditions, especially clarity, must be checked even for verbal definitions.

Source Text

(٤٤) وَمَا بِـ «لَفْظِيّ» لَدَيْهِمْ شُهِرَا تَبْدِيلُ لَفْظُ بِرَدِيفٍ أَشْـهَــرَا
الباجوري قوله: (وما بـ «لفظي»... إلخ) أَيْ : وَالَّذِي شُهِرَ عِنْدَهُمْ بِـ المُعَرِّفِ اللَّفْظِيّ... إلخ»، «مَا اسْمٌ مَوْصُولٌ ، وَشُهِرَ) صِلَتُهَا، وَلَدَيْهِمْ بِمَعْنَى : عِنْدَهُمْ ، ظَرْفٌ لِتِلْكَ الصَّلَةِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (تَبْدِيلُ ... إلخ) فِيهِ تَسَامُح ; لِأَنَّ المُعَرِّفَ اللَّفْظِيَّ لَيْسَ نَفْسَ التَّبْدِيلِ، بَلِ اللَّفْظُ الَّذِي أَتَى بِهِ بَدَلاً ; إِذِ التَّعَارِيفُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ الأَلْفَاظِ (۳) ، وَذَلِكَ كَأَنْ يُقَالَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ «البُرِّ»: «هُوَ : القَمْحُ».
(٤٥) وَشَرْطُ كُلِّ: أَنْ يُرَى مُطَرِدَا» «مُنْعَكِساً»، و«ظَاهِراً» لَا أَبْعَدَا
وَقَوْلُهُ : (بِرَدِيفٍ) أَيْ : بِمُرَادِفٍ، فَهُوَ فَعِيلٌ ; بِمَعْنَى : مُفَاعِلٍ . وَقَوْلُهُ: (أَشْهَرًا) أَيْ: عِنْدَ السَّامِعِ ، وَاحْتَرَزَ بِذَلِكَ عَنِ : الرَّدِيفِ الأَخْفَى، أَوِ المُسَاوِي، كَمَا هُوَ ظَاهِرٌ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَشَرْطُ كُلِّ ... إلخ) ظَاهِرُ كَلَامِهِ : اعْتِبَارُ مَا ذَكَرَهُ مِنَ الشُّرُوطِ فِي اللَّفْظِيّ كَغَيْرِهِ. وَتَعَقَّبَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ : بِأَنَّهُ لَا مَعْنَى لِاشْتِرَاطِ هَذِهِ الأُمُورِ فِيهِ; لأَنَّهُ لَا يُعْقَلُ تَخَلَّفُ شَيْءٍ مِنْهَا عَنْهُ; إِذْ لَا يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ لَفْظُ الرَّدِيفِ الأَشْهَرِ غَيْرَ جَامِعِ وَلَا غَيْرَ مَانِعِ ; لِأَنَّ مَدْلُولَهُ عَيْنُ مَدْلُولِ اللَّفْظِ غَيْرِ الأَشْهَرِ، وَلَا يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ دُونَ المُعَرَّفِ وَلَا مُسَاوِياً ; لِأَنَّ الفَرَضَ أَنَّهُ أَشْهَرُ مِنْهُ، وَلَا مَجَازاً ; لأَنَّ المَجَازَ وَالحَقِيقَةَ لَيْسَا مُتَرَادِفَيْنِ، وَلَا يُمْكِنُ أَيْضاً دُخُولُ الدَّورِ فِيهِ، كَمَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ ابْنُ قَاسِمِ فِي الآيَاتِ، وَهَكَذَا البَاقِي. اهـ وَهُوَ وَجِيه. لَكِنْ نَاقَشَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَهَكَذَا البَاقِي» بـ : أَنَّهُ يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ اللَّفْظُ الأَشْهَرُ مُشْتَرَكاً بَيْنَ مَعْنَى رَدِيفِهِ غَيْرِ الأَشْهَرِ وَبَيْنَ مَعْنى آخَرَ (۱) ، وَبِهَذَا يُعْلَمُ مَا فِي قَوْلِهِ : «لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُعْقَلُ تَخَلُّفُ شَيْءٍ مِنْهَا عَنْهُ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
الأنبابي :
قوله : (لَكِنْ نَاقَشَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ ... إلخ) فيه نظر؛ لأنَّه إذا لم يكن الآخر مشتركاً لم يكن رديفاً، وعلى تسليم أنَّه رديف إذا كان المشترك خالياً مِنَ القرينة كان غير ظاهر، وهو خلاف الفرض، وإن كان كلٌّ منهما مشتركاً بين معنيين؛ إلَّا أنَّ أحدهما أشهر فيهما ، والآخر خفيًا فيهما ، لم يمتنع التعريف بالمشترك؛ لأنَّ محلَّ منعه إذا لم يرد به جميع معانيه، وهنا قد أريد به معنياه معاً، وإن كان أحدهما أشهر في معنى من معنيه، والآخر خفيًا فيهما ، كانتِ الشُّهرة في هذا المعنى قرينة على إرادة تعريف الآخر بالنسبة لأحد معنييه، وإن كان كل منهما مشتهراً في معنى غير ما اشتهر فيه الآخر، لم يصح التّعريف; لعدم الشُّهرة مِنَ الجهة التي قصد بها التعريف، فتأمل.
(1) الشرشيمي : قوله : (وَبَيْنَ مَعْنى آخَرَ ) أي : كما إذا وضع لفظ «القمح» مثلاً على ابن زيد» مثلاً، ونظر فيه بعضهم: بأنَّه لا ترادف; إذ المتبادر منه التّساوي من كل الوجوه وقوله فيما سبق : (وَلَا يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ دُونَ المُعَرَّفِ) أي : دونه الظهور، وهو المعبّر عنه في المتن بقوله: «لَا أَبْعَدًا».
(44) And that which is known among them as 'verbal' is the replacement of a word by a more famous synonym. Al-Bajuri: His words: 'And that which... etc.' Meaning: that which is well-known among them as the verbal definer... etc. 'Ma' is a relative noun, 'shuhira' is its relative clause, and 'ladayhim' means 'among them', an adverb for that clause. His words: 'replacement... etc.' contains looseness, because the verbal definer is not the replacement itself, but the word that is brought as a substitute, since definitions are of the type of words. And that is like when it is said in defining 'al-burr': 'It is: al-qamh'. (45) And the condition of every (definition) is that it be seen as universal, converse, and clear, not remote. And his words: 'by a synonym' i.e., by a synonymous word, so it is fa'il in the sense of mufa'il. And his words: 'more famous' i.e., to the hearer, and by that he excludes the less famous or equal synonym, as is apparent. His words: 'and the condition of every... etc.' The apparent meaning of his speech is the consideration of what he mentioned of the conditions in the verbal (definition) as in other (definitions). And some objected to him: that there is no meaning to stipulating these matters in it, because it is inconceivable that any of them be absent from it; since it is impossible for the word of the more famous synonym to be non-universal or non-exclusive, because its meaning is identical to the meaning of the less famous word; and it is impossible for it to be less than the definiendum or equal, because the assumption is that it is more famous than it; and it is impossible for it to be metaphorical, because the metaphorical and the literal are not synonyms; and it is also impossible for circularity to enter into it, as Ibn Qasim stated in (his commentary on) al-Ayat, and likewise the rest. End. And it is sound. However, some of the verifiers criticized him regarding his statement 'and likewise the rest', saying that it is possible for the more famous word to be polysemous between the meaning of its less famous synonym and another meaning. And by this it is known what is in his statement 'because it is inconceivable that any of them be absent from it', so let it be considered. Al-Anbabi: His words: 'However, some verifiers criticized... etc.' There is a consideration in it; because if the other is not polysemous, it is not a synonym. And granting that it is a synonym, if the polysemous word is free from a contextual indicator, it is not clear, contrary to the assumption. And if each of them is polysemous between two meanings, except that one of them is more famous in both, and the other is obscure in both, definition by the polysemous is not prevented, because the place of its prevention is when not all its meanings are intended; and here both meanings are intended together. And if one of them is more famous in one of its two meanings and the other is obscure in both, the fame in that meaning is an indicator of the intention to define the other with respect to one of its two meanings. And if each of them is famous in a meaning different from what the other is famous for, the definition is not valid, due to lack of fame from the aspect intended by the definition. So reflect. (1) Al-Shirshimi: His words: 'and another meaning' i.e., as if the word 'al-qamh' were placed on 'Zayd's son' for example. And some considered it, saying that there is no synonymy, since the immediate understanding is equality from all aspects; and his statement earlier 'and it is impossible for it to be less than the definiendum' means 'less in clarity', which is expressed in the text by 'not remote'.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage defines the verbal definition (al-ta'rif al-lafzi) and discusses its conditions. The matn first states that a verbal definition is replacing a word with a more famous synonym. Al-Bajuri explains that the definer is not the act of replacement but the substitute word itself, as definitions are linguistic. He gives an example: defining 'al-burr' (wheat) as 'al-qamh' (another word for wheat). The matn then lists conditions for all definitions: being universal (mutarid), converse (mun'akis), clear (zahir), and not remote (la ab'ad). Al-Bajuri notes that some scholars objected that these conditions are automatically satisfied for verbal definitions because the synonym has the exact same meaning, so it cannot lack universality or exclusivity, cannot be less famous or equal, cannot be metaphorical, and cannot involve circularity. However, a verifier raised a nuanced objection: the more famous synonym might be polysemous (mushtarak), having another meaning besides the intended one. In that case, the definition would be ambiguous and not clear. Al-Anbabi then provides a detailed analysis of various scenarios of polysemy and fame. He argues that if the polysemous word has no contextual indicator, it lacks clarity. If both words are polysemous and one is more famous in both meanings, defining by it is permissible if both meanings are intended. If one is more famous in one meaning only, that fame can serve as a contextual indicator. But if each is famous in a different meaning, the definition fails due to lack of relevant fame. This refined analysis shows that the conditions are not automatically satisfied in verbal definitions and require careful consideration.

Key Terms
مشترك
رديف
أشهر
قرينة

Key Takeaway
Even verbal definitions require verification: a more famous synonym may be ambiguous if it is polysemous, so the clarity condition is not automatic.

The Terminology Shift: Muttarid and Mun'akis in Al-Bajuri and Al-Qarafi
Al-Bajuri clarifies a terminological shift in definition conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Al-Qarafi's interpretation of muttarid and mun'akis from the majority's interpretation as clarified by Al-Bajuri.

Core Idea
Al-Qarafi swaps muttarid (preventive) with mun'akis (comprehensive).

In studying definition conditions, you will encounter two conflicting terminologies. Al-Bajuri warns you that Al-Qarafi used muttarid to mean 'comprehensive' and mun'akis to mean 'preventive', opposite to the majority. This lesson helps you navigate this confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn's conditions
The matn states that every definition must be muttarid and mun'akis. Find this in the Arabic.
2
Extract Al-Qarafi's interpretation
Al-Bajuri quotes Al-Qarafi: muttarid = jami' (comprehensive), mun'akis = mani' (preventive). Write this down.
3
Contrast with the majority's view
The majority define muttarid as 'whenever the definer exists, the defined exists' (preventive) and mun'akis as 'whenever the defined exists, the definer exists' (comprehensive).
4
Check Al-Bajuri's conclusion
Al-Bajuri says the implication of the majority's terms is that muttarid is preventive and mun'akis is comprehensive — the reverse of Al-Qarafi.

الباجوري
قَوْلُهُ : (أَنْ يُرَى «مُطَرِدَا».. «مُنْعَكِساً») فَسَّرَ الفَرَّافِيُّ المُطَرِدَ» بـ: الجَامِعِ، وَالمُنْعَكِس بـ: المَانِعِ ؛ وَنَصُّ عِبَارَتِهِ فِي شَرْحِ التَّنْقِيحِ»: «وَقَوْلُنَا : جَامِعُ هُوَ مَعْنَى قَوْلِنَا : مُطَرِدُ ، وَقَوْلُنَا : مَانِعُ هُوَ مَعْنَى قَوْلِنَا : مُنْعَكِسُ .»
لَكِنْ مُقْتَضَى كَلَامِ الجُمْهُورِ خِلافُهُ ، حَيْثُ فَسَّرُوا المُطَرِدَ» بـ: «الَّذِي كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ وُجِدَ المُعَرَّفُ ـ بِفَتْحِ الرَّاءِ ، وَالمُنْعَكِسَ» بـ: «الَّذِي كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ المُعَرَّفُ - بِفَتْحِ الرَّاءِ - وُجِدَ هُوَ ؛ إِذْ مُقْتَضَاهُ : أَنَّ «المُطَّرِدَ : المَانِعُ ، وَالمُنْعَكِسَ» : الجَامِعُ، وَعَلَيْهِ :
فَحَقِيقَةُ الإِطْرَادِ: «أَنْ يَكُونَ كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ المُعَرِّفُ - بِالكَسْرِ - وُجِدَ المُعَرَّفُ ـ بِالفَتْحِ بِأَنْ لَا يَزِيدَ الأَوَّلُ عَلَى الثَّانِي بِأَفْرَادٍ يَصْدُقُ فِيهَا دُوْنَهُ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : حَيَوَانٌ نَاطِقٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ».
فَلَوْ زَادَ عَلَيْهِ بِتِلْكَ الأَفْرَادِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : جِسْمٌ نَامٍ حَسَّاسٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ»، فَإِنَّهُ يَزِيدُ بِالحِمَارِ وَالفَرَسِ مَثَلاً ، لَمْ يَصِحَ التَّعْرِيفُ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ غَيْرَ مُطَرِدٍ، فَإِنَّهُ يُوجَدُ وَلَا يُوجَدُ المُعَرَّفُ - بِالفَتْحِ - فِي الْأَفْرَادِ الَّتِي زَادَ بِهَا ، فَلَمْ يَكُنْ مَانِعاً .
وَحَقِيقَةُ الانْعِكَاسِ : أَنْ يَكُونَ كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ المُعَرَّفُ - بِالفَتْح - وُجِدَ المُعَرِّفُ - بِالكَسْرِ ) ؛ بِأَنْ لَا يَزِيدَ الأَوَّلُ عَلَى الثَّانِي بِأَفْرَادٍ يَصْدُقُ فِيهَا دُونَهُ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : «جِسْمٌ نَامٍ حَسَّاسٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الحَيَوَانِ».
فَلَوْ زَادَ عَلَيْهِ بِتِلْكَ الأَفْرَادِ ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : مُتَفَكِّرُ بِالقُوَّةِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ «الحَيَوَانِ»، فَإِنَّهُ يَزِيدُ بالحِمَارِ وَالفَرَسِ مَثَلاً ، لَمْ يَصِحُ التَّعْرِيفُ ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ غَيْرَ جَامِعٍ ، فَإِنَّهُ يُوجَدُ المُعَرَّفُ - بِالفَتْحِ - وَلَا يُوجَدُ هُوَ، فَلَمْ يَكُنْ جَامِعاً، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
Chapter on definitions. (45) And the condition of every [definition] is that it be seen as 'muttarid' (coextensive) and 'mun'akis' (converse), and 'apparent, not more remote.' Al-Bajuri: His statement: (that it be seen as 'muttarid' ... 'mun'akis'): Al-Qarafi interpreted 'muttarid' as 'comprehensive' and 'mun'akis' as 'preventive'; the text of his statement in Sharh al-Tanqih: 'Our saying 'comprehensive' is the meaning of our saying 'muttarid', and our saying 'preventive' is the meaning of our saying 'mun'akis'.' But the implication of the majority's statement contradicts this, for they interpreted 'muttarid' as 'that which, whenever it exists, the defined (with fatha) exists', and 'mun'akis' as 'that which, whenever the defined (with fatha) exists, it exists'; its implication is that the 'muttarid' is the preventive, and the 'mun'akis' is the comprehensive. And accordingly: The reality of 'itrad' (coextension) is that whenever the definer (with kasra) exists, the defined (with fatha) exists, such that the former does not exceed the latter in individuals that apply to it without it; as in your saying 'rational animal' in the definition of 'human'. If it exceeds it with those individuals, as in your saying 'growing, sensitive body' in the definition of 'human', for it exceeds with donkey and horse for example, the definition is not valid because it is not muttarid; for it exists while the defined (with fatha) does not exist in the individuals it added, so it is not preventive. And the reality of 'inkis' (converse) is that whenever the defined (with fatha) exists, the definer (with kasra) exists, such that the former does not exceed the latter in individuals that apply to it without it; as in your saying 'growing, sensitive body' in the definition of 'animal'. If it exceeds it with those individuals, as in your saying 'potentially thinking' in the definition of 'animal', for it exceeds with donkey and horse for example, the definition is not valid because it is not comprehensive; for the defined (with fatha) exists while it does not exist, so it is not comprehensive. Let it be contemplated.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions for a valid definition (ta'rif). The matn states that every definition must be muttarid (coextensive) and mun'akis (converse). Al-Bajuri begins by noting that al-Qarafi interpreted these terms differently: muttarid as 'comprehensive' (jami') and mun'akis as 'preventive' (mani'). However, he points out that the majority of scholars reverse this, defining muttarid as 'that which, whenever it exists, the defined exists' (i.e., the definer's existence necessitates the defined's existence), which actually makes muttarid a preventive condition (because if the definer is absent, the defined is absent), and mun'akis as 'that which, whenever the defined exists, the definer exists', which makes it comprehensive. Al-Bajuri then explains the true nature of itrad: the definer's existence never exceeds the defined's existence; it is a necessary condition. For example, 'rational animal' as a definition of 'human' is muttarid because whenever 'rational animal' is present, 'human' is present. If the definer is broader, like 'growing, sensitive body' for 'human', it fails because it also applies to animals (donkey, horse) that are not human. Similarly, the true nature of inkis (converse) is that the defined's existence never exceeds the definer's existence; it is a sufficient condition. For instance, 'growing, sensitive body' is mun'akis for 'animal' because every animal is a growing sensitive body. If the definer is narrower, like 'potentially thinking' for 'animal', it fails because not all animals are potentially thinking (e.g., donkey, horse are not). Thus, muttarid ensures no false positives, and mun'akis ensures no false negatives. The commentary uses Islamic logic examples: humans, animals, horses, etc.

Key Terms

مُنْعَكِس
جَامِع
مَانِع

According to the majority as presented by Al-Bajuri, what does 'mun'akis' mean?
A) Whenever the definer exists, the defined exists.

Key Takeaway
Al-Bajuri warns that Al-Qarafi swapped the meanings of muttarid and mun'akis, so always check a commentator's usage against the majority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a definition in a usul al-fiqh text (e.g., 'qiyas is extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a shared 'illah'). Determine whether the definer is 'muttarid' (preventive) and 'mun'akis' (comprehensive) according to the majority's understanding.

Testing Definitions: Muttarid (No False Positives) and Mun'akis (No False Negatives)
Al-Bajuri explains the reality of itrad and inkis with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a given definition satisfies the conditions of muttarid (preventive) and mun'akis (comprehensive) using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be both preventive (no false positives) and comprehensive (no false negatives).

After understanding the terminology, you must apply these conditions. Al-Bajuri gives concrete examples: 'rational animal' for human satisfies muttarid, while 'growing sensitive body' does not. Similarly, 'growing sensitive body' for animal satisfies mun'akis, but 'potentially thinking' does not. This lesson teaches you to perform such tests.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition of itrad (muttarid)
Muttarid means the definer (with kasra) must not apply to anything that the defined does not apply to. In other words, whenever the definer is found, the defined is found.
This prevents false positives (overly broad definitions).
2
Test a definition for itrad
Take the definer 'rational animal' and the defined 'human'. Check if there is any rational animal that is not human. Since all rational animals are human, it is muttarid. Now test 'growing sensitive body' for human: it applies to donkeys and horses, so it is not muttarid.
Al-Bajuri's example.
3
Understand the condition of inkis (mun'akis)
Mun'akis means the defined must not apply to anything that the definer does not apply to. Whenever the defined is found, the definer is found.
This prevents false negatives (overly narrow definitions).
4
Test a definition for inkis
Take the definer 'growing sensitive body' and the defined 'animal'. Check if there is any animal that is not a growing sensitive body. Since all animals are growing sensitive bodies, it is mun'akis. Now test 'potentially thinking' for animal: it does not apply to donkeys or horses, so it is not mun'akis.

Source Text

الباجوري
قَوْلُهُ : (أَنْ يُرَى «مُطَرِدَا».. «مُنْعَكِساً») فَسَّرَ الفَرَّافِيُّ المُطَرِدَ» بـ: الجَامِعِ، وَالمُنْعَكِس بـ: المَانِعِ ؛ وَنَصُّ عِبَارَتِهِ فِي شَرْحِ التَّنْقِيحِ»: «وَقَوْلُنَا : جَامِعُ هُوَ مَعْنَى قَوْلِنَا : مُطَرِدُ ، وَقَوْلُنَا : مَانِعُ هُوَ مَعْنَى قَوْلِنَا : مُنْعَكِسُ .»
لَكِنْ مُقْتَضَى كَلَامِ الجُمْهُورِ خِلافُهُ ، حَيْثُ فَسَّرُوا المُطَرِدَ» بـ: «الَّذِي كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ وُجِدَ المُعَرَّفُ ـ بِفَتْحِ الرَّاءِ ، وَالمُنْعَكِسَ» بـ: «الَّذِي كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ المُعَرَّفُ - بِفَتْحِ الرَّاءِ - وُجِدَ هُوَ ؛ إِذْ مُقْتَضَاهُ : أَنَّ «المُطَّرِدَ : المَانِعُ ، وَالمُنْعَكِسَ» : الجَامِعُ، وَعَلَيْهِ :
فَحَقِيقَةُ الإِطْرَادِ: «أَنْ يَكُونَ كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ المُعَرِّفُ - بِالكَسْرِ - وُجِدَ المُعَرَّفُ ـ بِالفَتْحِ بِأَنْ لَا يَزِيدَ الأَوَّلُ عَلَى الثَّانِي بِأَفْرَادٍ يَصْدُقُ فِيهَا دُوْنَهُ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : حَيَوَانٌ نَاطِقٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ».
فَلَوْ زَادَ عَلَيْهِ بِتِلْكَ الأَفْرَادِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : جِسْمٌ نَامٍ حَسَّاسٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الإِنْسَانِ»، فَإِنَّهُ يَزِيدُ بِالحِمَارِ وَالفَرَسِ مَثَلاً ، لَمْ يَصِحَ التَّعْرِيفُ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ غَيْرَ مُطَرِدٍ، فَإِنَّهُ يُوجَدُ وَلَا يُوجَدُ المُعَرَّفُ - بِالفَتْحِ - فِي الْأَفْرَادِ الَّتِي زَادَ بِهَا ، فَلَمْ يَكُنْ مَانِعاً .
وَحَقِيقَةُ الانْعِكَاسِ : أَنْ يَكُونَ كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ المُعَرَّفُ - بِالفَتْح - وُجِدَ المُعَرِّفُ - بِالكَسْرِ ) ؛ بِأَنْ لَا يَزِيدَ الأَوَّلُ عَلَى الثَّانِي بِأَفْرَادٍ يَصْدُقُ فِيهَا دُونَهُ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : «جِسْمٌ نَامٍ حَسَّاسٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الحَيَوَانِ».
فَلَوْ زَادَ عَلَيْهِ بِتِلْكَ الأَفْرَادِ ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : مُتَفَكِّرُ بِالقُوَّةِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ «الحَيَوَانِ»، فَإِنَّهُ يَزِيدُ بالحِمَارِ وَالفَرَسِ مَثَلاً ، لَمْ يَصِحُ التَّعْرِيفُ ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ غَيْرَ جَامِعٍ ، فَإِنَّهُ يُوجَدُ المُعَرَّفُ - بِالفَتْحِ - وَلَا يُوجَدُ هُوَ، فَلَمْ يَكُنْ جَامِعاً، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
Chapter on definitions. (45) And the condition of every [definition] is that it be seen as 'muttarid' (coextensive) and 'mun'akis' (converse), and 'apparent, not more remote.' Al-Bajuri: His statement: (that it be seen as 'muttarid' ... 'mun'akis'): Al-Qarafi interpreted 'muttarid' as 'comprehensive' and 'mun'akis' as 'preventive'; the text of his statement in Sharh al-Tanqih: 'Our saying 'comprehensive' is the meaning of our saying 'muttarid', and our saying 'preventive' is the meaning of our saying 'mun'akis'.' But the implication of the majority's statement contradicts this, for they interpreted 'muttarid' as 'that which, whenever it exists, the defined (with fatha) exists', and 'mun'akis' as 'that which, whenever the defined (with fatha) exists, it exists'; its implication is that the 'muttarid' is the preventive, and the 'mun'akis' is the comprehensive. And accordingly: The reality of 'itrad' (coextension) is that whenever the definer (with kasra) exists, the defined (with fatha) exists, such that the former does not exceed the latter in individuals that apply to it without it; as in your saying 'rational animal' in the definition of 'human'. If it exceeds it with those individuals, as in your saying 'growing, sensitive body' in the definition of 'human', for it exceeds with donkey and horse for example, the definition is not valid because it is not muttarid; for it exists while the defined (with fatha) does not exist in the individuals it added, so it is not preventive. And the reality of 'inkis' (converse) is that whenever the defined (with fatha) exists, the definer (with kasra) exists, such that the former does not exceed the latter in individuals that apply to it without it; as in your saying 'growing, sensitive body' in the definition of 'animal'. If it exceeds it with those individuals, as in your saying 'potentially thinking' in the definition of 'animal', for it exceeds with donkey and horse for example, the definition is not valid because it is not comprehensive; for the defined (with fatha) exists while it does not exist, so it is not comprehensive. Let it be contemplated.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions for a valid definition (ta'rif). The matn states that every definition must be muttarid (coextensive) and mun'akis (converse). Al-Bajuri begins by noting that al-Qarafi interpreted these terms differently: muttarid as 'comprehensive' (jami') and mun'akis as 'preventive' (mani'). However, he points out that the majority of scholars reverse this, defining muttarid as 'that which, whenever it exists, the defined exists' (i.e., the definer's existence necessitates the defined's existence), which actually makes muttarid a preventive condition (because if the definer is absent, the defined is absent), and mun'akis as 'that which, whenever the defined exists, the definer exists', which makes it comprehensive. Al-Bajuri then explains the true nature of itrad: the definer's existence never exceeds the defined's existence; it is a necessary condition. For example, 'rational animal' as a definition of 'human' is muttarid because whenever 'rational animal' is present, 'human' is present. If the definer is broader, like 'growing, sensitive body' for 'human', it fails because it also applies to animals (donkey, horse) that are not human. Similarly, the true nature of inkis (converse) is that the defined's existence never exceeds the definer's existence; it is a sufficient condition. For instance, 'growing, sensitive body' is mun'akis for 'animal' because every animal is a growing sensitive body. If the definer is narrower, like 'potentially thinking' for 'animal', it fails because not all animals are potentially thinking (e.g., donkey, horse are not). Thus, muttarid ensures no false positives, and mun'akis ensures no false negatives. The commentary uses Islamic logic examples: humans, animals, horses, etc.

Key Terms
مُطَرِد
مُنْعَكِس
إِطْرَاد
انْعِكَاس
مُعَرِّف
مُعَرَّف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following definitions fails the condition of mun'akis?
A) 'Human is a rational animal'
B) 'Animal is a growing sensitive body'
C) 'Animal is a potentially thinking being'
D) 'Human is a laughing animal'

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be both preventive (muttarid) and comprehensive (mun'akis) to perfectly include all intended cases and exclude all others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Test the definition 'wine is every intoxicating beverage derived from grapes' for muttarid and mun'akis. Provide counterexamples if it fails.

Conditions for a Valid Definition: Clarity, Non-Obscurity, and Non-Metaphor
Al-Bajuri explains remaining conditions for a valid definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four conditions for a valid definition is violated in a given definition and explain why using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
A definition must illuminate; obscurity and unqualified metaphor defeat its purpose.

A definition is meant to make the unknown known. The logicians thus insisted that the definer (al-hadd) must be clearer than the defined (al-mahdud). Al-Bajuri unpacks four conditions from the matn: the definition must be apparent (zahir), not more obscure (la ab'ad), not equally obscure (la musawi), and not metaphorical without a contextual indicator (la tajawwuz). Each condition is illustrated with concrete examples from Islamic discourse, such as defining fire as 'a body like the soul' or defining a scholar as 'a sea'. Understanding these conditions helps you evaluate definitions in kalam, fiqh, and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being discussed
For each of the four conditions (zahir, la ab'ad, la musawi, la tajawwuz), note the Arabic term and its translation. Understand the underlying rationale: a definition must be clearer than the defined.
2
Apply the 'not more obscure' condition
Consider the example: defining fire as 'a body like the soul'. The soul is more debated and obscure than fire, so the definition violates la ab'ad. The definer is more hidden than the defined.
The condition guards against using a term that is less known than the thing being defined.
3
Distinguish 'not more obscure' from 'not equal'
The third condition (la musawi) prohibits the definer from being equally obscure. The example given: defining 'moving entity' as 'that which is not stationary' when both are equally clear fails to elucidate.
If both terms are equally well-known, the definition does not add knowledge.
4
Recognize the exception for metaphor
Metaphorical language (tajawwuz) is forbidden unless accompanied by a clear contextual indicator (qarina) that resolves ambiguity. Example: defining 'scholar' as 'a sea that is gentle to people' is invalid without context.
Without a qarina, the definition becomes ambiguous and fails to clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَظَاهِراً) أَيْ : عِنْدَ السَّامِعِ .
(٤٦) وَلَا مُسَاوِياً»، وَالَا تَجَوُّزَا» بِلَا قَرِيْنَةٍ تُـحَرِّزَا
الباجوري
وَقَوْلُهُ : (لَا أَبْعَدَا)، وَلَا مُسَاوِياً تَصِريحٌ بِالمَفْهُوم (۳) .
وَالمُرَادُ : أَبْعَدَ عَنِ الذِّهْنِ، وَهُوَ الأَخْفَى، وَذَلِكَ كَقَوْلِكَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ النَّارِ»: «هِيَ : جِسْمُ كَالنَّفْسِ (۳) ، فَإِنَّهُ أَخْفَى مِنَ المُعَرَّفِ ؛ لِشِدَّةِ خَفَاءِ النَّفْسِ؛ بِدَلِيلِ كَثْرَةِ الخِلَافِ فِيهَا وَالمُرَادُ : مُسَاوِياً فِي الخَفَاءِ، وَذَلِكَ كَقَوْلِكَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ المُتَحَرِّكِ» : «هُوَ: مَا لَيْسَ بِسَاكِنِ إِذَا اسْتَوَى كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا عِنْدَ السَّامِعِ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا تَجَوزا ) ... إلخ) أَيْ: وَلَا بِلَفْظِ تُجَوِّزَ بِهِ ... إلخ (١) ، كَمَا قَالَهُ المُصَنِّفُ فِي شَرْحِهِ ، وَذَلِكَ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ العَالِمِ»: «هُوَ : بَحْرُ يُلَاطِفُ النَّاسَ، فَإِنَّ هَذَا لَفْظُ (۷)،
الأنبابي
قوله : (كَالنَّفْسِ) بسكون الفاء.
His statement: (and 'apparent') i.e., to the listener. (46) 'and not equal, and not metaphorical without a contextual indicator that prevents [ambiguity].' Al-Bajuri: And his statement: ('not more remote' and 'not equal') is an explicit mention of the understood [condition]. And the meaning is: more remote from the mind, which is the more hidden; that is like your saying in the definition of 'fire': 'It is a body like the soul', for it is more hidden than the defined due to the extreme hiddenness of the soul, evidenced by the abundance of disagreement about it. And the meaning [of 'equal'] is equal in hiddenness; that is like your saying in the definition of 'moving entity': 'It is that which is not stationary' when each of them is equally understood to the listener. Let it be contemplated. His statement: ('and not metaphorical') ... etc. i.e., and not with a word that has been used metaphorically ... etc., as the author said in his commentary. That is like saying in the definition of 'scholar': 'He is a sea that is gentle to people,' for this is a [metaphorical] expression. Al-Anbabi: His statement: ('like the soul') with sukun on the fa.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the conditions for a valid definition. The matn adds that the definition must be 'apparent' (zahir), 'not more remote' (la ab'ad), 'not equal' (la musawiyan), and 'not metaphorical' (la tajawwuz) without a contextual indicator. Al-Bajuri first explains 'zahir' as meaning clear to the listener. Then he discusses 'la ab'ad': the definer must not be more obscure (further from the mind) than the defined. He gives an example: defining fire as 'a body like the soul' is invalid because the soul is more obscure than fire, as shown by the many disagreements about the soul's nature. Next, 'la musawiyan' means the definer should not be equally obscure to the defined; if both are equally clear or unclear, the definition fails to provide elucidation. Example: defining 'moving entity' as 'that which is not stationary' when both terms are equally clear to the listener. Finally, 'la tajawwuz' prohibits using metaphorical language without a clear contextual indicator. Example: defining a 'scholar' as 'a sea that is gentle to people' is metaphorical and thus invalid unless a qarina (contextual clue) removes ambiguity. The marginal note by al-Anbabi clarifies that in the example of 'like the soul', the word 'nafs' is pronounced with sukun on the fa. This passage guards against definitions that are obscure, circular, or figurative, ensuring that definitions truly explain.

أَبْعَد
مُسَاوِي
تَجَوُّز

Key Takeaway
A definition must be clearer than the defined, not equally obscure, and free from metaphor without context to fulfill its explanatory purpose.

Two Conditions of Definition: Not More Obscure, Not Equally Obscure
Explanation of two negative conditions for definition clarity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given definition violates the conditions of being more apparent than the defined and not being equally obscure.

Core Idea
A definition that obscures rather than clarifies defeats its purpose.

In the tradition of Islamic logic, a valid definition must not only be logically sound but also cognitively accessible. The commentator Bajuri, explaining a verse from al-Sullam, emphasizes two crucial negative conditions: the definition must not be more obscure (ab'adu) than the term being defined, nor equal to it in obscurity. He provides concrete examples to illustrate these pitfalls. This lesson guides you through analyzing definitions for these conditions using the same criteria.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The matn states the conditions: not more obscure (لا أبعد) and not equal in obscurity (لا مساو). The commentary clarifies that 'more obscure' means further from the mind (أبعد عن الذهن) and thus more hidden (الأخفى).
The opposite is being apparent (ظاهر).
2
Examine the example for 'more obscure'
Bajuri gives the definition of fire as 'a body like the soul'. Compare the clarity of the definiendum (fire) with the definiens (a body like the soul). Fire is known through sensory experience; the soul is a matter of intense philosophical disagreement. Hence the definiens is more obscure.
The soul's hiddenness is evidenced by the abundance of disagreement (كثرة الخلاف).
3
Examine the example for 'equal obscurity'
The definition of 'the moving' (المتحرك) as 'that which is not stationary' (ما ليس بساكن) is problematic if both terms are equally unclear to the hearer. If the hearer does not know what 'moving' means, they won't understand 'not stationary' either.
The condition depends on the state of the hearer (عند السامع).
4
Apply the condition to any definition
Given any definition, ask: Is any part of the definiens more hidden than the definiendum? If so, the definition fails this condition. Also, if the definiens and definiendum are equally obscure, it fails.
This is a test of utility: definitions must illuminate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَشَرْطُ كُلِّ : أَنْ يُرَى مُطَرِدَا مُنْعَكِساً، وَظَاهِراً لَا أَبْعَدَا وَلَا مُسَاوِياً. قَوْلُهُ : (لَا أَبْعَدَا)، وَلَا مُسَاوِياً تَصْريحٌ بِالمَفْهُوم. وَالمُرَادُ : أَبْعَدَ عَنِ الذِّهْنِ، وَهُوَ الأَخْفَى، وَذَلِكَ كَقَوْلِكَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ النَّارِ: «هِيَ : جِسْمُ كَالنَّفْسِ»، فَإِنَّهُ أَخْفَى مِنَ المُعَرَّفِ؛ لِشِدَّةِ خَفَاءِ النَّفْسِ؛ بِدَلِيلِ كَثْرَةِ الخِلَافِ فِيهَا. وَالمُرَادُ : مُسَاوِياً فِي الخَفَاءِ، وَذَلِكَ كَقَوْلِكَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ المُتَحَرِّكِ: «هُوَ: مَا لَيْسَ بِسَاكِنِ» إِذَا اسْتَوَى كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا عِنْدَ السَّامِعِ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
And the condition of every [definition]: that it be seen as mutually applicable and converse, and apparent, not more obscure and not equal in obscurity. His statement: (not more obscure) and not equal in obscurity is an explicit mention of the concept. And what is meant: more distant from the mind, and it is the more hidden, such as your saying in defining fire: 'It is a body like the soul,' for it is more hidden than the defined due to the intensity of the soul's hiddenness, evidenced by the abundance of disagreement about it. And what is meant: equal in obscurity, such as your saying in defining the moving: 'It is that which is not stationary' when each of them is equal to the hearer, so let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for a valid definition (ta'rif) as stated in the matn of al-Sullam. The definition must be 'apparent' (zahir), meaning it must be clearer than the thing being defined. Two pitfalls are highlighted: the definition must not be more obscure (ab'ada) than the defined, nor equal in obscurity (musawiyan). The commentator, Bajuri, clarifies that 'more obscure' means further from the mind, i.e., more hidden. He provides an example: defining fire as 'a body like the soul' is invalid because the soul (al-nafs) is even more obscure than fire, as evidenced by the extensive disagreement about its nature. Similarly, the condition 'not equal in obscurity' is illustrated by defining 'the moving' (al-mutaharrik) as 'that which is not stationary'—if both terms are equally unclear to the hearer, the definition fails. The commentary thus emphasizes that a definition must serve to clarify, not to add confusion.

Key Terms
لا أبعد (lā ab‘ad): A condition that the definition must not be more obscure than the defined; 'ab‘ad' means further from the mind, i.e., more hidden.
لا مساو (lā musāwin): A condition that the definition must not be equal in obscurity to the defined; both must not be equally unclear.
المعرف (al-mu‘arraf): The thing being defined (open to the definite article); the defined term. [Note: In later texts, al-mu‘arraf can also refer to the defining expression, but here it is the defined.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Bajuri reject the definition of fire as 'a body like the soul'?
A) Because the soul is a more well-known thing than fire.
B) Because the soul is more obscure than fire, violating the 'not more obscure' condition.
C) Because fire is not a body.
D) Because the soul is not a body.

Key Takeaway
A definition must be clearer than the term it defines; if it introduces more obscurity or matches the obscurity, it is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a definition from a fiqh or kalam text (e.g., definition of 'salah' or 'iman') and test whether any part of the definiens is more obscure or equally obscure as the definiendum. Write your assessment.

Distinguishing Specifying vs. Preventing Contexts in Definitions
Bajuri explains the condition on metaphorical terms in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a specifying context (qarīna mu‘ayyina) and a preventing context (qarīna māni‘a) and identify whether a given metaphorical definition is valid.

Core Idea
A metaphor with only a preventing context is still ambiguous; only a specifying context removes confusion in definitions.

When defining a term, using a metaphorical word can lead to confusion unless there is a clear indicator of the intended meaning. This lesson explores the two types of contexts—preventing and specifying—and shows why only a specifying context makes a metaphorical definition acceptable.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the condition
The source states that a definition must not use a metaphorical term (tajawwuz) without a context that protects against misunderstanding. Understand that this is a general rule.
The Arabic phrase is 'lā tajawwuzā bi-lā qarīna' - no metaphorical term without a context.
2
Identify the two types of contexts
A preventing context (qarīna māni‘a) only rules out the literal meaning but does not pinpoint the intended figurative meaning. A specifying context (qarīna mu‘ayyina) positively indicates the intended figurative meaning.
The author clarifies that the context negated in the rule is the specifying context, not the preventing one.
3
Apply the distinction to an example
Consider defining a scholar as 'a sea that treats people kindly and reveals subtleties.' The phrase 'treats people kindly and reveals subtleties' is a specifying context; it tells you that 'sea' is meant figuratively to indicate vast knowledge and generosity.
Without the specifying context, 'sea' alone could be literal or metaphorical in an undefined way.
4
Evaluate a definition
Given a metaphorical definition, determine if it contains a specifying context. If it only has a preventing context or none, the definition is invalid.
A preventing context alone (e.g., 'not literal') is insufficient; the context must specify the intended meaning.

وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا تَجَوُّزَا بِلَا قَرِيْنَةٍ تُـحُرِّزَا) أَيْ: وَلَا بِلَفْظِ تُجُوِّزَ بِهِ بِلَا قَرِينَةٍ تُحُرِّزَ بِهَا عَنْ غَيْرِ المَعْنَى المُرَادِ. وَإِنْ كَانَ فِيهِ قَرِينَةٌ مَانِعَةٌ مِنْ إِرَادَةِ المَعْنَى الأَصْلِيِّ، فَالقَرِينَةُ المَنْفِيَّةُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : بِلَا قَرِيْنَةٍ بِهَا تُحُرِّزَا» إِنَّمَا هِيَ المُعَيِّنَةُ، لَا المَانِعَةُ. وَبِذَلِكَ انْدَفَعَ الاِعْتِرَاضُ : بِأَنَّ المَجَازَ لَا يَتَحَقَّقُ إِلَّا بِقَرِينَةٍ فَكَيْفَ يَقُولُ المُصَنِّفُ : وَلَا تَجَوُّزًا بِلَا قَرِينَةٍ ... إلخ»؟ وَاحْتَرَزَ بِذَلِكَ : عَمَّا لَوْ تُجُوِّزَ بِهِ مَعَ قَرِينَةٍ مُعَيِّنَةٍ؛ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ العَالِمِ: «هُوَ : بَحْرٌ يُلاطِفُ النَّاسَ يُظهِرُ الدَّقَائِقَ وَالنِّكَات»، فَإِنَّهُ تَعْرِيفٌ صَحِيحٌ؛ لِعَدَمِ الالْتِبَاسِ حِينَئِذٍ، وَلَا حَاجَةَ فِي هَذِهِ الحَالَةِ لِقَوْلِنَا : «يُلاطِفُ النَّاسَ لِلاسْتِغْنَاءِ عَنْهُ بِقَوْلِنَا : «يُظْهِرُ ... إلخ»؛ لِأَنَّ المُعَيِّنَةَ تَكْفِي عَنِ المَانِعَةِ، كَمَا هُوَ مُقَرَّرٌ فِي مَحَلِّهِ.
And his statement: 'and not with a metaphorical [term] without a context that prevents [ambiguity]' means: and not with a word used metaphorically without a context that protects from something other than the intended meaning. Even if it contains a context that prevents the original meaning, the context negated in his statement 'without a context that prevents' is only the specifying context, not the preventing context. And by that the objection is repelled: that metaphor does not occur except with a context, so how can the author say 'and not with a metaphorical [term] without a context'? And by that he made an exception for where [the term] is used metaphorically with a specifying context, such as when you say in defining the scholar: 'He is a sea that treats people kindly and reveals subtleties and witticisms,' for it is a valid definition because there is no confusion then, and there is no need in this case for our saying 'treats people kindly' to dispense with our saying 'reveals...' because the specifying context suffices instead of the preventing context, as is established in its place.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage addresses the condition that a definition must not employ a metaphorical term (tajawwuz) without a context (qarina) that clarifies the intended meaning. The commentator distinguishes between two types of contexts: a preventing context (qarina mani'a) that only rules out the literal meaning but does not specify the intended figurative meaning, and a specifying context (qarina mu'ayyina) that positively indicates the intended figurative meaning. The matn's phrase 'without a context that prevents' is interpreted to mean without a specifying context, because a preventing context is always present in any metaphor. The commentator then answers a potential objection: since metaphor necessarily requires a context, how can the matn forbid a metaphorical term without a context? The answer is that the forbidden case is when there is no specifying context; a metaphor with only a preventing context is still ambiguous. An acceptable use is when one defines a scholar as 'a sea that treats people kindly and reveals subtleties and witticisms'—here the specifying context (treats people kindly and reveals subtleties) clarifies that 'sea' is intended metaphorically to mean a scholar, not the literal sea. The commentator notes that in this valid case, the specifying context alone suffices; the preventing context is unnecessary.

Key Terms
تجوز
قرينة معينة
قرينة مانعة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the metaphorical definition 'The scholar is a sea that treats people kindly' considered valid?
A) Because 'sea' is the literal meaning of the word.
B) Because the phrase 'treats people kindly' serves as a specifying context.
C) Because any context works, even if vague.
D) Because the metaphor 'sea' is more apparent than 'scholar'.

Key Takeaway
A metaphorical definition is valid only when it includes a specifying context that uniquely identifies the intended meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify whether each of the following metaphorical definitions uses a specifying context or only a preventing context: (a) 'The scholar is a lion' – no further description. (b) 'The jurist is a beacon that guides to the straight path' – with the phrase 'guides to the straight path'.

Find a definition in a classical Islamic text (e.g., a dictionary or commentary) that uses a metaphor and evaluate whether it includes a specifying context. If not, suggest how to add one.

Constructing Valid Metaphorical Definitions
Bajuri provides a valid example of a metaphorical definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid definition using a metaphorical term with a specifying context, modeled after the example of 'alim as bahr.

Core Idea
A specifying context turns an ambiguous metaphor into a precise definition.

This lesson applies the condition from the previous lesson: a metaphorical term must be accompanied by a specifying context. We examine the example of defining a scholar as 'a sea that treats people kindly and reveals subtleties' and learn to create our own valid metaphorical definitions for Islamic concepts.

Guided Path
1
Analyse the given example
The example defines 'alim (scholar) as 'bahr' (sea) but immediately adds 'yulatifu al-nas yuzhir al-daqa'iq wa al-nukat' (treats people kindly and reveals subtleties and witticisms). This phrase specifies that 'sea' is meant metaphorically to indicate vast knowledge and generosity.
The specifying context is a whole phrase, not just a single word. It must be sufficient to pinpoint the intended meaning.
2
Understand why the specifying context suffices
The commentator notes that the specifying context alone removes confusion, so there is no need for a separate preventing context. The phrase 'treats people kindly' already rules out a literal sea and points to a human scholar.
The specifying context can serve both functions: preventing the literal meaning and specifying the figurative one.
3
Choose a new Islamic concept to define
Select a term from Islamic sciences, such as 'the Qur'an', 'iman', or 'al-Rahman'. Think of a suitable metaphor that captures an important aspect.
Ensure the metaphor is not obscure; it should be commonly understood in Islamic discourse.
4
Add a specifying context
Attach a phrase that uniquely identifies the intended meaning. For example, define 'the Qur'an' as 'a sun that illuminates the hearts and guides to truth'. The phrase 'illuminates the hearts and guides to truth' specifies that 'sun' means a source of guidance, not a literal sun.
Test the definition by asking: does the specifying context rule out all other plausible interpretations?

Conditions of Definition: No Circularity and No Ambiguity without Context
Bajuri explains two constraints on valid definitions: avoiding circularity and avoiding homonyms without clarifying context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular definitions (explicit and implicit) and ambiguous definitions (homonyms/metaphors without clarifying context) in Islamic scholarly texts, and explain why they are invalid.

Core Idea
Valid definitions must be free from circular dependency and unambiguous, either by using clear terms or by providing sufficient context.

Definitions are the foundation of clear knowledge in the Islamic sciences. A flawed definition can lead to confusion or circular reasoning. In this passage, Bajuri expands on two critical constraints: a definition must not depend on the very thing it defines (circularity), and it must not use ambiguous words like homonyms or metaphors unless a context clarifies the intended meaning. These rules protect definitions from becoming logically empty or misleading.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two conditions
The matn states two prohibitions: (1) do not define something by what is known through the definiendum (circularity), and (2) do not use a homonym without a clarifying context. Bajuri explains each with examples.
2
Analyse circularity: direct vs. indirect
Check if any term in the definiens ultimately depends on the definiendum. Direct circularity (e.g., defining 'sun' by 'day', which is defined by 'sun') is explicit. Indirect circularity occurs through a chain (e.g., defining 'two' by 'even', which uses 'equals', which uses 'two things', which refers back to 'two').
The example of 'sun' and 'day' shows explicit circularity; the 'two' example shows implicit circularity with one or more mediations.
3
Analyse ambiguity: homonyms and metaphors
If the definiens contains a word with multiple meanings (homonym) or is used metaphorically, look for a clarifying context (qarīna mu'ayyina). The context must actively specify the intended meaning, not just prevent the original meaning. Without such context, the definition is invalid.
Bajuri distinguishes between a clarifying context (mu'ayyina) and a preventing context (mani'a). Only the clarifying one suffices.
4
Evaluate the examples
Apply the conditions to the examples: 'sun is a planet that appears by day' (circular), 'sun is an eye' (ambiguous without context), 'sun is an eye that shines in the horizons' (valid because of clarifying context), 'scholar is a sea that shows subtleties' (valid metaphor with context). Determine why each is acceptable or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعرفات
(٤٧) وَالَا بِمَا يُدْرَى بِمَحْدُودِ»، وَلَا مُشْتَرَكِ مِنَ القَرِينَةِ خَلَا»
الباجوري
(1) تجوز به بلا قرينة تحرز بها عن غير المعنى المراد، وإن كان فيه قرينة مانعة من إرادة المعنى الأصلي، فالقرينة المنفية في قوله : بلا قرينة بها تحرزا إنما هي المعينة، لا المانعة. وبذلك اندفع الاعتراض : بأن المجاز لا يتحقق إلا بقرينة فكيف يقول المصنف : ولا تجوزا بلا قرينة ... إلخ»؟ واحترز بذلك : عما لو تجوز به مع قرينة معينة؛ كأن تقول في تعريف العالم»: «هو : بحر يلاطف الناس يظهر الدقائق والنكات»، فإنه تعريف صحيح؛ لعدم الالتباس حينئذ، ولا حاجة في هذه الحالة لقولنا : «يلاطف الناس للاستغناء عنه بقولنا : «يظهر ... إلخ»؛ لأن المعينة تكفي عن المانعة، كما هو مقرر في محله.
قوله : (ولا بما يدرى بمحدود) أي : ولا بما يعلم بواسطة المعرف - بالفتح، فالمراد بـ (المحدود) : مطلق المعرف. وإنما امتنع التعريف بذلك؛ للزوم الدور حينئذ، فإن كلا من المعرف - بالفتح - والمعرف - بالكسر - متوقف على الآخر في هذه الحالة، وهو : إما مصرح، وذلك إذا كان التعريف متوقفا على المعرفة من غير واسطة؛ كتعريف «الشمس» بـ : «أنها كوكب يظهر نهارا، فإنه يتوقف على المعرفة بلا واسطة؛ حيث أخذوا فيه «النهار» وقد عرفوه بـ: «أنه ما بين طلوع الشمس وغروبها». وإما مضمر، وذلك إذا كان التعريف متوقفا على المعرفة بواسطة أو أكثر؛ كتعريف «الاثنين» بـ: «أنهما أول عدد ينقسم إلى متساويين، فإنه يتوقف على المعرفة بواسطة؛ حيث أخذوا فيه المتساويين، وقد عرفوهما بـ : أنهما الشيئان غير المتفاضلين، وقد عرفوا الشيئين» بـ: «الاثنين. وكتعريف الاثنين» بـ: «أنهما أول زوج، فإنه يتوقف على المعرفة بأكثر من واسطة؛ حيث أخذوا فيه الزوج وقد عرفوه بـ : «أنه المنقسم إلى متساويين، وقد عرفوا «المتساويين» بـ: «الشيئين غير المتفاضلين، وقد عرفوا الشيئين» بـ : الاثنين»؛ أفاد الملوئي في كبيريه»
قوله : (ولا مشترك) ... إلخ) أي: ولا بمشترك لفظي خلا من القرينة المعينة للمراد؛ كأن تقول في تعريف الشمس»: «هي : عين، فلو وجدت القرينة المذكورة؛ كأن تقول فيما ذكر : هي : عين تضيء في الآفاق لم يمنع التعريف به.
Chapter on definitions. (47) And not by what is known through the definiendum, nor a homonym devoid of context. Al-Bajuri: (1) [A definition] may use metaphor without a context that distinguishes it from the intended meaning, even if there is a context that prevents intending the original meaning. The negated context in his statement 'without a context by which it is distinguished' is the clarifying context, not the preventing context. By this the objection is repelled: that metaphor does not occur except with a context, so how can the author say 'and not a metaphor without a context...'? And he cautions thereby against the case where it is used metaphorically with a clarifying context; as if you say in defining 'the scholar': 'He is a sea that is gentle with people, showing subtleties and witticisms.' For that is a valid definition because there is no confusion then, and there is no need in this case for our saying 'gentle with people' since it is dispensed with by our saying 'showing...'—because the clarifying context suffices over the preventing context, as established in its place. His statement: 'and not by what is known through the definiendum' means: not by what is known by means of the thing being defined (with fatḥa), so what is meant by 'al-maḥdūd' is the absolute thing being defined. And definition by this is prevented only due to the occurrence of circularity then, for each of the thing being defined and the definition is dependent on the other in this case. And it is either explicit, which is when the definition is dependent on the definiendum without mediation; like defining 'the sun' as 'a planet that appears by day,' for it depends on the definiendum without mediation since they took in it 'day' and they defined it as 'what is between sunrise and sunset.' Or implicit, which is when the definition is dependent on the definiendum by one or more mediations; like defining 'two' as 'the first number divisible into two equals,' for it depends on the definiendum by one mediation since they took in it 'two equals' and they defined them as 'two things that are not unequal,' and they defined 'two things' as 'two.' And like defining 'two' as 'the first even number,' for it depends on the definiendum by more than one mediation since they took in it 'even' and they defined it as 'what is divisible into two equals,' and they defined 'two equals' as 'two things not unequal,' and they defined 'two things' as 'two.' Al-Mallawi stated this in his larger [commentary]. His statement: 'and not a homonym... etc.' means: not by a lexical homonym devoid of the context that clarifies the intended meaning; as if you say in defining 'the sun': 'It is an eye.' If the mentioned context existed, as if you say in what was mentioned: 'It is an eye that shines in the horizons,' definition by it is not prevented.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of two crucial conditions for valid definitions in logic. The author begins by addressing a potential objection concerning metaphorical definitions. The claim is that the matn (text) says definitions must not be metaphorical without a context; but metaphor always requires a context. The commentator clarifies that the context negated here is the 'clarifying' (mu'ayyina) context, not the 'preventing' (mani'a) context. A preventing context blocks the original meaning, but a clarifying context specifies the intended metaphorical meaning. Thus, a definition like 'the scholar is a sea that is gentle and shows subtleties' is valid because the phrase 'that is gentle...' provides a clarifying context that removes ambiguity. Then the commentator turns to the condition 'not by what is known through the definiendum,' which is the rule against circular definitions (dawr). Circularity occurs when the definition depends on the very thing being defined, either directly (explicit) or through a chain (implicit). The example given is defining 'sun' using 'day,' but 'day' itself is defined by the sun—explicit circularity. A more subtle implicit circularity is shown with the number two: define 'two' as 'first number divisible into two equal parts,' but 'two equal parts' requires defining 'two things,' which in turn refers back to 'two.' Similarly for defining 'two' as 'first even number' with multiple mediations. The final condition is about homonyms (mushtarak). A definition must not use a word that has multiple meanings without a context that specifies which meaning is intended. For example, defining 'sun' as 'eye' is ambiguous, but if you say 'eye that shines in the horizons,' the ambiguity is resolved and the definition is acceptable.

Key Terms
محذود
مشترك
قرينة
دور

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of circularity (dawr) in definition?
A) Defining 'wine' as 'intoxicating drink' and 'intoxicant' as 'what causes drunkenness.'
B) Defining 'prayer' as 'the act of bowing and prostrating' and 'prostration' as 'a part of prayer.'
C) Defining 'day' as 'the period when the sun is visible' and 'sun' as 'the star that appears during the day.'
D) Defining 'book' as 'a collection of written pages' and 'page' as 'a piece of paper in a book.'

Key Takeaway
Two essential conditions for a valid definition: no circular dependency (explicit or implicit) and no unclarified ambiguity (homonym or metaphor without clarifying context).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If 'effective cause' is defined as 'what necessitates the ruling in the original case,' which in turn relies on qiyas, what problem arises? Explain.

Explicit and Implicit Circular Definition
Author classifies circular definitions into two types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given definition contains explicit or implicit circularity.

Core Idea
A definition must not rely on the definiendum, either directly or indirectly.

In the science of definition, one key condition is that the definition must not be circular (dawr). The text explains two forms: explicit circularity, where the definition depends directly on the definiendum, and implicit circularity, where the dependence occurs through a chain of definitions. Understanding this distinction helps you avoid a common logical flaw when defining terms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined (e.g., 'sun', 'two'). This is the target that the definition should explain without relying on it.
2
Scan the definition for dependency
Examine each term in the definition. Ask: is any term defined using the definiendum itself? For explicit circularity, this dependency is direct.
3
Check for indirect dependency
If no direct dependency, look for a chain: does the definition use a term that is defined using another term that ultimately leads back to the definiendum? This is implicit circularity.
The chain may have one or more intermediaries.
4
Classify the circularity type
Based on the above, determine whether the definition has explicit circularity (direct) or implicit circularity (indirect). Apply to the examples: 'sun' is explicit; 'two' is implicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ لَا بِمَا يُدْرَى بِمَحْدُودٍ، وَهُوَ إِمَّا مُصَرَّحٌ، وَذَلِكَ إِذَا كَانَ التَّعْرِيفُ مُتَوَقِّفاً عَلَى المُعَرَّفِ مِنْ غَيْرِ وَاسِطَةٍ؛ كَتَعْرِيفِ الشَّمْسِ بِأَنَّهَا كَوْكَبٌ يَظْهَرُ نَهَاراً، فَإِنَّهُ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى المُعَرَّفِ بِلا وَاسِطَةٍ؛ حَيْثُ أَخَذُوا فِيهِ النَّهَارَ وَقَدْ عَرَّفُوهُ بِأَنَّهُ مَا بَيْنَ طُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ وَغُرُوبِهَا. وَإِمَّا مُضْمَرٌ، وَذَلِكَ إِذَا كَانَ التَّعْرِيفُ مُتَوَقِّفاً عَلَى المُعَرَّفِ بِوَاسِطَةٍ أَوْ أَكْثَرَ؛ كَتَعْرِيفِ الِاثْنَيْنِ بِأَنَّهُمَا أَوَّلُ عَدَدٍ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى مُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ، فَإِنَّهُ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى المُعَرَّفِ بِوَاسِطَةٍ؛ حَيْثُ أَخَذُوا فِيهِ المُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ، وَقَدْ عَرَّفُوهُمَا بِأَنَّهُمَا الشَّيْئَانِ غَيْرَ المُتَفَاضِلَيْنِ، وَقَدْ عَرَّفُوا الشَّيْئَيْنِ بِالِاثْنَيْنِ. وَكَتَعْرِيفِ الِاثْنَيْنِ بِأَنَّهُمَا أَوَّلُ زَوْجٍ، فَإِنَّهُ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى المُعَرَّفِ بِأَكْثَرَ مِنْ وَاسِطَةٍ؛ حَيْثُ أَخَذُوا فِيهِ الزَّوْجَ وَقَدْ عَرَّفُوهُ بِأَنَّهُ المُنْقَسِمُ إِلَى مُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ، وَقَدْ عَرَّفُوا المُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ بِالشَّيْئَيْنِ غَيْرِ المُتَفَاضِلَيْنِ، وَقَدْ عَرَّفُوا الشَّيْئَيْنِ بِالِاثْنَيْنِ؛ أَفَادَهُ المَلَوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ. قَالَ الْأَنْبَابِيُّ: قَوْلُهُ: (وَقَدْ عَرَّفُوا الشَّيْئَيْنِ بِالِاثْنَيْنِ) هَذَا يُفِيدُ أَنَّ الِاثْنَيْنِ هُنَا أَعَمُّ مِنَ الِاثْنَيْنِ فِيمَا سَبَقَ؛ لِأَنَّ الِاثْنَيْنِ فِيمَا سَبَقَ هُمَا الْفَرْدَانِ، فَلَا يَصْدُقَانِ عَلَى الْأَرْبَعَةِ مَثَلًا؛ بِدَلِيلِ تَعْرِيفِهِمَا بِأَنَّهُمَا أَوَّلُ عَدَدٍ، وَالِاثْنَيْنِ هُنَا بِمَعْنَى مُطْلَقِ الْأَمْرَيْنِ؛ تَسَاوَيَا أَمْ لَا، وَإِلَّا لَمَا احْتِيجَ لِوَصْفِ الشَّيْئَيْنِ بِكَوْنِهِمَا غَيْرَ مُتَفَاضِلَيْنِ، فَإِنْ قُصِدَ بِقَوْلِهِ: وَقَدْ عَرَّفُوا الشَّيْئَيْنِ بِالِاثْنَيْنِ أَنَّهُمْ عَرَّفُوا الشَّيْئَيْنِ غَيْرَ الْمُتَفَاضِلَيْنِ بِالِاثْنَيْنِ؛ كَانَ الِاثْنَانِ أَعَمَّ أَيْضًا؛ إِذِ الشَّيْئَانِ غَيْرُ الْمُتَفَاضِلَيْنِ تَفْسِيرٌ لِلْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ، وَالْمُتَسَاوِيَانِ يَشْمَلَانِ الْأَرْبَعَةَ، بِخِلَافِ الِاثْنَيْنِ أَوَّلًا، فَإِنَّهُمَا خَاصَّانِ بِأَوَّلِ الْأَعْدَادِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَةِ؛ بِدَلِيلِ قَوْلِهِ: أَوَّلُ عَدَدٍ ... إِلَخْ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَلَا دَوْرَ، تَأَمَّلْ.
And not by what is known by the definiendum. It is either explicit, which is when the definition depends on the definiendum without an intermediary, like defining 'sun' as 'a planet that appears by day', for it depends on the definiendum without an intermediary, since they included in it 'day' and they defined it as 'what is between the rising of the sun and its setting'. Or implicit, which is when the definition depends on the definiendum through one or more intermediaries, like defining 'two' as 'the first number that divides into two equal parts', for it depends on the definiendum through an intermediary, since they included in it 'two equal parts', and they defined them as 'two things that are not unequal', and they defined 'two things' as 'two'. And like defining 'two' as 'the first pair', for it depends on the definiendum through more than one intermediary, since they included in it 'pair' and they defined it as 'what divides into two equal parts', and they defined 'two equal parts' as 'two things that are not unequal', and they defined 'two things' as 'two'. Al-Malawi mentioned this in his 'Kabir'. Al-Anbabi said: His saying 'and they defined 'two things' as 'two'' indicates that 'two' here is more general than 'two' previously, because 'two' previously refers to two individuals, so they do not apply to four, for example, as evidenced by their definition as 'the first number'. 'Two' here means absolutely two things, whether equal or not, otherwise there would be no need to qualify the two things as 'not unequal'. If by his saying 'and they defined 'two things' as 'two'' it is meant they defined 'two things not unequal' as 'two', then 'two' is also more general, because 'two things not unequal' is an interpretation of 'two equal parts', and 'two equal parts' includes four, unlike 'two' firstly, for they are specific to the first of equal numbers, as evidenced by his saying 'the first number', etc. In that case, there is no circularity; reflect.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage discusses the prohibition of circular definitions (al-dawr) in the science of definition. The author explains that a definition must not be known by means of the definiendum itself. This prohibition is divided into two types: explicit (musarrah) and implicit (mudmar). Explicit circularity occurs when the definition depends directly on the definiendum without any intermediary. For example, defining 'sun' as 'a planet that appears by day' is circular because 'day' is defined in terms of the sun (the time between sunrise and sunset). Thus, the definition of 'sun' relies on the concept of the sun. Implicit circularity occurs when the definition depends on the definiendum through one or more intermediaries. Two examples are given for 'two' (al-ithnayn). First, defining 'two' as 'the first number that divides into two equal parts' is implicitly circular because 'two equal parts' (mutasawiyan) are defined as 'two things that are not unequal', and 'two things' (shay'an) are defined as 'two'. So the definition goes through a chain that returns to 'two'. Second, defining 'two' as 'the first pair' (awwal zawj) involves more intermediaries: 'pair' (zawj) is defined as 'what divides into two equal parts', then 'two equal parts' defined as 'two things not unequal', then 'two things' as 'two'. This shows a longer chain. The author then notes that Al-Malawi mentioned this in his 'Kabir'. Al-Anbabi comments that in the implicit examples, the term 'two' used in the definitional chain is actually more general than the definiendum 'two' (which is a specific number). The 'two' in the chain means any two things, while the definiendum 'two' refers to the specific number two, which is the first number. Therefore, the chain is not truly circular because the intermediate 'two' is broader. The passage thus refines the understanding of circularity: if the term in the definition is more general, it does not constitute a vicious circle.

Key Terms
دَوْر
مُصَرَّح
مُضْمَر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Which of the following is an example of implicit circular definition (dawr mudmar)?
A) Defining 'sun' as 'a planet that appears by day'.
B) Defining 'two' as 'the first number that divides into two equal parts'.
C) Defining 'man' as 'a rational animal'.
D) Defining 'wine' as 'an intoxicating beverage'.

Key Takeaway
Circular definitions can be explicit or implicit; both are invalid because they rely on the definiendum.

5 min
The Generality Condition: When an Implicit Chain is Not Circular
Al-Anbabi refines the concept of implicit circularity with the generality condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether an implicit definition chain actually constitutes circularity by checking the generality of the intermediary terms.

Core Idea
Not every chain that returns to the definiendum is circular; the meaning must be exactly the same.

In the previous lesson, we learned about implicit circularity. However, al-Anbabi points out a crucial refinement: if the intermediary term in the chain is more general than the definiendum, then there is no vicious circle. This nuance prevents false accusations of circularity and deepens our understanding of what constitutes a legitimate definition chain.

Guided Path
1
Trace the entire definition chain
Identify all terms in the definition that are themselves defined, and link them back to the definiendum. For example, 'two' → 'two equal parts' → 'two things not unequal' → 'two'.
2
Compare the meaning of the definiendum at each step
Determine whether the definiendum appears with the same specificity. In the 'two' example, the first 'two' (definiendum) is the number two specifically. The 'two' in 'two equal parts' is more general (any two things).
Al-Anbabi notes that the 'two' in the chain is 'two things' absolutely, not necessarily the number two.
3
Apply the generality test
If the intermediary term is broader in meaning than the original definiendum, then the chain does not create a vicious circle because the definition does not depend on the exact same concept.
4
Conclude about circularity
If the meanings match exactly at each step, it is implicit circularity. If the intermediary is more general, there is no circularity.
The passage concludes with 'there is no circularity; reflect.'

According to al-Anbabi, why is the definition of 'two' as 'the first number that divides into two equal parts' not actually circular?
A) Because the definition does not use the word 'two' at all.
