Avoiding Ambiguity: Homonyms in Definitions
Author explains that definitions must not use homonyms without disambiguating context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a definition that uses a homonym is valid based on the presence or absence of disambiguating context and the intention of the definer.

Core Idea
A homonym without context obscures meaning; context or intending all meanings restores clarity.

In Islamic logic, a valid definition must be clear and unambiguous. One common source of ambiguity is the use of homonyms (mushtarak) — words with multiple distinct meanings. The author of our text explains that using a homonym in a definition is prohibited unless a context (qarina) specifies which meaning is intended, or unless the definer intends all meanings of the homonym at once. This lesson will walk you through the rule and its exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the homonym in the definition
Look for a word that has more than one conventional meaning. For example, 'ayn (عين) can mean 'eye' or 'spring' or 'sun' (as a celestial body). In the definition 'the sun is an eye', the word 'eye' is a homonym.
If the word is not a homonym, this condition does not apply.
2
Check for disambiguating context (qarina)
See if the definition includes additional words that clarify which meaning is intended. For example, 'an eye that shines in the horizons' specifies the celestial meaning.
A qarina can be any phrase or description that resolves the ambiguity.
3
Determine if the definer intends all meanings
If the homonym is used without a qarina, check if the definer means all the word's meanings simultaneously. If so, the definition is still valid. Example: 'qawl' in the definition of 'proposition' covers both mental and spoken 'statement'.
This exception applies only when the homonym's meanings are not mutually exclusive in the context.
4
Apply the rule to evaluate validity
If a homonym is used with no qarina and not all meanings are intended, the definition is invalid (mumtani'). If a qarina is present or all meanings are intended, the definition is valid.
This rule ensures definitions achieve their purpose: to convey a clear concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ: (وَلَا مُشْتَرَك) ... إِلَخْ، أَيْ: وَلَا بِمُشْتَرَكٍ لَفْظِيٍّ خَلَا مِنَ الْقَرِينَةِ الْمُعَيِّنَةِ لِلْمُرَادِ؛ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الشَّمْسِ: هِيَ عَيْنٌ، فَلَوْ وُجِدَتِ الْقَرِينَةُ الْمَذْكُورَةُ؛ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِيمَا ذُكِرَ: هِيَ عَيْنٌ تُضِيءُ فِي الْآفَاقِ لَمْ يَمْتَنِعُ التَّعْرِيفُ بِهِ. وَقَالَ الشِّرْشِيمِيُّ: قَوْلُهُ: (وَلَا مُشْتَرَك) هَذَا يُغْنِي عَنْهُ «ظَاهِرُ». وَمَحَلُّ الِامْتِنَاعِ: إِذَا لَمْ يُرِدْ بِذَلِكَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ جَمِيعَ الْمَعَانِي الَّتِي وُضِعَ لَهَا، وَإِلَّا جَازَ التَّعْرِيفُ بِهِ، كَتَعْرِيفِ الْقَضِيَّةِ بِأَنَّهَا قَوْلٌ ... إِلَخْ، وَالْقَوْلُ مُشْتَرَكٌ بَيْنَ الْمَعْقُولِ وَالْمَلْفُوظِ، وَالْمُرَادُ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ الْمَذْكُورِ كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا.
His saying: 'And not a homonym ... etc.' means: and not by a verbal homonym devoid of the context determining the intended meaning. For example, if you say in defining 'sun': 'It is an eye.' Then if the mentioned context exists, such as if you say in what was mentioned: 'It is an eye that shines in the horizons,' then definition by it is not prevented. Al-Shirshimi said: His saying 'And not a homonym' dispenses with 'apparent'. And the place of prohibition is when one does not intend by that homonym all the meanings for which it was coined; otherwise, definition by it is permitted, like the definition of 'proposition' (qadiyya) as 'a statement (qawl) ... etc.' And 'qawl' is a homonym between the mental and the uttered, and the intended in that definition is both of them.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage addresses another condition for a valid definition: it must not use a homonym (mushtarak) without a disambiguating context (qarina). A homonym is a word that has multiple distinct meanings. If a definition uses such a word without specifying which meaning is intended, it fails to convey a clear concept. For example, defining 'sun' as 'an eye' is ambiguous because 'eye' can mean the organ or a celestial body. However, if a context is added, such as 'an eye that shines in the horizons', the intended meaning is clear and the definition becomes valid. The commentator al-Shirshimi notes that this condition is already covered by the requirement that the definition be 'apparent' (zahir), meaning it should be clear. The author then refines the rule: if the homonym is used intending all its meanings simultaneously, then it is acceptable. For instance, 'qawl' (statement) can mean both a mental utterance (ma'qul) and a spoken utterance (malfulz). In the definition of 'proposition' (qadiyya) as 'a statement that ...', both meanings are intended because a proposition exists in the mind and is expressed in speech. Thus, using a homonym that covers all relevant meanings is not problematic.

Key Terms
مُشْتَرَك
قَرِينَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because 'eye' is a homonym and no qarina is provided.
B) Because the definition is too brief.
C) Because 'eye' is a metaphor and metaphors are not allowed.
D) Because the sun is not an eye in any sense.

Key Takeaway
A definition using a homonym is valid only if accompanied by a disambiguating context (qarina) or if the definer intends all meanings of the homonym.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Examine the following definition from fiqh: 'Sadaqah is wealth given for the sake of Allah.' The word 'wealth' (mal) is a homonym (can mean cash, property, etc.). Does this definition need a qarina? If so, provide an appropriate qarina to make the definition valid.

Why Judgments Are Excluded from Definitions
Discusses why judgments are excluded from definitions and the ensuing circularity debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why including a judgment (hukm) in a definition (hadd) creates a vicious circle (dawr) and identify valid exceptions.

Core Idea
A judgment presupposes conceptualization; including it in a definition creates a vicious circle.

This passage from al-Bajuri’s commentary lays down a fundamental rule of definition in Islamic logic: judgments must be excluded from definitions. Understanding why will sharpen your ability to construct valid definitions and spot circular reasoning in theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Logicians (and scholars in general) hold that judgments—such as 'existent' or 'created'—must not enter into definitions. Why? Because a judgment about something presupposes that the thing is already conceptualized.
2
Identify the reason for prohibition
A judgment is a predication (e.g., 'Zayd is existent') that can only be made after the subject is known. If a definition includes a judgment, it requires prior knowledge of the definiendum, leading to a circular dependency.
3
Recognize the circularity (dawr)
The definition would depend on the definiendum (since judgment needs the definiendum) and the definiendum would depend on the definition (since definition explains it). This is a logical fallacy called dawr.
4
Examine the exception for homonyms
If a term is a homonym (mushtarak) and used in all its meanings, definition by it is allowed. Example: defining 'qadiyya' (proposition) as 'qawl' (statement), where 'qawl' means both mental and uttered speech.
The exception does not violate the rule because no judgment is introduced.
5
Follow the commentary debate
Some scholars attempted to resolve the circularity by claiming the judgment's subject is a specific aspect of the definiendum. Al-Atar criticizes this using a grammatical example: in 'the virtuous man came', 'virtuous' is an adjective, not a predicate, so it is not a judgment.
This shows the resolution fails within Arabic linguistic analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَعِنْدَهُمْ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ الْمَرْدُودِ أَنْ تَدْخُلَ الْأَحْكَامُ فِي الْحُدُودِ. وَمَحَلُّ الِامْتِنَاعِ: إِذَا لَمْ يُرِدْ بِذَلِكَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ جَمِيعَ الْمَعَانِي الَّتِي وُضِعَ لَهَا، وَإِلَّا جَازَ التَّعْرِيفُ بِهِ، كَتَعْرِيفِ الْقَضِيَّةِ بِأَنَّهَا قَوْلٌ ... إِلَخْ، وَالْقَوْلُ مُشْتَرَكٌ بَيْنَ الْمَعْقُولِ وَالْمَلْفُوظِ، وَالْمُرَادُ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ الْمَذْكُورِ كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا. قَوْلُهُ: (وَعِنْدَهُمْ أَي: الْمَنَاطِقَةُ) وَإِنَّمَا خَصَّهُمْ بِالذِّكْرِ؛ لِأَنَّهُمُ الْبَاحِثُونَ أَوَّلًا عَنْ ذَلِكَ، وَإِلَّا فَعِنْدَ غَيْرِهِمْ كَذَلِكَ. وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ الْمُرَادَ: عِنْدَ الْعُلَمَاءِ مُطْلَقًا. وَالظَّرْفُ عَلَى كُلِّ مِنَ الِاحْتِمَالَيْنِ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِقَوْلِهِ: «الْمَرْدُودِ» وَقَدَّمَهُ مَعَ كَوْنِ الْعَامِلِ مُضَافًا إِلَيْهِ: وَصِلَةٌ لِـ «أَلْ»؛ لِلضَّرُورَةِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: (مِنْ جُمْلَةِ الْمَرْدُودِ... إِلَخْ) أَيْ: لِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الشَّيْءِ فَرْعٌ عَنْ تَصَوُّرِهِ، فَهُوَ مُتَوَقِّفٌ عَلَى الْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَلْزَمُ الدَّوْرُ؛ لِتَوَقُّفِ كُلٍّ مِنَ التَّعْرِيفِ وَالْمُعَرَّفِ عَلَى الْآخَرِ، وَقَدْ دُفِعَ هَذَا الدَّوْرُ بِأَوْجُهِ مَا بَيْنَ بَعِيدٍ وَغَيْرِ سَدِيدٍ. وَدَفَعَهُ بَعْضُ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ: بِأَنَّ الْمَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ بِالْحُكْمِ الْمَذْكُورِ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الْمَأْخُوذُ ... (يتبع) قَالَ الْأَنْبَابِيُّ: قَوْلُهُ: (وَحِينَئِذٍ يَلْزَمُ الدَّوْرُ) عَلَى هَذَا يَكُونُ قَوْلُهُ: «وَعِنْدَهُمْ ... إِلَخْ» دَاخِلًا فِي قَوْلِهِ: «وَلَا بِمَا يُدْرَى بِمَحْدُودٍ»، وَإِنَّمَا ذَكَرَهُ؛ اهْتِمَامًا بِهِ. قَالَ الْعَطَّارُ: وَأَنَا أَقُولُ: هَذَا لَا يَسْتَقِيمُ؛ لِأَنَّ الْمَرْفُوعَ وَقَعَ لِلِاسْمِ الْوَاقِعِ خَبَرًا عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ، وَالصِّفَةُ وَالْمَوْصُوفُ كَالشَّيْءِ الْوَاحِدِ، فَقَدْ حُكِمَ بِالِاسْمِ بِقَيْدِ كَوْنِهِ مَرْفُوعًا عَلَى الْفَاعِلِ، وَهَلْ يَصِحُّ أَنْ يُقَالَ فِي مِثْلِ قَوْلِنَا: «جَاءَ الرَّجُلُ الْفَاضِلُ» أَنَّ «الْفَاضِلَ» مَحْكُومٌ بِهِ عَلَى الرَّجُلِ؟ كَيْفَ وَهَذَا التَّرْكِيبُ تَوْصِيفِيٌّ؟
And according to them, among the rejected is that judgments enter into definitions. And the place of prohibition is when one does not intend by that homonym all the meanings for which it was coined; otherwise, definition by it is permitted, like the definition of 'proposition' (qadiyya) as 'a statement ... etc.' And 'qawl' is a homonym between the mental and the uttered, and the intended in that definition is both of them. His saying: 'And according to them' (i.e., the logicians). He specified them because they are the primary investigators of that, otherwise, it is the same according to others. It is possible that the intended is: according to the scholars absolutely. The adverb in both possibilities is attached to his saying 'the rejected'. And he placed it before despite the operator being added to it: a connection to 'al' due to necessity. And his saying: 'from among the rejected ... etc.' i.e., because a judgment about a thing is a branch of its conceptualization, so it depends on the subject of judgment, and then circularity follows because each of the definition and the definiendum depends on the other. And this circularity has been refuted by some ways, some far-fetched and some unsound. And some verifying scholars refuted it: by saying that the subject of the mentioned judgment in the definition is only that which is taken ... (continues) Al-Anbabi said: His saying 'and then circularity follows' means that his saying 'and according to them ... etc.' is included in his saying 'and not by what is known by the definiendum', and he only mentioned it out of concern for it. Al-Atar said: And I say: This is not correct, because the raised phrase occurs for the noun that is the predicate of the subject, and the adjective and the described are like one thing, so one has judged the noun with the condition of its being raised upon the subject. Is it valid to say in such as our saying 'the virtuous man came' that 'virtuous' is judged upon 'man'? How so when this construction is attributive?
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule that judgments (ahkam) such as 'existent' or 'created' must not enter into definitions (hudud). The reason is that a judgment about something presupposes conceptualization of that thing. If a definition includes a judgment, it would require prior knowledge of the definiendum, leading to a vicious circle (dawr). For instance, if one defines 'human' as 'a rational animal that is existent', the judgment 'existent' depends on knowing what 'human' is, which is what the definition is supposed to provide. Thus, the definition and the definiendum would depend on each other. The logicians (al-mantaqiqa) are specifically mentioned as they are the primary authorities on this, but the rule applies universally. The author notes that some attempts to refute this circularity exist, but they are either far-fetched or unsound. However, some verifying scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) have offered a resolution: the subject of the judgment in the definition is not the definiendum itself but a specific aspect of it. The text is cut off here, but the marginal comments continue. Al-Anbabi observes that this whole discussion is actually a subset of the earlier rule 'not by what is known by the definiendum', but it is highlighted due to its importance. Al-Atar then criticizes the resolution, using a grammatical example: In the phrase 'the virtuous man came', 'virtuous' (al-fadil) is an adjective, not a predicate; it is not a judgment upon 'man'. So the analogy fails. Thus, the debate about including judgments in definitions remains nuanced.

Key Terms
حُكْم
حَدّ
دَوْر

A) Because judgments are always false.

C) Because definitions must be one word.
D) Because judgments are only used in syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
Including a judgment in a definition creates a vicious circle because the judgment presupposes conceptualization of the definiendum; thus, definitions must be purely descriptive.

The Problem of Circularity in Definitions
Explaining why judgments are rejected in definitions due to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical problem of circularity (al-dawr) when a definition includes a judgment and explain why logicians reject such definitions.

Core Idea
A judgment about something requires prior conception, so including it in a definition creates an endless loop.

In Islamic logic, definitions (hudud) are carefully constructed to convey the essence of a concept. The passage explains why logicians firmly reject including judgments (ahkam) in definitions. A judgment about something presupposes that we already know what that something is. If the definition itself contains a judgment, we are caught in a circle: the definition assumes the very knowledge it is supposed to provide. Let's work through this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rejected practice
Find where the commentator states that including judgments in definitions is among the rejected things (al-mardud). Understand that this is a settled rule among logicians.
The Arabic phrase is: 'وَعِنْدَهُمْ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ المَرْدُودِ أَنْ تَدْخُلَ الأَحْكَامُ فِي الحُدُودِ'.
2
Understand the rationale: judgment depends on conception
The commentator explains: 'because the judgment about something is a branch of its conception' (لِأَنَّ الحُكْمَ عَلَى الشَّيْءِ فَرْعٌ عَنْ تَصَوُّرِهِ). This means to judge something, you must first have a mental picture of it.
Conception (tasawwur) is the mental grasp of a meaning without affirmation or denial.
3
Trace the circularity (al-dawr)
If the definition includes a judgment, then the definition already requires conception of the definiendum (the thing being defined). But the definition is supposed to produce that conception. Thus, each depends on the other, forming a vicious circle.
The text says: 'يَلْزَمُ الدَّورُ لِتَوَقُفِ كُلٍّ مِنَ التَّعْرِيفِ وَالمُعَرَّفِ عَلَى الْآخَرِ'.
4
Consider a concrete example
Think of defining 'agent' (fa'il) as 'the noun that is raised'. The judgment 'raised' (marfu') is part of the definition. Does it presuppose knowing what 'agent' is? If so, the definition would be circular.
This example will be resolved in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَعِنْدَهُمْ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ المَرْدُودِ أَنْ تَدْخُلَ الأَحْكَامُ فِي الحُدُودِ
قَوْلُهُ : (وَعِنْدَهُمْ) أَي : المَنَاطِقَةُ، وَإِنَّمَا خَصَّهُمْ بِالذِّكْرِ ; لأَنَّهُمُ البَاحِثُونَ أَوَّلاً عَنْ ذَلِكَ، وَإِلَّا فَعِنْدَ غَيْرِهِمْ كَذَلِكَ.
وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ المُرَادَ : عِنْدَ العُلَمَاءِ مُطلَقاً .
وَالظَّرْفُ عَلَى كُلِّ مِنَ الِاحْتِمَالَيْنِ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِقَوْلِهِ : «المَرْدُودِ ، وَقَدَّمَهُ مَعَ كَوْنِ العَامِلِ مُضَافاً إِلَيْهِ : وَصِلَةٌ لِـ «أَلْ» ; لِلضَّرُورَةِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ جُمْلَةِ المَرْدُودِ... إلخ) أَيْ : لِأَنَّ الحُكْمَ عَلَى الشَّيْءِ فَرْعٌ عَنْ تَصَوُّرِهِ، فَهُوَ مُتَوَفِّفٌ عَلَى المَحْكُوم عَلَيْهِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَلْزَمُ الدَّورُ ; لِتَوَقُفِ كُلٌّ مِنَ التَّعْرِيفِ وَالمُعَرَّفِ عَلَى الْآخَرِ، وَقَدْ دُفِعَ هَذَا الدَّوْرُ بِأَوْجُهِ مَا بَيْنَ بَعِيدٍ وَغَيْرِ سَدِيدٍ.
وَدَفَعَهُ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ : بِأَنَّ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ بِالحُكْمِ المَذْكُورِ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ المَأْخُوذُ جِنْساً فِي التَّعْرِيفِ، لَا المُعَرَّفُ ; أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ بِالرَّفْعِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ ابْنِ أَجْرُومَ لِلفَاعِلِ بـ: «أَنَّهُ الاِسْمُ المَرْفُوعُ ... إلخ، هُوَ الاِسْمُ ، لَا الفَاعِلُ، حَتَّى يَلْزَمَ الدَّورُ ، فَلْيُتامَّلْ .
And according to them, from among the rejected things is that judgments enter into definitions. His statement: 'And according to them' i.e., the logicians. He specifically mentioned them because they are the primary investigators of that, otherwise it is likewise according to others. And it is possible that the intended meaning is 'according to the scholars' absolutely. The adverb in both probabilities is attached to his statement 'the rejected', and he advanced it despite the operator being added to it: it is a connector for 'al' due to necessity. And his statement: 'from among the rejected... etc.' means: because the judgment about something is a branch of its conception, so it depends on the thing judged, and then circularity would follow because each of the definition and the definiendum depends on the other. And this circularity has been refuted by various ways, some far-fetched and some unsound. And a certain verifier (al-Sabban) refuted it by saying that the thing judged by the mentioned judgment in the definition is the genus taken in the definition, not the definiendum. Do you not see that the thing judged by the state of being raised (raf') in Ibn Ajurrum's definition of the agent (fa'il) as 'the noun raised... etc.' is the noun, not the agent, so that circularity does not follow? So let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn statement that including judgments (ahkam) in definitions (hudud) is among the rejected practices. The commentator begins by clarifying the pronoun 'هم' (them) as referring to logicians (manatiqa), but notes it could also mean scholars universally. He then explains the grammatical connection of the adverb 'عندهم' to 'المردود' and mentions that the matn advanced it for emphasis. The key reason for rejection is then given: a judgment about something depends on prior conception (tasawwur) of that thing. If the judgment itself forms part of the definition, then the definition presupposes conception of the definiendum, and the definiendum's conception is supposed to come from the definition — hence circularity (dawr). The commentator notes that attempts to resolve this circularity vary in soundness. He then presents the resolution by a certain verifier (identified in the margin as al-Sabban): the judgment in the definition actually applies to the genus (jins) taken in the definition, not to the definiendum itself. He illustrates with the grammatical definition of fa'il (agent) by Ibn Ajurrum: 'the noun that is raised'. Here the judgment of being raised attaches to 'noun' (the genus), not to 'fa'il' (the definiendum). Thus the circularity is avoided because the judgment's subject is the genus, which is already known, and the definiendum is restricted by additional conditions like 'that is raised by the agent'. The commentary invites the reader to ponder this subtle point.

الحكم
التعريف

Resolving Circularity: Judgment Applies to the Genus
Resolving circularity by showing judgments apply to genus, not definiendum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the verifier's resolution to a definition containing a judgment and verify that no circularity remains.

Core Idea
Circularity is avoided when the judgment in a definition applies to the genus, not the definiendum.

The previous lesson showed that including judgments in definitions can lead to circularity. However, some definitions that appear to contain judgments are actually valid. The commentator presents a resolution by a verifier (al-Sabban): the judgment does not apply to the definiendum itself, but to the genus taken in the definition. This subtle distinction saves the definition from circularity. Let's see it in action.

Guided Path
1
Recall the circularity problem
A definition containing a judgment would be circular if the judgment presupposes conception of the definiendum. For example, defining 'agent' (fa'il) as 'the noun that is raised' seems to assume knowledge of 'agent'.
The judgment 'raised' is about 'agent' if understood directly.
2
Understand the verifier's resolution
The verifier says: 'the thing judged by the mentioned judgment in the definition is the genus taken in the definition, not the definiendum.' The judgment attaches to the genus, which is already known.
The Arabic: 'المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ بِالحُكْمِ المَذْكُورِ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ المَأْخُوذُ جِنْساً فِي التَّعْرِيفِ، لَا المُعَرَّفُ'.
3
Apply to the example: Ibn Ajurrum's definition of fa'il
In 'al-ismu al-marfu'u' (the noun that is raised), the judgment 'raised' (marfu') is about 'ism' (noun), not about 'fa'il'. 'Ism' is the genus, 'fa'il' is the definiendum. The definition adds further conditions (like being raised by the agent) to restrict the genus to the agent.
Thus, knowledge of 'fa'il' is not needed to understand the judgment; 'ism' is known independently.
4
Verify no circularity
Since the judgment applies to the genus (ism), and the genus is already known, the definition provides new information (the differentia) to distinguish the definiendum within that genus. The circularity disappears.
The commentator says: 'so that circularity does not follow' (حَتَّى يَلْزَمَ الدَّورُ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَعِنْدَهُمْ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ المَرْدُودِ أَنْ تَدْخُلَ الأَحْكَامُ فِي الحُدُودِ
قَوْلُهُ : (وَعِنْدَهُمْ) أَي : المَنَاطِقَةُ، وَإِنَّمَا خَصَّهُمْ بِالذِّكْرِ ; لأَنَّهُمُ البَاحِثُونَ أَوَّلاً عَنْ ذَلِكَ، وَإِلَّا فَعِنْدَ غَيْرِهِمْ كَذَلِكَ.
وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ المُرَادَ : عِنْدَ العُلَمَاءِ مُطلَقاً .
وَالظَّرْفُ عَلَى كُلِّ مِنَ الِاحْتِمَالَيْنِ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِقَوْلِهِ : «المَرْدُودِ ، وَقَدَّمَهُ مَعَ كَوْنِ العَامِلِ مُضَافاً إِلَيْهِ : وَصِلَةٌ لِـ «أَلْ» ; لِلضَّرُورَةِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ جُمْلَةِ المَرْدُودِ... إلخ) أَيْ : لِأَنَّ الحُكْمَ عَلَى الشَّيْءِ فَرْعٌ عَنْ تَصَوُّرِهِ، فَهُوَ مُتَوَفِّفٌ عَلَى المَحْكُوم عَلَيْهِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَلْزَمُ الدَّورُ ; لِتَوَقُفِ كُلٌّ مِنَ التَّعْرِيفِ وَالمُعَرَّفِ عَلَى الْآخَرِ، وَقَدْ دُفِعَ هَذَا الدَّوْرُ بِأَوْجُهِ مَا بَيْنَ بَعِيدٍ وَغَيْرِ سَدِيدٍ.
وَدَفَعَهُ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ : بِأَنَّ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ بِالحُكْمِ المَذْكُورِ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ المَأْخُوذُ جِنْساً فِي التَّعْرِيفِ، لَا المُعَرَّفُ ; أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ بِالرَّفْعِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ ابْنِ أَجْرُومَ لِلفَاعِلِ بـ: «أَنَّهُ الاِسْمُ المَرْفُوعُ ... إلخ، هُوَ الاِسْمُ ، لَا الفَاعِلُ، حَتَّى يَلْزَمَ الدَّورُ ، فَلْيُتامَّلْ .
And according to them, from among the rejected things is that judgments enter into definitions. His statement: 'And according to them' i.e., the logicians. He specifically mentioned them because they are the primary investigators of that, otherwise it is likewise according to others. And it is possible that the intended meaning is 'according to the scholars' absolutely. The adverb in both probabilities is attached to his statement 'the rejected', and he advanced it despite the operator being added to it: it is a connector for 'al' due to necessity. And his statement: 'from among the rejected... etc.' means: because the judgment about something is a branch of its conception, so it depends on the thing judged, and then circularity would follow because each of the definition and the definiendum depends on the other. And this circularity has been refuted by various ways, some far-fetched and some unsound. And a certain verifier (al-Sabban) refuted it by saying that the thing judged by the mentioned judgment in the definition is the genus taken in the definition, not the definiendum. Do you not see that the thing judged by the state of being raised (raf') in Ibn Ajurrum's definition of the agent (fa'il) as 'the noun raised... etc.' is the noun, not the agent, so that circularity does not follow? So let it be pondered.

Metaphorical Use of 'Hudud' for 'Rusum'
Author interprets 'hudud' metaphorically as 'rusum'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the term 'hudud' is used metaphorically for 'rusum' in logical texts and explain the reasoning based on the nature of definitions.

Core Idea
Hudud can mean descriptions when the context involves accidental attributes.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies that the word 'hudud' in the matn does not refer to essential definitions but to descriptions. This interpretation is necessary because judgments, being accidental, cannot be part of an essential definition. Understanding this metaphorical usage is crucial for correctly interpreting later logical discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic term
Locate the word 'hudud' in the phrase 'an tadkhula al-ahkamu fi al-hudud'. Note that it is usually translated as 'definitions' but something seems off if judgments are to 'enter' them.
The term appears after grammatical variants are explained.
2
Recall the standard distinction
Remember that a hadd is an essential definition (genus + differentia), while a rasm is an accidental description (properties). Here, judgments are 'ahkam' which are accidental rulings, not part of the essence.
In logic, 'hadd' and 'rasm' are distinct, but in metaphor they may be conflated.
3
Analyze why literal reading fails
If 'hudud' meant essential definitions, there would be no need to warn that judgments cannot enter them because it is obvious—judgments are not part of the quiddity. The warning indicates that someone might imagine they could enter, which is only plausible for descriptions.
This is the key logical argument: the contextual indication (qarina) reveals the intended meaning.
4
Identify the metaphorical relation
The commentator offers two possibilities: one-step metaphor via opposition (hadd vs. rasm are opposites), or two-step via specification (first 'hudud' means definitions in general, then narrowed to descriptions as a subset).
Both rely on a relation between the literal and figurative meanings.
5
Confirm the interpretation
Check that understanding 'hudud' as 'rusum' resolves the tension: judgments can properly be part of a description because descriptions include accidental attributes. The commentator credits al-Malawi for this insight.
This interpretation is a standard move in logic commentaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (أَنْ تَدْخُلَ ... إلخ) بِفَتْحِ النَّاءِ وَضَمِّ الخَاءِ، أَوْ بِالعَكْسِ، أَوْ بِضَمِّ التَّاءِ وَكَسْرِ الخَاءِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : ((الأَحْكَامُ) بِالرَّفْعِ عَلَى الفَاعِليَّةِ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ، وَعَلَى النِّيَابَةِ عَنِ الفَاعِلِ عَلَى الثَّانِي، وَبِالنَّصْبِ عَلَى المَفْعُولِيَّةِ عَلَى الثَّالِثِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (فِي الحُدُودِ) أَرَادَ بِهَا هُنَا : الرُّسُومُ مَجَازاً : - إِمَّا بِمَرْتَبَةٍ إِنْ أُرِيدَ بِهَا الرُّسُومُ مِنْ أَوَّلِ الأَمْرِ ; لِعَلَاقَةِ : التَّضَادٌ . - أَوْ بِمَرْتَبَتَيْنِ إِنْ أُرِيدَ بِهَا التَّعَارِيفُ ، ثُمَّ أُرِيدَ بِهَا الرُّسُومُ; لِعَلَاقَةِ: الخُصُوص وَالعُمُومِ، وَالقَرِينَةُ : أَنَّهُ لَا يُتَوَهَّمُ إِمْكَانُ دُخُولِهَا فِي الحُدُودِ حَتَّى يَحْتَاجَ لِلتَّنْبِيهِ عَلَى انْتِفَائِهِ; لِأَنَّ الحُكْمَ لَيْسَ جُزءاً مِنَ المَاهِيَّةِ بِخِلَافِ الرُّسُومِ ، فَإِنَّهُ قَدْ يُتَوَهَّمُ دُخُولُهَا فِيهِ، فَاحْتَاجَ لِلتَّنْبِيهِ عَلَى انْتِفَائِهِ; أَفَادَهُ المَلوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ».
His statement: 'that they enter... etc.' with a fatha on the nun and a damma on the kha', or the reverse, or with a damma on the ta' and a kasra on the kha'. And his statement: 'the judgments' with raf' (nominative) as subject in the first reading, as substitute for the subject in the second, and with nasb (accusative) as object in the third. And his statement: 'in the definitions' — he intended here, by it, the descriptions (rusum) metaphorically: either in one step, if the descriptions are intended from the first, by the relation of opposition; or in two steps, if definitions are intended first, then descriptions are intended by the relation of specific and general. And the contextual indication is that it is not imagined that they (judgments) could enter into (essential) definitions such that one would need to warn against it, because the judgment is not a part of the quiddity (mahiyya), unlike descriptions, for it might be imagined that they enter into them, so one needed to warn against its negation. This is what al-Malawi conveyed in his larger commentary.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and metaphorical clarification of the key phrase 'an tadkhula al-ahkamu fi al-hudud'. The commentator first notes three possible vowellings of the verb 'tadkhula': (1) 'tadkhula' with fatha on the nun and damma on the kha' (active voice), (2) the reverse (passive voice? actually 'tudkhala' with damma on ta' and fatha on kha'? The text says 'or the reverse' which likely means 'tudkhala' with damma on ta' and fatha on kha'), and (3) 'tudkhila' with damma on ta' and kasra on kha' (causative? or another passive). Correspondingly, 'al-ahkamu' is read as nominative (subject) in the first, as nominative (substitute for subject) in the second, and as accusative (object) in the third. This shows the commentator's concern for the precise reading. Then he turns to the word 'hudud' (definitions). He asserts that here it means 'rusum' (descriptions) metaphorically. He provides two possibilities for the metaphor: (a) one-step metaphor: the word 'hudud' is originally meant for descriptions from the start, via the relation of opposition (because definitions and descriptions are opposites in that definitions capture essence while descriptions capture accidents); (b) two-step metaphor: first 'hudud' is intended for definitions in general, then narrowed to descriptions via the relation of specific to general (since descriptions are a type of definition in the broader sense). The reason for this metaphorical interpretation is given: it is not conceivable that judgments could be part of an essential definition (hadd), because judgments are accidental and not part of the quiddity. Hence, there would be no need to warn against their inclusion in hadd — but the matn does warn, so the intended meaning must be rusum (where accidental attributes are appropriate). He credits this insight to al-Malawi's larger commentary.

Key Terms
الحدود
الرسوم
المجاز
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because judgments cannot be parts of essential definitions, so the intended meaning must be descriptions.
C) Because the author al-Malawi said so.
D) Because the grammatical reading requires it.

Key Takeaway
The term 'hudud' in logical contexts can refer to descriptions when the subject matter involves accidental attributes, guided by the contextual indication that warns against a literal reading.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Why Rulings Cannot Enter Essential Definitions
Author explains why rulings cannot be part of definitions and that 'hudud' here means 'rusum' metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why rulings (ahkam) cannot be part of essential definitions (hudud) and identify when the term 'hudud' is used metaphorically to mean descriptions (rusum).

Core Idea
Rulings are accidental, so they cannot be in essential definitions; they can only be in descriptions.

The passage addresses a key rule in logic: rulings cannot be included in definitions. But the commentator clarifies that the term 'hudud' here is used metaphorically to mean 'rusum' (descriptions), because it is impossible for rulings—being accidental—to be part of the essence. This lesson will help you understand the distinction between essential definitions and descriptions, and why rulings are excluded from the former but can appear in the latter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The passage states that according to the logicians, rulings entering into definitions is rejected. Note the variant readings of the phrase 'an tadkhula al-ahkamu...' that the commentator discusses.
The variant readings affect grammar but not the core meaning.
2
Understand the metaphorical use of 'hudud'
The commentator says that 'hudud' here means 'rusum' metaphorically. He provides two ways this metaphor works: either directly due to opposition, or via the relation of particularity and generality.
This explains why the rule is plausible: rulings can be part of descriptions but not essential definitions.
3
Recognize the reason for the prohibition
Rulings are not part of the essence (mahiyya). Essential definitions consist only of genus and differentia, which constitute the essence. Since rulings are accidental, they cannot be part of an essential definition.
Descriptions, on the other hand, can include accidents, so rulings are allowed there.
4
Apply the distinction to a real example
Consider the definition of 'wine' in fiqh. An essential definition would be 'an intoxicating beverage made from grapes' (genus: beverage, differentia: intoxicating). The ruling 'forbidden' is not part of this essence; it is a ruling attached by the Lawgiver.
This clarifies why the rule is important for correct conceptualization.

Source Text

- إِمَّا بِمَرْتَبَةٍ إِنْ أُرِيدَ بِهَا الرُّسُومُ مِنْ أَوَّلِ الأَمْرِ ؛ لِعَلَاقَةِ : التَّضَادٌ .
- أَوْ بِمَرْتَبَتَيْنِ إِنْ أُرِيدَ بِهَا التَّعَارِيفُ ، ثُمَّ أُرِيدَ بِهَا الرُّسُومُ؛ لِعَلَاقَةِ: الخُصُوص وَالعُمُومِ، وَالقَرِينَةُ : أَنَّهُ لَا يُتَوَهَّمُ إِمْكَانُ دُخُولِهَا فِي الحُدُودِ حَتَّى يَحْتَاجَ لِلتَّنْبِيهِ عَلَى انْتِفَائِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ الحُكْمَ لَيْسَ جُزءاً مِنَ المَاهِيَّةِ بِخِلَافِ الرُّسُومِ ، فَإِنَّهُ قَدْ يُتَوَهَّمُ دُخُولُهَا فِيهِ، فَاحْتَاجَ لِلتَّنْبِيهِ عَلَى انْتِفَائِهِ؛ أَفَادَهُ المَلوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ
And according to them, from the category of rejected things is that rulings enter into definitions. His statement: ('that they enter... etc.') with fatha on the nun and dhamma on the kha', or with the opposite, or with dhamma on the ta' and kasra on the kha'. And his statement: ('the rulings') with raf' as subject on the first reading, as substitute for the subject on the second, and with nasb as object on the third. And his statement: ('in the definitions') he intended here the descriptions metaphorically: either in one step if descriptions are intended from the beginning, due to the relation of opposition; or in two steps if definitions are intended first and then descriptions are intended, due to the relation of particularity and generality, and the evidence is that it is not imagined possible for them [rulings] to enter into essential definitions such that one would need to point out their impossibility, because the ruling is not a part of the essence, unlike descriptions, for it might be imagined that they enter into them, so he needed to point out their impossibility. This is benefited from al-Malawi in his major work.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage addresses the rule that rulings (ahkam) cannot be included in definitions (hudud). The commentator first explains the different possible readings of the phrase 'an tadkhula al-ahkamu,' detailing variant vowellings and grammatical analyses. He then clarifies that the term 'hudud' in the matn is used metaphorically to mean 'rusum' (descriptions), not essential definitions. Two possibilities for this metaphorical usage are given: either the term is transferred from essential definitions to descriptions in one step due to opposition, or in two steps—first the general term 'definitions' is used for all definitions, then it is narrowed to descriptions as a specific type, due to the relation of particularity and generality. The reason for this metaphorical interpretation is that it is not conceivable that rulings, which are accidental, could be part of the essence (mahiyya); thus they cannot be parts of essential definitions. However, they can be parts of descriptions, since descriptions include accidental properties. Therefore, the statement 'rulings enter into definitions' is rejected, and if someone thinks it possible, it would only be in descriptions, not essential definitions.

Key Terms
الأَحْكَامُ (al-ahkām): rulings, accidental properties
الحُدُودُ (al-ḥudūd): essential definitions, here used metaphorically
الرُّسُومُ (al-rusūm): descriptions, definitions that may include accidents

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator interpret 'hudud' as 'rusum' in the statement 'rulings enter into definitions'?
A) Because rulings are always part of descriptions.
B) Because it is impossible for rulings to be part of essential definitions, so the statement must refer to descriptions.
C) Because the original author made a grammatical error.
D) Because definitions and descriptions are the same thing.

Key Takeaway
Rulings are accidental and cannot be part of essential definitions, but they can be part of descriptions (rusum); 'hudud' in this rule is metaphorical for 'rusum'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the definition of 'wine' (khamr) in fiqh: 'an intoxicating beverage made from grapes' (essential) vs 'a forbidden intoxicating beverage' (including a ruling). Which is a hadd and which is a rasm? Explain based on the passage.
?
Construct a definition of 'salah' (prayer) as a hadd (without rulings) and as a rasm (including the ruling of obligation). Use the distinction from the lesson.

Hadd and Rasm

The Use of 'Aw' (Or) in Definitions vs Descriptions
Author prohibits 'aw' for division in definitions, permits in descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of 'aw' (division, doubt, obscurity, choice) and determine their permissibility in definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum) according to the logicians.

Core Idea
Not all 'or's are equal: 'aw' for division breaks definitions but serves descriptions.

In Arabic logic, the particle 'aw' (or) carries different meanings, each with distinct rules in definition and description. This lesson will guide you through the four types of 'aw' and their permissibility in hudud and rusum, using examples from the tradition. You will also examine the scholarly disagreement over 'aw' for choice and learn why al-Attar rejected its use in definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of 'aw'
List the four categories: taqsim (division), shakk (doubt), ibham (obscurity), and takhyir (choice). Understand their distinct functions.
Division 'aw' separates a genus into species; doubt 'aw' expresses uncertainty; obscurity 'aw' implies indistinctness; choice 'aw' offers alternatives.
2
Apply the basic rule to division 'aw'
The author rules that 'aw' for division is prohibited in hudud (essential definitions) because a single species cannot have two differentiae. However, it is allowed in rusum (descriptions) because proper accidents can be multiple.
Example: 'The animal is rational or irrational' is division; 'Man is a rational animal or a laugh' (alternative proper accidents) is description.
3
Analyze the controversy over 'aw' for choice
Some scholars, like al-Isfahani, allow 'aw' for choice in descriptions (e.g., 'Man is a rational animal, or a writer') because the definer chooses which proper accident to mention. Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya extended this to definitions, citing the definition of nazar: 'thought leading to knowledge or predominant opinion'.
The nazar example uses 'aw' for division, not choice, which is a key flaw.
4
Evaluate al-Attar's critique
Al-Attar refutes Zakariyya: the 'aw' in the nazar definition is for division, not choice. Moreover, choice 'aw' must follow a request (like 'marry Hind or her sister'), and definitions contain no such request. The definer's choice does not involve the addressee.
Al-Attar shows the importance of correctly identifying the type of 'aw' before applying permissibility rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَجُوزُ فِي الحُدُودِ ذِكْرُ أَوْ وَجَائِةٌ فِي الرَّسْمِ فَادْرِ مَا رَوَوْا
قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا يَجُوزُ فِي الحُدُودِ... إلخ) الفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الحُدُودِ وَالرُّسُومِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي أَوْ» الَّتي لِلتَّقْسِيمِ ، وَأَمَّا الَّتي لِلشَّكٌ أَوْ لِلإِبْهَامِ فَهِيَ مُمْتَنِعَةٌ فِيهِمَا .
وَلَمْ يَتَعَرَّضُوا لِـ (أَوْ) الَّتِي لِلتَّحْيِيرِ، وَاسْتَظْهَرَ بَعْضُ المُحَفِّقِينَ جَوَازَهَا فِي الرُّسُومِ؛ كَانْ تَقُولَ : الإِنْسَانُ : حَيَوَانٌ ضَاحِدٌ ، أَوْ كَاتِبٌ»؛ بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّكَ مُخَيَّرٌ بَيْنَ التَّمْيِيزِ بِالخَاصَّةِ الأُولَى، والتَّمْيِيزِ بِالخَاصَّةِ الثَّانِيَةِ.
وَمَا ذَكَرَهُ المُصَنِّفُ مِنْ عَدَمِ جَوَازِ أَوْ الَّتِي لِلتَّقْسِيمِ فِي الحُدُودِ وَجَوَازِهَا فِي الرُّسُومِ لَمْ يَنْفَرِدْ بِهِ، بَلْ صَرَّحَ بِهِ الأَصْبَهَانِيُّ حَيْثُ قَالَ : وَتَجُوزُ أَوْ فِي الرَّسْمِ، بِخِلَافِ الحَدِّ؛ لِأَنَّ النَّوْعَ الوَاحِدَ يَسْتَحِيلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ لَهُ فَصْلَانِ عَلَى البَدَلِ بِخِلَافِ الخَاصَّنَيْنِ عَلَى البَدَلِ. اهـ. وَخَالَفَ شَيْخُ الإِسْلَامِ زَكَرِيَّا فِي ذَلِكَ، فَجَوَّزَهَا فِي الحُدُودِ، وَاسْتَنَدَ إِلَى تَعْرِيفِهِمُ النَّظَرَ أَنَّهُ الفِكْرُ المُؤَدِّي إِلَى عِلْمٍ، أَوْ غَلَبَةِ الظَّنِّ»؛ بِمَعْنَى أَنَّ النَّظَرَ قِسْمَانِ : أَحَدُهُمَا : يُؤَدِّي إِلَى عِلْمٍ ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : العَالَمُ حَادِتْ، وَكُلُّ حَادِثٍ لَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِنْ مُحْدِثٍ وَثَانِيهِمَا : يُؤَدِّي إِلَى غَلَبَةِ ظَنِّ ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ يَطُوفُ لَيْلاً بِالسَّلَاحِ، وَكُلُّ مَنْ هُوَ كَذَلِكَ فَهُوَ لِصٌ .
قال العطار : هذا فاسد لفظاً ومعنى : أمَّا لفظاً : فلأنَّ «أو» التي للتخيير هي الواقعة بعد ما يدلُّ على الطلب، وقد امتنع الجمع بين متعاطفيها ؛ كقولك : «تزوّج هند أو أختها»، ولا طلب هنا . وأمَّا معنى فلانه جعل التخيير من جهة المخاطب، كما يفيده قوله : «بمعنى مخير ... إلخ»، ومعلوم أنَّ حقَّ التَّخيير إنَّما يكون لذاكر التَّعريف، كما يُفيده قوله : «بين التمييز؛ لأنَّ التَّمييز إنَّما يكون من جهة المعرّف، فكلامُهُ متضاربٌ .
And it is not permitted in definitions to mention 'aw' (or) and it is permissible in the description, so understand what they narrated. His statement: ('And it is not permitted in definitions... etc.') The difference between definitions and descriptions is only concerning 'aw' that is for division. As for that which is for doubt or for obscurity, it is prohibited in both. And they did not address 'aw' for choice (takhyir), and some verifiers considered its permissibility in descriptions, such as you say: 'Man is a rational animal, or a writer', meaning that you are given a choice between distinguishing by the first proper accident or distinguishing by the second proper accident. And what the author mentioned about the impermissibility of 'aw' for division in definitions and its permissibility in descriptions is not unique to him; rather al-Isfahani stated it explicitly when he said: 'And 'aw' is permissible in the description, unlike the definition, because a single species cannot have two differentiae alternatively, unlike two proper accidents alternatively.' End quote. And Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya opposed this, deeming it permissible in definitions, relying on their definition of 'nazar' (speculative reasoning) as 'thought leading to knowledge or predominant opinion', meaning that nazar is of two types: one leads to knowledge, as in 'The world is originated, and every originated thing necessarily has an originator'; and the second leads to predominant opinion, as in 'Zayd roams at night with weapons, and everyone who does so is a thief.' Al-Attar said: This is invalid in wording and meaning: as for wording, because 'aw' for choice occurs after what indicates a request, and it is not permissible to combine the two conjoined items, as in 'Marry Hind or her sister', and there is no request here. As for meaning, he made the choice from the side of the addressee, as indicated by his statement 'meaning you are given a choice... etc.', but it is known that the right of choice belongs to the presenter of the definition, as indicated by his statement 'between distinguishing', because distinguishing is from the side of the definer, so his words are contradictory.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage addresses the use of the particle 'aw' (or) in definitions and descriptions. The author states that 'aw' is not allowed in essential definitions (hudud) but is permissible in descriptions (rusum). The commentator specifies that this distinction applies only to 'aw' used for division (taqsim). 'Aw' used for doubt (shakk) or obscurity (ibham) is prohibited in both. The commentator then mentions that some scholars considered 'aw' for choice (takhyir) permissible in descriptions, giving the example 'Man is a rational animal, or a writer,' meaning the definer can choose which proper accident to mention. The author's ruling is supported by al-Isfahani, who argues that a single species cannot have two differentiae alternatively (since each differentia is essential), but two proper accidents can be alternative descriptions. A dissenting view from Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya is noted: he allowed 'aw' in definitions, citing the definition of 'nazar' (speculative reasoning) as 'thought leading to knowledge or predominant opinion'—here 'aw' indicates division into two types, not disjunction of differentiae. However, al-Attar criticizes this, arguing that the 'aw' in this example is for division, not for choice, and that the definition given for 'nazar' actually uses 'aw' for division, not for alternative differentiae. Al-Attar's critique highlights that the 'aw' for choice must follow a request, and there is no request in definitions; moreover, the choice in definitions belongs to the definer, not the addressee, so the example is flawed.

Key Terms
أَوْ
التَّقْسِيمُ
التَّحْيِيرُ
النَّظَرُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because a single species cannot have two differentiae alternatively, but two proper accidents can be alternatives.
C) Because 'aw' is only used for division in descriptions, not in definitions.
D) Because the logicians did not consider 'aw' in definitions.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'aw' must be classified by its meaning (division, doubt, obscurity, choice) before applying the rule: division 'aw' is prohibited in hudud but allowed in rusum; other types are prohibited in both, except choice 'aw' which is debated for descriptions.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
7 min
The Rule of 'Aw' in Definitions and Descriptions
Explains the rule about using 'aw' in hadd vs. rasm and debates a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of 'aw' are permissible in hadd and rasm, and evaluate counterexamples like the definition of nazar.

Core Idea
Not all 'or's are equal: 'aw' for division is banned in definition but allowed in description.

In this lesson, we explore a subtle rule from classical logic: the use of the particle 'aw' (or) in definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum). The passage distinguishes between different meanings of 'aw' and presents a debate over whether the definition of nazar (consideration) as 'thought leading to knowledge or preponderant certainty' violates the rule. Understanding this rule sharpens your ability to evaluate definitions rigorously in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Read the matn line: 'It is not permissible in definitions to mention 'or', and it is permissible in descriptions.' Note that this is the default rule.
The rule applies specifically to 'aw' for division (taqsīm).
2
Distinguish types of 'aw'
The commentary clarifies that 'aw' for doubt (shakk) or obscurity (ibhām) is impermissible in both hadd and rasm. 'Aw' for option (takhīr) is debated but some allow it in rasm. Identify which type is the focus of the rule.
3
Analyze the counterexample
Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya uses the definition of nazar as 'thought leading to knowledge or preponderant certainty' to argue that 'aw' for division can appear in a hadd. Examine this definition: it uses 'aw' to divide the outcome of thought into two types.
The definition of nazar is a well-known example in kalam.
4
Evaluate the response
The commentator counters: (1) This definition may not be a true hadd because it uses accidental properties (preponderant certainty is not essential); (2) even if accepted, it contains two separate definitions, and the rule only forbids 'aw' in a single definition. Assess which response is more convincing.
This shows how critical refinement works: the rule is preserved even when challenged.

(٤٩) وَلَا يَجُوزُ فِي الحُدُودِ ذِكْرُ أَوْ وَجَائِزٌ فِي الرَّسْمِ فَادْرِ مَا رَوَوْا
قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا يَجُوزُ فِي الحُدُودِ... إلخ) الفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الحُدُودِ وَالرُّسُومِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي أَوْ الَّتي لِلتَّقْسِيمِ، وَأَمَّا الَّتي لِلشَّكِّ أَوْ لِلإِبْهَامِ فَهِيَ مُمْتَنِعَةٌ فِيهِمَا .
وَلَمْ يَتَعَرَّضُوا لِـ (أَوْ) الَّتِي لِلتَّحْيِيرِ، وَاسْتَظْهَرَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ جَوَازَهَا فِي الرُّسُومِ؛ كَانْ تَقُولَ : الإِنْسَانُ : حَيَوَانٌ ضَاحِكٌ، أَوْ كَاتِبٌ؛ بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّكَ مُخَيَّرٌ بَيْنَ التَّمْيِيزِ بِالخَاصَّةِ الأَوْلَى، وَالتَّمْيِيزِ بِالخَاصَّةِ الثَّانِيَةِ .
وَمَا ذَكَرَهُ المُصَنِّفُ مِنْ عَدَمِ جَوَازِ أَوْ الَّتِي لِلتَّقْسِيمِ فِي الحُدُودِ وَجَوَازِهَا فِي الرُّسُومِ لَمْ يَنْفَرِدْ بِهِ، بَلْ صَرَّحَ بِهِ الأَصْبَهَانِيُّ حَيْثُ قَالَ : وَتَجُوزُ أَوْ فِي الرَّسْمِ، بِخِلَافِ الحَدِّ؛ لِأَنَّ النَّوْعَ الوَاحِدَ يَسْتَحِيلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ لَهُ فَصْلَانِ عَلَى البَدَلِ بِخِلَافِ الخَاصَّتَيْنِ عَلَى البَدَلِ . وَخَالَفَ شَيْخُ الإِسْلَامِ زَكَرِيَّا فِي ذَلِكَ، فَجَوَّزَهَا فِي الحُدُودِ، وَاسْتَنَدَ إِلَى تَعْرِيفِهِمُ النَّظَرَ أَنَّهُ الفِكْرُ المُؤَدِّي إِلَى عِلْمٍ، أَوْ غَلَبَةِ الظَّنِّ؛ بِمَعْنَى أَنَّ النَّظَرَ قِسْمَانِ : أَحَدُهُمَا : يُؤَدِّي إِلَى عِلْمٍ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : العَالَمُ حَادِثٌ، وَكُلُّ حَادِثٍ لَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِنْ مُحْدِثٍ وَثَانِيهِمَا : يُؤَدِّي إِلَى غَلَبَةِ ظَنِّ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ يَطُوفُ لَيْلاً بِالسَّلَاحِ، وَكُلُّ مَنْ هُوَ كَذَلِكَ فَهُوَ لِصٌ .
وَقَدْ يُمْنَعُ كَوْنَ ذَلِكَ التَّعْرِيفِ حَدًّا، وَعَلَى تَسْلِيمِ ذَلِكَ فَهُوَ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ حَدَّانِ، وَالمَنْعُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الحَدِّ الوَاحِدِ .
Section on definitions. (49) It is not permissible in definitions to mention 'or', and it is permissible in description, so understand what they narrated. His statement: (It is not permissible in definitions... etc.) The distinction between definitions and descriptions is only in 'or' that is for division. As for that which is for doubt or obscurity, it is forbidden in both. They did not address 'or' that is for option, and some of the verifying scholars deemed it permissible in descriptions, such as you say: 'Man: a laughing animal, or a writer', meaning you are given a choice between distinguishing by the first property and distinguishing by the second property. And what the author mentioned, of the impermissibility of 'or' for division in definitions and its permissibility in descriptions, is not unique to him; rather al-Isfahani explicitly stated it when he said: 'Or is permissible in description, unlike definition, because a single species is impossible to have two differentiae on an alternative basis, unlike two properties on an alternative basis.' And Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya disagreed with that, and permitted it in definitions, citing their definition of consideration (nazar): 'It is thought leading to knowledge or preponderant certainty', meaning that consideration is two types: one leads to knowledge, as in your saying: 'The world is originated, and every originated thing necessarily has an originator'; and the second leads to preponderant certainty, as in your saying: 'Zayd walks at night with weapons, and everyone who does so is a thief.' And it may be prevented that that definition is a definition (hadd), and if that is granted, then in reality it is two definitions, and the prevention is only in the single definition.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage explains the rule regarding the use of the word 'or' (أو) in logical definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum). The author states that 'or' is not permissible in definitions but is permissible in descriptions. The commentary clarifies that this distinction applies specifically to 'or' used for division (أو التي للتقسيم), where it indicates alternatives. 'Or' used for doubt or obscurity is impermissible in both. As for 'or' used for option (التخيير), some scholars allowed it in descriptions, exemplified by saying 'Man is a laughing animal, or a writer'—meaning you may choose either property to distinguish man. The author's rule is supported by al-Isfahani, who argued that a single species cannot have two differentiae alternatively, but can have two properties alternatively. However, Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya disagreed and allowed 'or' in definitions, citing the definition of 'nazar' (consideration) as 'thought leading to knowledge or preponderant certainty.' He argued this is a valid definition, though it uses 'or' for division. The commentary then addresses potential objections: first, that this definition may not be a true definition because it includes extrinsic attributes; second, even if it is a definition, it is actually two definitions in one, and the rule against 'or' applies only to a single definition. Thus, the passage carefully delineates the conditions under which 'or' can appear in definitions and descriptions, using the example of nazar to illustrate the debate.

Key Terms
أو
حد
رسم
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'Aw' for division (taqsīm)
B) 'Aw' for doubt (shakk)
C) 'Aw' for option (takhīr)
D) 'Aw' for obscurity (ibhām)

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of 'aw' in definitions depends on its meaning type; 'aw' for division is banned in hadd but allowed in rasm, with nuanced exceptions as debated by scholars.

Rule on Using 'Or' in Definitions and Descriptions
Author states the rule forbidding 'or' in definitions but permitting it in descriptions, with discussion of an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that 'or' is impermissible in a single definition (hadd) but permissible in a description (rasm), and explain why the definition of nazar as 'leading to knowledge or opinion' is actually two definitions.

Core Idea
A single definition cannot contain 'or'; a disjunctive definition is two definitions, not one.

This lesson examines a key rule from al-Sullam: the particle 'aw' (or) is forbidden in definitions (hudud) but allowed in descriptions (rusum). Al-Bajuri's commentary explains the reasoning and addresses an objection concerning the definition of nazar (reflection). Understanding this rule helps you evaluate whether a given definition is properly formed according to the standards of Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule from the matn
Read the line from al-Sullam: 'It is not permissible in definitions to mention 'or', and it is permissible in description.' Mark the distinction between hadd and rasm.
2
Classify the type of 'or'
Al-Bajuri clarifies that the 'or' in question is either for division (taqsim) or choice (takhyir). Understand that these disjoin parts, violating the unity required in a single definition.
3
Examine the objection against the definition of nazar
Some object that 'nazar is reflection that leads to knowledge or opinion' cannot be a hadd because ta'diya (leading) is external to the essence, and 'or' disjoins. Trace the objection in the passage.
4
Analyze al-Bajuri's response
Al-Bajuri argues that even if the definition is problematic, it is actually two definitions (one for knowledge, one for opinion). Therefore, the prevention of 'or' applies only to a single definition. Understand why this preserves the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز في الحدود ذكر أو وجائز في الرسم فادر ما رووا. قال الباجوري: قوله: (ذكر أو) أي التي للتقسيم أو التخيير، على ما مر. قوله: (فادر ما رووا) أي فاعلم الذي رووه من عدم الجواز في الأول والجواز في الثاني. وقد يمنع كون ذلك التعريف حدا؛ لأن التأدية إلى علم أو ظن أمر خارج عن حقيقة النظر. وعلى تسليم ذلك فهو في الحقيقة حدان. والمنع إنما هو في الحد الواحد.
And it is not permissible in definitions to mention 'or', and it is permissible in description, so know what they narrated. Al-Bajuri said: His statement: 'mention 'or'' meaning that which is for division or choice, as has passed. His statement: 'so know what they narrated' meaning: know what they narrated of impermissibility in the first and permissibility in the second. And it might be prevented that that definition is a definition, because leading to knowledge or opinion is something external to the reality of reflection. And if that is granted, it is in reality two definitions. And the prevention is only concerning a single definition.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line from al-Sullam that states a rule about the use of the particle 'aw' (or) in logical definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum). The matn says: 'It is not permissible in definitions to mention 'or', and it is permissible in description, so know what they narrated.' Al-Bajuri first clarifies that the 'aw' here is either the 'aw' of division (taqsim) or the 'aw' of choice (takhyir), as explained earlier. Then he explains the phrase 'so know what they narrated': the student must learn that the scholars have narrated (i.e., transmitted) the rule that 'aw' is disallowed in definitions but allowed in descriptions. The passage then presents an objection: someone might argue that the definition of 'nazar' (theoretical reflection) as 'that which leads to knowledge or opinion' should be prevented from being a definition, because 'leading to knowledge or opinion' includes the disjunctive 'or', and the phrase 'lead to' (ta'diya) is something external to the essence of reflection. Al-Bajuri responds: even if we grant that objection, that 'definition' is actually two definitions in reality, because it covers two distinct types (one leading to knowledge, one to opinion). Thus, the prevention only applies to a single definition that uses 'or', not to a pair of definitions masquerading as one. The rule is thereby preserved.

Rule Against 'Or' in Definitions
Bajuri explains the prohibition of 'or' in definitions and its allowance in descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the particle 'or' (أو) is prohibited in essential definitions (hudud) but permissible in descriptive definitions (rusum), and apply this rule to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A definition must be one essence; 'or' shatters its unity.

In this passage, al-Bajuri and his commentator lay down a crucial rule: you cannot use 'or' when giving the real definition (hadd) of something, but you may use it in a description (rasm). This distinction is not arbitrary—it follows from the nature of definition itself. A definition must capture the single, unified essence of a thing; 'or' introduces multiplicity, implying the thing could be defined in alternative ways, which would negate the definition's purpose. Descriptions, which list accidental properties, do not require such unity, so 'or' is allowed there. Let's work through the logic behind this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the text's ruling
Read the matn: 'ولا يجوز في الحدود ذكر أو وجائز في الرسم فادر ما رووا'. Note that it gives two distinct rulings: prohibition in definitions (hudud) and permissibility in descriptions (rusum).
The particle 'or' here is specifically for division (taqsim) or choice (takhyir), not for doubt.
2
Understand the commentator's expansion
Bajuri specifies that the 'or' in question is 'التي للتقسيم أو التخيير' (that which is for division or choice). He then interprets the command 'فادر ما رووا' as an instruction to know the transmitted ruling: impermissible in the first (definitions) and permissible in the second (descriptions).
This expansion clarifies the scope and confirms the transmitted tradition.
3
Grasp the logical rationale
A definition (hadd) must indicate the essence (mahiyya) of the definiendum as a single composite. If you say 'man is a rational animal or a laughing animal', the definition becomes ambiguous—which one is the real essence? Thus 'or' destroys the definition's unicity. A description (rasm), by contrast, lists accidents or proper attributes; it doesn't claim to capture the essence, so using 'or' to list alternatives is acceptable.
This rationale is not explicit in the passage but is the underlying logical principle.
4
Apply the rule to an Islamic science example
Consider defining 'salah' (prayer) in fiqh: a proper definition must be one unified expression. For instance, 'Salah is a set of specific actions and words beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'—no 'or' allowed. But a description could say 'Salah is an act of worship that includes standing, bowing, prostrating, or sitting'—here 'or' is permissible because it lists alternatives.
This shows the practical impact of the rule in Islamic jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعرفات (٤٩) وَلَا يَجُوزُ فِي الحُدُودِ ذِكْرُ «أَوْ وَجَائِزٌ فِي الرَّسْمِ فَادْرِ مَا رَوَوْا) الباجوري قَوْلُهُ : ( ذِكْرُ أَوْ أَي : الَّتِي لِلتَّقْسِيمِ أَوِ التَّخْيِيرِ، عَلَى مَا مَرَّ. «أَوْ») قَوْلُهُ : (فَادْرِ مَا رَوَوْا) أَيْ: فَاعْلَمِ الَّذِي رَوَوْهُ مِنْ عَدَمِ الجَوَازِ فِي الأَوَّلِ، وَالجَوَازِ فِي الثَّانِي .
Section on definitions: 'And it is not permissible in definitions to mention 'or', but it is permissible in descriptions, so understand what they have narrated.' Al-Bajuri: His statement: 'mentioning 'or' means: that which is for division or choice, as has passed. ... His statement: 'so understand what they have narrated' means: know what they narrated of the impermissibility in the first, and the permissibility in the second.'
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling on the use of the particle 'or' (أو) in definitions (حدود) versus descriptions (رسوم). The matn states unequivocally that 'or' is impermissible in definitions but permissible in descriptions. Bajuri expands this by specifying that the 'or' in question is that which indicates division (تقسيم) or choice (تخيير), as previously discussed. He then explains the command 'fa-dri ma rawaw' (so understand what they narrated) as an instruction to know the transmitted ruling: impermissibility in definitions and permissibility in descriptions. The reason for impermissibility in definitions is that a definition (hadd) must be a single composite that indicates the essence; introducing 'or' would imply that the definition has multiple alternatives, thus violating its unicity. In descriptions (rasm), which are not required to be singly essential, 'or' can be used to list accidental features.

Key Takeaway

Anbabi's Critique of 'Or' in Descriptions
Anbabi refutes a justification for 'or' in descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Anbabi's objection to the inclusion of 'or' in descriptions and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
Allowing 'or' in a description necessarily introduces multiplicity, defeating its unity.

In the ongoing debate about whether the word 'or' (أو) can appear in definitions and descriptions, Anbabi delivers a sharp critical refinement. He targets a previous justification that attempted to allow 'or' in descriptions (rasm) while prohibiting it in definitions (hadd). Anbabi argues that the reasoning is inconsistent: the same multiplicity that rules out 'or' in definitions also rules it out in descriptions. Understanding his objection sharpens your ability to assess the coherence of logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target justification
Anbabi is responding to an earlier claim that 'or' is permissible in descriptions because descriptions are less strict than definitions. Find that claim in the preceding commentary.
2
Extract Anbabi's core premise
He states that including 'or' in a description entails multiplicity (ta'addud) in reality and in essence (fi nafs al-amr). This multiplicity contradicts the assumption that the description is one unified expression (wahdatuhu).
The word 'or' presents alternatives, each describing a different possible entity, thus splitting the description.
3
Apply the same logic to definitions
Realise that Anbabi is applying the identical reasoning used to exclude 'or' from definitions: if 'or' causes multiplicity in descriptions, it must also be invalid there. Thus the special justification for descriptions collapses.
The principle is: any defining expression must be univocal.
4
Assess the logical validity
Check if Anbabi's argument holds: does 'or' always produce multiplicity? In a classical logical context, yes—'or' indicates at least one alternative, so the expression is not a single fixed description. His reasoning is internally sound.
Islamic logicians insisted that definitions must be unambiguous (mani' and jami').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأنبابي فيه لا معنى له؛ لأنَّه لا يمكن ولا يعقل دخولها فيه؛ لأنَّه يلزم من دخولها فيه تعدده في الحقيقة ونفس الأمر، فينافي فرض وحدته في ذلك، فبطل التّمسك بهذا الجواب . اهـ (صبَّان (۱) .
Al-Anbabi: There is no meaning to it; because it is not possible nor reasonable for it to enter into it; because from its entry into it, its multiplicity in reality and in itself is necessitated, so it contradicts the assumption of its unity in that, so the adherence to this answer is invalid. End. (Sabban)
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) by al-Anbabi. He is objecting to a previous justification (likely the one given by Bajuri or another commentator) that allowed 'or' in descriptions. Anbabi argues that this justification is meaningless because even in descriptions, including 'or' entails a multiplicity (ta'addud) in reality and in the essence of the description. Since the description is assumed to be one thing (a single composite), the entry of 'or' would contradict that assumption. Therefore, the argument that 'or' is permissible in descriptions is invalid. This shows a stricter view that 'or' cannot appear in any defining expression, whether definition or description.

Key Takeaway
Anbabi's critique shows that any use of 'or' in a definition or description violates the requirement of unity, so the same prohibition applies to both.

Definition and Division of the Quantifier (Sur)
Al-Bajuri defines the quantifier and explains its division into universal and particular with four moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the quantifier (sur) as used in Islamic logic and identify its four divisions (universal positive, universal negative, particular positive, particular negative) in both categorical and conditional propositions.

Core Idea
The quantifier indicates encompassment (ihata) of all or some individuals/states—like a city wall surrounding a city.

In this lesson, we explore al-Bajuri's commentary on the definition of the quantifier (sur). The quantifier is a key logical concept that specifies the scope of a proposition. Understanding its definition and division is essential for evaluating syllogisms in Islamic logic, especially when analyzing arguments from the Quran, Hadith, and juristic rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the word 'al-sur' in the Arabic passage. Al-Bajuri defines it as 'what indicates encompassment (al-ihata)'. Note that the term is a technical term in logic, metaphorically derived from the city wall (sur al-balad).
2
Extract the genus and scope
The genus is 'what indicates encompassment'. In categorical propositions (hamliyya), it encompasses all or some individuals (afrad). In conditional propositions (shartiyya), it encompasses all or some possible states (awda').
Examples: 'kull' (every) for universal categorical, 'ba'd' (some) for particular categorical; 'kullama' (whenever) for universal conditional, 'qad yakun' (sometimes) for particular conditional.
3
Recognize the division into universal and particular
The quantifier is divided into universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i). Each of these can be positive (ijabi) or negative (salbi), resulting in four types.
Universal positive: 'Every human is mortal'; Universal negative: 'No human is immortal'; Particular positive: 'Some human is wise'; Particular negative: 'Some human is not just'.
4
Understand the metaphorical basis
The term 'sur' literally means a city wall that encloses the entire city or a part of it. Similarly, the quantifier encloses (encompasses) the subject domain wholly or partially. This is a metaphor from language, but a technical term in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالأَوَّلُ) أَي : الَّذِي هُوَ الكُلِّيَّةُ بِالمَعْنَى الَّذِي أَرَادَهُ المُصَنِّفُ مِنْهَا فِيمَا تَقَدَّمَ، وَلَمْ يَقُلْ : وَالأُوْلَى)؛ نَظَراً لِكَوْنِهَا قِسْماً، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ فِي نَظِيرِهِ وَقَوْلُهُ : (إِمَّا مُسَوَّرٌ) أَيْ : بِالسُّورِ الكُلِّي أَوِ الجُزْئِيِّ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَإِمَّا مُهْمَلُ) أَيْ : مِنَ السُّوْرِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَالسُّورُ ... إلخ) هُوَ : مَا دَلَّ عَلَى الإِحَاطَةِ بِجَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ، أَوْ بِبَعْضِهَا فِي الحَمْلِيَّةِ»؛ كَـ: «كُلّ» وَ: «بَعْضِ»، كَمَا سَيَذْكُرُهُ المُصَنِّفُ . - وَ : مَا دَلَّ عَلَى الإِحَاطَةِ بِجَمِيعِ الأَوْضَاعِ - أي : الأَحْوَالِ المُمْكِنَةِ ، أَوْ بِبَعْضِهَا فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ ; كَـ: كُلَّمَا وَ : «قَدْ يَكُونُ (۳) ، كَمَا سَيَأْتي . سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ؛ تَشْبِيهاً لَهُ بِسُورِ البَلَدِ المُحِيطِ بِكُلِّهَا أَوْ بَعْضِهَا؛ بِجَامِعِ الإِحاطَةِ، فَهُوَ اسْتِعَارَةٌ بِاعْتِبَارِ اللُّغَةِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ حَقِيقَةٌ بِاعْتِبَارِ اصْطَلَاحِ المَنَاطِقَةِ. قَوْلُهُ : (كُليًّا) وَجُزْئِيًّا وَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا: إِمَّا إِيجَابِيٌّ ، وَإِمَّا سَلْبِيٌّ ; فَأَقْسَامُهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ، كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ المُصَنِّفُ بَعْدُ . الأنبابي قوله : (مَا دَلَّ عَلَى الإِحَاطَةِ) أي سواء كان لفظاً ; نحو: «كل» و «بعض»، أو لا كـ: كون النكرة في سياق النفي، والإضافة (٤) التي دلّت قرينة على عمومها أو عدمه [ج/ ۲۰] . (1) الشرشيمي: قوله : (مُعْتَبَرَةٌ فِي العُلُومِ) أي : تقع في الأقيسة؛ بأن تكون جزءاً من القياس . (۲) الشرشيمي: قوله : (لَا عَلَى مَا قِيلَ ... إلخ) هذا مردودٌ بأدلَّةٍ تُعلم مِنَ المطولات، كما ذكره الصَّبَّان. (۳) الشرشيمي: قوله : (وَ): «قَدْ يَكُونُ ) أي : في المنفصل ; كقولك : «قَدْ يَكُونُ الشَّيءُ : إِمَّا أَبْيَضُ، أَوْ أَسْوَدُ . (٤) في (ج) : وكالإضافة، بدلاً من «والإضافة).
His saying: (And the first) i.e., that which is the universal in the sense intended by the author from it previously, and he did not say 'the first (feminine)' because it is a division, as preceded in its counterpart. And his saying: (Either quantified) i.e., with the universal or particular quantifier. And his saying: (Or unquantified) i.e., from the quantifier. His saying: (The quantifier... etc.) is: what indicates encompassment of all individuals or some of them in the categorical [proposition], like 'every' and 'some', as the author will mention. And: what indicates encompassment of all conditions – i.e., possible states – or some of them in the conditional [proposition], like 'whenever' and 'sometimes', as will come. It is named thus by analogy to the wall of a city that surrounds all of it or part of it, due to the commonality of encompassment, so it is a metaphor from the linguistic perspective, although it is a technical term in the convention of logicians. His saying: (Universal and particular) and each of them is either positive or negative; so its divisions are four, as the author will mention later. Al-Ansari: his saying: (What indicates encompassment) i.e., whether it is a word like 'every' and 'some', or not, like an indefinite noun in the context of negation, or a possessive construction that a contextual clue indicates its generality or not. Al-Shirshimi: his saying: (Considered in the sciences) i.e., they occur in syllogisms, as being a part of the syllogism. Al-Shirshimi: his saying: (Not on what is said ... etc.) this is refuted by proofs learned from the extended works, as al-Sabban mentioned. Al-Shirshimi: his saying: ('and sometimes') i.e., in the disjunctive, like your saying: 'The thing is sometimes either white or black.' Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on the quantifier (sur). Al-Bajuri comments on the author's text. First, he explains why the author used the masculine 'al-awwal' instead of feminine: because 'kulliyya' (universal) is considered a division. He then clarifies that 'either quantified' means with a universal or particular quantifier, and 'unquantified' means without a quantifier. He defines the quantifier as that which indicates encompassment of all or some individuals in categorical propositions (e.g., 'every' and 'some'), and in conditional propositions it indicates encompassment of all or some possible states (e.g., 'whenever' and 'sometimes'). The term 'sur' is metaphorical: it is likened to a city wall that surrounds all or part of the city, based on the common meaning of encompassment. In ordinary language it is a metaphor, but in logical terminology it is a technical term. Al-Bajuri then notes that the quantifier is divided into universal and particular, and each can be positive or negative, resulting in four divisions (universal positive, universal negative, particular positive, particular negative), as the author will later detail. The marginal notes add: al-Ansari explains that the quantifier can be a word like 'every' or a non-word like an indefinite in negation context or an idafa with contextual clue. Al-Shirshimi's notes clarify the earlier remark about the proposition types being considered in the sciences (i.e., used in syllogisms) and that the opposing view is refuted.

Key Terms
السُّوْر
الإِحَاطَة
الحَمْلِيَّة
الشَّرْطِيَّة
الأَوْضَاع
كُلِّي
جُزْئِي

Checkpoint

D) Eight: including both categorical and conditional

Key Takeaway
The quantifier is a logical tool that indicates the scope of a proposition; its correct identification and classification into universal/particular and positive/negative is foundational for sound reasoning in Islamic sciences.
