The Definition of Logical Contradiction
Al-Bajuri defines contradiction as opposition of two propositions in quality with one necessarily true.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of contradiction and determine whether any two propositions satisfy the conditions for contradiction.

Core Idea
Contradiction is not just any difference—it requires the two propositions to differ specifically in quality.

In this lesson, we dissect the core definition of tanāquḍ (contradiction) as presented by al-Bajuri in his commentary on al-Sullam's poem. The definition is concise: 'khulf al-qadiyyatayn fī kayf wa ṣidq wāḥid amr qufī.' We will examine each component: the genus (khulf), the differentia (fī kayf), and the necessary truth condition. Understanding this precise definition is essential for distinguishing genuine contradictions from other types of opposition in logical and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum (the term being defined) in the matn. It is 'tanāquḍ' (contradiction). Note that the matn presents it as a subject (mubtada') for further elaboration.
The definiendum is explicitly stated at the beginning of the verse.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The definition is 'khulf al-qadiyyatayn fī kayf'. The genus is 'khulf' (opposition/difference), which encompasses all types of differences. The differentia is 'fī kayf' (in quality), which restricts the opposition specifically to the quality (affirmative/negative) of the propositions.
The genus is further specified by being added to 'al-qadiyyatayn' to exclude opposition in non-propositional expressions.
3
Analyze the exclusions in the genus
By adding 'al-qadiyyatayn', the definition excludes opposition in other types of expressions: command sentences ('Stand! Do not stand!'), relational compounds ('Zayd's servant, not Zayd's servant'), and singular terms ('Zayd, not Zayd'). Understand that logicians primarily focus on propositions, but some also apply 'tanāquḍ' to singulars.
The specification of 'propositions' is because they are the primary objects of logical inquiry, not to rule out singulars definitively.
4
Understand the differentia 'fī kayf'
The phrase 'fī kayf' restricts the opposition to quality alone. This excludes differences in subject, predicate, or other aspects. For example, 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is standing' differ in subject, not quality, so they are not contradictory. The only way to achieve contradiction is to have one proposition affirmative and the other negative, with identical subject and predicate (and other conditions like time, place, etc.).
Al-Bajuri gives examples: different subject, different predicate, and a third example showing the necessity of difference in quality.
5
Consider the truth condition
The definition also includes 'wa ṣidq wāḥid amr qufī' (and the truth of one is a necessitated matter). This means that in genuine contradiction, one proposition must be true and the other false; they cannot both be true or both false. This condition is a consequence of the definition, ensuring that the opposition is exhaustive.
This part of the matn is not fully elaborated in the provided window, but it is integral to the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَنَاقُضٌ : خُلْفُ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ فِي كَيْفِ، وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ أَمْرٌ قُفِي
قوله: (فصل في التناقض) أي: في تعريفه وأحكامه، وقد أشار للأول بالبيت الأول، وللثاني بما بعده.
ومعنى التناقض لغة: إثبات الشيء ورفعه، واصطلاحاً ما ذكره المصنف.
قوله: (تناقض) مبتدأ، والمسوّع: إرادة الجنس، أو وقوعه في معرض التفصيل الآتي.
وقوله: (خلف القضيتين... إلخ) الخلف: اسم مصدر بمعنى الاختلاف، وهو جنس دخل فيه جميع الاختلافات.
وخرج بإضافته إلى القضيتين: خلف غيرهما: من المركبات الإنشائية؛ كـ: قم، لا تقم.
أو المركبات الإضافية؛ كـ: غلام زيد، لا غلام زيد.
والمفردات؛ كـ: زيد، لا زيد.
ومقتضى ذلك: أن اختلاف المفردات لا يسمى تناقضاً في اصطلاح المناطقة، وهو ما صرح به الملوي في كبيريه، لكن في كلام بعضهم ما يفيد أنه يسمى بذلك في اصطلاحهم، وعليه فتَخْصيص المصنف القضيتين بالذكر؛ لكون القضايا هي المقصودة لهم بالأصالة، لا للاحتراز عن المفردين.
وخرج بقوله: في كيف خلف القضيتين في غيره من موضوع، أو محمول، أو عدول، أو تحصيل، أو غير ذلك:
فالأول كما في قولك: زيد قائم، عمرو قائم.
والثاني كما في قولك: زيد قائم، زيد كاتب.
والثالث كما في قولك: زيد هو قائم، زيد هو لا قائم.
Contradiction: opposition of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is a necessitated matter. His saying: (Chapter on Contradiction) i.e., concerning its definition and its rulings, and he alluded to the first by the first verse, and to the second by what follows it. The meaning of contradiction in language: affirming a thing and negating it; and in terminology: what the author mentioned. His saying: (Contradiction) is a subject, and the justification (masawwa') is the intention of the genus, or its occurrence in the context of the coming detail. As the author mentioned. His saying: (opposition of the two propositions... etc.) Opposition (khulf) is a verbal noun meaning difference, and it is a genus that includes all differences. By adding it to the two propositions, it excludes opposition of other than them: from compositional compounds (murakkabat insha'iyya) like: 'Stand! Do not stand!'; or relational compounds (murakkabat idafiyya) like: 'Zayd's servant, not Zayd's servant'; and singulars like: 'Zayd, not Zayd.' Consequently, difference in singulars is not called contradiction in the terminology of the logicians, and that is what al-Mallawi stated in his two major works, but in the words of some there is something indicating that it is called by that in their terminology, and accordingly, the author's specification of the two propositions is due to the fact that propositions are their primary intended objects, not to exclude the two singulars. And through his saying: 'in quality' it excludes opposition of the two propositions in other than it, such as subject, predicate, modification (adl), determination (tahsil), or other than that. So the first is like your saying: 'Zayd is standing, 'Amr is standing'; the second: 'Zayd is standing, Zayd is writing'; the third: 'Zayd is standing, Zayd is not standing'.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is the definition of contradiction (tanāquḍ). The matn verse by al-Sullam states: 'Contradiction is opposition of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is a required matter.' Al-Bajuri then interprets the definition. First, he explains that the section deals with definition and rulings. The linguistic meaning of tanāquḍ is affirming and negating the same thing. The technical meaning is what the author gave. Then he parses the matn: 'تناقض' is the subject (mubtada') and the justification for it being indefinite is that it indicates a genus or because it is coming in the context of detail. The word 'خلف' (opposition) is a verbal noun meaning difference; it is the genus of the definition, covering all types of opposition. Adding it to 'القضيتين' (the two propositions) excludes opposition that occurs in non-propositional things: command sentences like 'Stand! Do not stand!', relational compounds like 'Zayd's servant, not Zayd's servant', and singulars like 'Zayd, not Zayd'. However, there is a debate: some logicians do not call difference in singulars 'contradiction' in their terminology; al-Mallawi is quoted as saying so. But others include it. The author's specifying 'propositions' is because they are the primary objects, not necessarily to exclude singulars. Next, the phrase 'في كيف' (in quality) restricts the opposition to quality (affirmative/negative), excluding differences in other aspects: subject (e.g., 'Zayd is standing, 'Amr is standing' — different subjects), predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is standing, Zayd is writing'), modification (adl) (e.g., 'Zayd is standing, Zayd is not standing' is actually a difference in quality; the example might be misordered or the term adl refers to something else). The key point is that for two propositions to be contradictory, they must differ in quality, not in any other aspect.

Key Terms
تناقض
خلف
كيف
قضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, what does the phrase 'fī kayf' exclude from being a contradiction?
A) Differences in subject
B) Differences in truth value
C) Differences in quantity
D) Differences in modality

Key Takeaway
Contradiction requires two propositions that oppose each other specifically in quality (affirmative vs. negative), with one necessarily true, and that all other aspects (subject, predicate, etc.) are identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Given the two propositions: 'All actions are created by Allah' and 'No actions are created by Allah', determine if they are contradictory according to the definition. Explain which components of the definition they satisfy or fail.
?
Task 2: In the context of Usul al-Fiqh, the statement 'The sacrifice is obligatory' and 'The sacrifice is not obligatory' are contradictory. Identify the genus and differentia in this example as per the definition of tanāquḍ.

Shakl vs Darb: The Two Dimensions of a Syllogism
Bajuri defines shakl and darb for syllogistic figures and moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shakl and darb in a categorical syllogism by identifying whether quantifiers are considered in the form.

Core Idea
Shakl is the skeleton; darb adds the flesh of quantity and quality.

This lesson unpacks a key definition from Bajuri’s commentary, where he clearly separates the concepts of figure (shakl) and mood (darb). Understanding this distinction is essential for analyzing syllogistic validity in Islamic logic, as it determines which combinations of premises yield necessary conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the basic structure of a categorical syllogism.
A syllogism has two premises (minor and major) that share a middle term. The form (hay'a) of these premises together is what Bajuri is defining.
2
Identify the two conditions for form: with or without quantifiers.
Quantifiers (aswār) include universal/particular and affirmative/negative. Shakl ignores these; darb includes them.
3
Apply to an example: Take the syllogism 'All A are B; all B are C; therefore all A are C'.
Its shakl is the first figure (middle term B is subject in major and predicate in minor). Its darb is AAA (both premises universal affirmative). Note that the same shakl could have different darbs.
Example from Kalam: 'All temporally originated things are contingent; the world is temporally originated; therefore the world is contingent.'
4
Verify the definition from the passage: darb requires noting the quantifiers; shakl does not.
Bajuri explicitly says that darb is 'with the condition of considering the quantifiers', while shakl is 'without that condition'.
The passage concludes with 'so do not neglect', stressing the importance of this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَعَلَى الثَّانِي، فَاسْمُ الإِشَارَةِ رَاجِعُ لِاعْتِبَارِ الأَسْوَارِ، وَالضَّمِيرُ عَائِدٌ لِلمَذْكُورِ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيِ القِيَاسِ، بِتَقْدِيرِ المُضَافِ السَّابِقِ، وَالمَعْنَى: وَقْتَ اعْتِبَارِ الأَسْوَارِ يُشَارُ لِهَيْئَةِ قَضِيَّتَيِ القِيَاسِ بِالضَّرْبِ. وَالمُرَادُ بِالإِشَارَةِ هُنَا: الدَّلَالَةُ، فَاللَّامُ فِي قَوْلِهِ: «لَهُ» بِمَعْنَى: عَلَى، كَمَا يُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ كَلَامِ الشَّيْخِ المَلْوِيِّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ. وَالحَاصِلُ: أَنَّ الضَّرْبَ اسْمٌ لِهَيْئَةِ قَضِيَّتَيِ القِيَاسِ الحَاصِلَةِ مِنِ اجْتِمَاعِ الصُّغْرَى مَعَ الكُبْرَى بِاعْتِبَارِ طَرَفَيِ المَطْلُوبِ مَعَ الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ بِشَرْطِ اعْتِبَارِ الأَسْوَارِ؛ كَأَنْ يُلَاحَظَ كَوْنُ هَاتَيْنِ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ، بِخِلَافِ الشَّكْلِ، فَإِنَّهُ اسْمٌ لِلْهَيْئَةِ المَذْكُورَةِ لَا بِهَذَا الشَّرْطِ، بَلْ بِشَرْطِ عَدَمِ اعْتِبَارِ الأَسْوَارِ، أَوْ بِلَا شَرْطٍ عَلَى مَا تَقَدَّمَ، فَلَا تَغْفَلْ.
And regarding the second, the demonstrative pronoun refers to the consideration of the quantifiers, and the pronoun refers to what was mentioned from the two propositions of the syllogism, with the estimation of the preceding addition. The meaning: at the time of considering the quantifiers, one points to the form of the two propositions of the syllogism as the mood. And what is meant by indication here is signification; so the lam in his statement 'lahu' is in the sense of 'ala, as is understood from the words of al-Mallawi in his major [commentary]. The conclusion: that the mood is a name for the form of the two propositions of the syllogism resulting from the combination of the minor with the major, considering the two terms of the sought conclusion along with the middle term, with the condition of considering the quantifiers; such that one notes that these two propositions are universal. Unlike the shape, for it is a name for the aforementioned form without this condition, but with the condition of not considering the quantifiers, or without any condition as previously, so do not neglect.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion by Bajuri, clarifying the technical distinction between the terms 'shakl' (figure) and 'darb' (mood) in categorical syllogisms. The author begins by explaining a preceding statement about the second [something omitted]. He specifies that the demonstrative 'that' refers to the consideration of the quantifiers (universal/particular, affirmative/negative). The pronoun in 'lahu' refers back to the two premises. The meaning is that when we consider the quantifiers, we denote the specific arrangement of the two premises as the 'darb' (mood). He then clarifies that 'indication' here means signification, and the lam in 'lahu' is equivalent to 'ala. The conclusion is that 'darb' is the name for the form of the two premises taking into account the subject and predicate of the conclusion along with the middle term, but with the condition of considering the quantifiers (e.g., noting both premises are universal). In contrast, 'shakl' is the name for the same form without that condition—either with the condition of not considering quantifiers, or absolutely without any condition, as earlier stated. The passage thus defines two distinct technical terms essential for analyzing syllogistic validity.

Key Terms
شكل (shakl)
ضرب (darb)
السوار (aswār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Shakl considers the middle term only, darb considers both terms.
B) Darb includes the consideration of quantifiers, while shakl does not.
C) Shakl is the Greek term, darb is the Arabic translation.
D) Darb applies only to compound syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
The figure (shakl) of a syllogism is determined by the arrangement of the middle term alone, while the mood (darb) also considers the quantity and quality of the premises.

Definition and Components of Analogy (Tamthil)
Author defines analogy and lists its four components with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four components of an analogy (tamthil) and explain how they correspond to the minor, major, and middle terms of a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Analogy links two particulars through a common attribute; its four pillars mirror the terms of a categorical syllogism.

In this passage from the commentary on al-Sullam, the author defines analogy (tamthil) and breaks it down into four essential components. You will learn to recognize each component and see how this logical structure corresponds to the parts of a categorical syllogism. Understanding this is the first step in mastering analogical reasoning, which is central to Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of analogy
Find the lines where analogy is defined. Notice it is 'applying a particular to a particular due to a commonality.'
2
List the four components
From the passage, extract the four pillars: mushabbah (compared thing), mushabbah bihi (thing compared to), hukm (ruling), jami' (commonality). Know their alternative names: minor term, original, major term, middle term.
The 'original' (asl) is the thing compared to, not the commonality.
3
Match components to syllogistic terms
Notice how the minor term (musabbah) corresponds to the subject of the conclusion, the major term (hukm) to the predicate, and the middle term (jami') to the common attribute linking them.
4
Apply the components to an example
Take the example 'Nabidh is forbidden like khamr due to intoxication' and label each component: mushabbah = nabidh, mushabbah bihi = khamr, hukm = forbidden, jami' = intoxication.
5
Compare with al-Taftazani's definition
Read the last part about Sa'd al-Din's definition and note how it aligns with the author's. Both agree that analogy is likening one particular to another in a shared meaning to establish a ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(113) وَحَيْثُ جُزْئِيٌّ عَلَى جُزْنِي حُمِلْ لِجَامِعِ فَذَاكَ: تَمْثِيْلٌ جُعِلْ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَحَيْثُ جُزْئِيٌّ عَلَى جُزْنِي حُمِلْ بِإِسْكَانِ «اليَاءِ» الثَّانِيَةِ لِلوَزْنِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (لِجَامِعِ) أَيْ: بَيْنَ المُشَبَّهِ وَالمُشَبَّهِ بِهِ، وَذَلِكَ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : النَّبِيذُ حَرَامٌ كَالخَمْرِ بِجَامِعِ الإِسْكَارِ، وَأَرْكَانُهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ : ١ - مُشَبَّهُ، وَيُسَمَّى حَدًّا أَصْغَرَ. ٢ - وَمُشَبِّهُ بِهِ، وَيُسَمَّى أَصْلاً. ٣ - وَحُكْمْ، وَيُسَمَّى حَدًّا أَكْبَرَ. ٤ - وَجَامِعٌ، وَيُسَمَّى حَدًّا أَوْسَطَ؛ كَذَا يُؤخَذُ مِنْ شَرْحِ المَلَّوِيِّ الصَّغِيرِ. وَفِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ أَنَّ هَذَا اصْطِلاحُ المَنَاطِقَةِ، لَكِنَّهُ لَمْ يَذْكُرْ أَنَّ المُشَبَّهَ بِهِ يُسَمَّى أَصْلاً فِي اصْطِلاحِهِمْ، فَلْيُرَاجَعْ. قَوْلُهُ : (فَذَاكَ : تَمْثِيلٌ جُعِلْ) اسْمُ الإِشَارَةِ عَائِدٌ لِلحَمْلِ المَفْهُومِ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ حُمِلْ، وَصَرِيحُ هَذَا أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ يُفَسِّرُ التَّمْثِيلَ بِحَمْلِ جُزْئِيٌّ عَلَى جُزْئِيٌّ فِي الحُكْمِ بِجَامِعٍ بَيْنَهُمَا، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ مُخَالِفٍ لِمَا ذَكَرَهُ السَّعْدُ مِنْ أَنَّهُ تَشْبِيهُ جُزْئِيِّ بِجُزْئِي فِي مَعْنَى مُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهُمَا لِيَثْبُتَ فِي المُشَبَّهِ الحُكْمُ الثَّابِتُ فِي المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ المُعَلَّلُ بِذَلِكَ المَعْنَى، فَلْيُتَأمَّلْ.
And when a particular is applied to a particular due to a commonality, that is called analogy. His statement: (And when a particular is applied to a particular) with the second ya' made silent for meter. His statement: (due to a commonality) i.e., between the compared thing and the thing compared to, such as your saying: Nabidh is forbidden like khamr due to the commonality of intoxication. Its pillars are four: 1. The compared thing, called the minor term. 2. The thing compared to, called the original. 3. The ruling, called the major term. 4. The commonality, called the middle term. Thus is taken from al-Mallawi's small commentary. And in his large commentary he said that this is the terminology of the logicians, but he did not mention that the thing compared to is called 'original' in their terminology, so it should be checked. His statement: (that is called analogy). The demonstrative pronoun refers to the application understood from his saying 'is applied'. The explicit meaning of this is that the author explains analogy as the application of a particular to another particular in ruling due to a commonality between them, and it is not different from what Sa'd mentioned: that it is likening a particular to a particular in a shared meaning so that the ruling established in the thing compared to, which is justified by that meaning, becomes established in the compared thing. So let it be considered.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage explains verse (113) of al-Sullam, which defines analogy (tamthil) as 'applying a particular to a particular due to a commonality'. The commentator clarifies the component parts: the compared thing (musabbaha) called the minor term (had asghar), the thing compared to (musabbaha bihi) called the original (asl), the ruling (hukm) called the major term (had akbar), and the commonality (jami') called the middle term (had awsat). An example is given: 'Nabidh is forbidden like khamr due to intoxication.' The commentator notes that in al-Mallawi's large commentary, the term 'asl' for the thing compared to is not explicitly mentioned in their terminology, so it should be verified. The passage then shows that the author's definition of analogy as 'applying a particular to a particular' agrees with Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani's definition: 'likening a particular to another in a shared meaning to establish the ruling'. This definition is foundational for understanding analogical reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence, where analogy (qiyas) is a primary source of law.

Key Terms
تمثيل
جامع
أصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the analogy 'Nabidh is forbidden like khamr due to intoxication', which component is the 'commonality' (jami')?
A) Nabidh
B) Khamr
C) Forbidden
D) Intoxication

Key Takeaway
Analogy (tamthil) is defined as applying a ruling from one particular to another due to a common attribute, with four components that correspond to the minor, major, and middle terms of a syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the analogy 'The sale of grapes for wine is invalid like the sale of khamr due to the commonality of ripening for intoxication', identify the four components and write them down.

Distinguishing Nabi and Rasul
Commentary defining nabi and rasul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between nabi and rasul using their defining characteristics as given in the commentary.

Core Idea
A rasul is a nabi with a mission to deliver the revelation.

In this passage, al-Bajuri clarifies a key theological distinction between 'nabi' and 'rasul'. While often used interchangeably in everyday speech, the classical scholars of usul al-fiqh and kalam differentiate them by the command to convey. Let's break down the definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms being defined
Locate the two terms 'rasul' and 'nabi' in the Arabic text. They are the definienda.
2
Extract the shared genus
Both are defined as a human (insaan) who receives revelation (yuha ilayhi) of a shari'a to act upon.
The genus is 'human', the relevant difference is the command to convey.
3
Identify the differentia
For rasul, the definition includes 'and he is commanded to convey it' (wa umira bi-tablighihi). For nabi, it explicitly says 'even if he is not commanded to convey it' (wa in lam yu'mar bi-tablighihi). This command is the differentia.
4
Note the relationship
Since rasul has an additional attribute, it is more specific (akhas) than nabi. This is the well-known view (mashhur).
There are other views, but focus on this one for now.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة (١٤٢ ) ثُمَّ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ سَرْمَدَا عَلَى رَسُولِ اللَّهِ خَيْرِ مَنْ هَدَى (١٤٣) وَآلِهِ وَصَحْبِهِ النِّقَاتِ السَّالكِيْنَ سُبُلَ النَّجَاةِ (١٤٤) مَا قَطَعَتْ شَمْسُ النَّهَارِ أَبْرُجَا وَطَلَعَ البَدْرُ المُنِيرُ فِي الدُّجَى
الباجوري
قَوْلُهُ : (ثُمَّ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ ... إلخ) «ثُمَّ» لِلتَّرْتِيبِ الذِّكْرِي .
وَقَوْلُهُ : (سَرْمَدَا) أَيْ : دَائِماً .
وَقَوْلُهُ : (عَلَى رَسُولِ اللَّهِ مِنَ المَعْلُومِ :
- أَنَّ الرَّسُولَ» أَخَصُّ مِنَ النَّبِيِّ»؛ لِأَنَّ مَعْنَى الرَّسُولِ : إِنْسَانُ أُوحِيَ إِلَيْهِ بِشَرْعِ يُعْمَلُ بِهِ وَأُمِرَ بِتَبْلِيغِهِ»، وَمَعْنَى النَّبِيِّ: «إِنْسَانُ أُوحِيَ إِلَيْهِ بِشَرْعِ يُعْمَلُ بِهِ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُؤمَرُ بِتَبْلِيغِهِ»؛ هَذَا هُوَ المَشْهُورُ .
- وَقِيلَ : إِنَّهُمَا مُتَرَادِفَانِ .
- وَقِيلَ غَيْرَ ذَلِكَ ، كَمَا أَوْضَحْنَاهُ فِي غَيْرِ هَذَا المَحَلِّ .
قَوْلُهُ : (خَيْرِ مَنْ هَدَى أَيْ : خَيْرِ مَنْ هَدَى النَّاسَ إِلَى اللهِ تَعَالَى، وَإِذَا كَانَ خَيْرٌ مَنْ ذُكِرَ، صلى الله عليه وسلم كَانَ خَيْرَ غَيْرِهِ بِالْأَوْلَى.
قَوْلُهُ : (الثَّقَاتِ) جَمْعُ: «ثِقَةٍ»؛ بِمَعْنَى : المَوْلُوقِ بِهِ.
وَقَوْلُهُ : (الصَّالِحِيْنَ سُبُلَ النَّجَاةِ أَي : المُتَتَّبِّعِينَ طُرُقَ النَّجَاةِ ؛ أَعْنِي : الأُمُورَ المُوَصِّلَةَ إِلَيْهَا كَـ: الأَمْرِ بِالمَعْرُوفِ وَالنَّهْيِ عَنِ المُنْكَرِ ، وَكَـ : الصَّلَاةِ وَالصَّوْمِ وَالزَّكَاةِ، .. إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ مِنْ سَائِرِ المَأمُورَاتِ وَالمَنْهِيَّاتِ .
وَفِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ بِالكِنَايَةِ، فَيَكُونُ المُصَنِّفُ قَدْ شَبَّهَ النَّجَاةَ بِشَيْءٍ لَهُ سُبُل حِرِّيَّةٌ، وَطَوَى لَفْظَ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ، وَرَمَزَ إِلَيْهِ بِشَيْءٍ مِنْ لَوَازِمِهِ وَهُوَ السُّبُلُ . وَيَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ فِيهِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ تَصْرِيحِيَّةٌ، فَيَكُونُ قَدْ شَبَّهَ طُرُقَ النَّجَاةِ الَّتِي هِيَ الأُمُورُ المَذْكُورَةُ بِالسُّبُلِ الحِرِّيَّةِ ، وَاسْتَعَارَ لَفْظَ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ لِلمُشَبَّهِ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ فَالسُّلُوكُ تَرْشِيحٌ. قَوْلُهُ : (مَا قَطَعَتْ شَمْسُ النَّهَارِ ... إلخ) أَيْ : مُدَّةُ قَطْعِ شَمْسِ النَّهَارِ ... إلخ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَطَلَعَ البَدْرُ ... إلخ) أَيْ : وَمُدَّهُ طُلُوعِ البَدْرِ ... إلخ، فَ «مَا » مَصْدَرِيَّةٌ ظَرْفِيَّةٌ وَالغَرَضُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ : التَّعْمِيمُ فِي جَمِيعِ الأَوْقَاتِ عَلَى طَرِيقِ الكِنَايَةِ، كَمَا هُوَ عَادَةُ العَرَبِ.
Conclusion (142) Then blessings and peace be eternal upon the Messenger of Allah, the best of those who guided (143) and upon his family and his companions, the trustworthy, those who follow the paths of salvation (144) as long as the sun of daytime cuts through its towers and the bright full moon rises in the darkness. al-Bajuri: His saying: (Then blessings and peace... etc.) 'Then' is for sequential mention. His saying: (eternal) meaning perpetually. His saying: (upon the Messenger of Allah) It is known that 'messenger' (rasul) is more specific than 'prophet' (nabi); because the meaning of messenger is: a human to whom a shari'a is revealed to be acted upon and he is commanded to convey it; and the meaning of prophet is: a human to whom a shari'a is revealed to be acted upon even if he is not commanded to convey it. This is the well-known view. And it is said that they are synonymous. And other views exist, as we have explained elsewhere. His saying: (the best of those who guided) meaning the best of those who guided people to Allah Almighty, and if he is the best of those mentioned, peace be upon him, then he is even more so the best of others. His saying: (the trustworthy) plural of 'thiqa' meaning one trusted. His saying: (those who follow the paths of salvation) meaning those who pursue the ways of salvation, I mean: the things that lead to it, such as commanding good and forbidding evil, and such as prayer, fasting, zakat, and so forth from the rest of the commanded and forbidden acts. And in the author's speech there is a metaphor by way of implication (isti'ara bil-kinaaya). Thus the author likened salvation to something having real paths, and omitted the word of the likened-to, and alluded to it by something from its concomitants, namely the paths. It is also possible that there is an explicit metaphor, so that he likened the ways of salvation, which are the mentioned things, to real paths, and borrowed the word of the likened-to for the likened. In either case, 'following' is a reinforcement (tarshih). His saying: (as long as the sun of daytime cuts through its towers) meaning the duration of the sun cutting through its towers, etc. His saying: (and the bright full moon rises in the darkness) meaning the duration of the moon rising, etc. So 'ma' is a subjunctive particle of time, and the purpose of that is generalization over all times by way of allusion, as is the custom of the Arabs.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the closing lines of the matn. The commentator, al-Bajuri, begins by explaining the word 'thumma' as indicating sequence in mention, not time. Then he explains 'sarmada' as eternal. The main logical point is the definition of 'rasul' vs. 'nabi'. He states that 'rasul' is more specific (akhas) than 'nabi' because a messenger is commanded to convey the revealed shari'a, whereas a prophet may not be. This is the well-known (mashhur) view. He then adds that some say they are synonymous, and there are other opinions. He then explains 'khayr man hada' as the best guide. Next, he defines 'thiqa' (trustworthy) and explains 'subul al-najah' (paths of salvation) as the various acts of worship and enjoining good and forbidding evil. He analyzes the author's use of metaphor: either an implicit metaphor (kinaaya) where salvation is compared to something with paths, or an explicit metaphor (tasrihiyya) where the paths themselves are compared to real paths. Finally, he explains 'ma qata'at...' as a time-madh expression indicating perpetuity. The entire commentary is a good example of how scholars unpack poetic lines with technical precision.

نبي
أخص
مشهور

Unpacking the Metaphor of Paths of Salvation
Commentator explains the metaphor in the author's closing lines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an implicit metaphor (isti'ara bil-kinaaya) in an Arabic text and explain its components.

Core Idea
A metaphor can be implied by mentioning a concomitant of the likened-to rather than the term itself.

In the closing du'a, the author refers to 'subul al-najah' (paths of salvation). Al-Bajuri analyzes this as a metaphor, showing how a classical scholar unpacks figurative language. Let's walk through the analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal phrase
The phrase 'subul al-najah' literally means 'paths of salvation'. Recognize that it likely has a figurative sense.
2
Recognize the comparison
Salvation is being compared to a destination with paths. The likened-to (salvation) is not directly named as a destination, but its concomitant (paths) is mentioned.
3
Classify the metaphor
According to al-Bajuri, it is an implicit metaphor (isti'ara bil-kinaaya) because the word for the likened-to is omitted and only a concomitant is alluded to.
Alternatively, it could be explicit if the paths of salvation (deeds) are compared to real paths.
4
Identify the reinforcement
The word 'following' (suluk) is a reinforcement (tarshih) that extends the metaphor by referring to the act of traversing the paths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the closing lines of the matn. The commentator, al-Bajuri, begins by explaining the word 'thumma' as indicating sequence in mention, not time. Then he explains 'sarmada' as eternal. The main logical point is the definition of 'rasul' vs. 'nabi'. He states that 'rasul' is more specific (akhas) than 'nabi' because a messenger is commanded to convey the revealed shari'a, whereas a prophet may not be. This is the well-known (mashhur) view. He then adds that some say they are synonymous, and there are other opinions. He then explains 'khayr man hada' as the best guide. Next, he defines 'thiqa' (trustworthy) and explains 'subul al-najah' (paths of salvation) as the various acts of worship and enjoining good and forbidding evil. He analyzes the author's use of metaphor: either an implicit metaphor (kinaaya) where salvation is compared to something with paths, or an explicit metaphor (tasrihiyya) where the paths themselves are compared to real paths. Finally, he explains 'ma qata'at...' as a time-madh expression indicating perpetuity. The entire commentary is a good example of how scholars unpack poetic lines with technical precision.

Key Terms
استعارة بالكناية
استعارة تصريحية
ترشيح
لازم
مشبه به
مشبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the metaphor analyzed by al-Bajuri, what is the likened-to (al-mushabbah) that is omitted?
A) The paths themselves
B) Salvation (al-najah)
C) The author's du'a
D) The full moon

Key Takeaway
An implicit metaphor (isti'ara bil-kinaaya) works by alluding to the likened-to through one of its necessary concomitants, as seen in 'subul al-najah'.

Distinguishing Rasul from Nabi
Author defines Rasul and Nabi contrastively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a Rasul and a Nabi based on the well-known definition, identifying the differentia.

Core Idea
A Rasul must convey the law; a Nabi may not—making 'Rasul' more specific.

In Islamic theology, the terms 'Rasul' and 'Nabi' are often used but have distinct technical meanings. This passage from the commentary on al-Sullam clarifies the well-known definition, setting the differentia as the command to convey the revelation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly identifying the roles of prophets in the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Extract the genus
Both Rasul and Nabi are defined as 'insan' (human). This is the common genus that applies to both.
The genus is the broader category they share.
2
Identify the differentia for Rasul
The differentia for Rasul is that he is commanded to convey (amara bi tablighihi). This distinguishes him from a mere recipient of revelation.
This command is the key element.
3
Identify the differentia for Nabi
The differentia for Nabi is the absence of the command to convey (wa in lam yu'mar bi tablighihi). A Nabi receives a law but may not be obligated to propagate it.
The absence of the command is the distinguishing feature.
4
Compare the definitions
Notice that the definitions share the first part (revelation of a law to be acted upon) but differ in the command to convey. Therefore, every Rasul is a Nabi, but not every Nabi is a Rasul.
This logical relationship is important in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (ثُمَّ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ ... إلخ) «ثُمَّ» لِلتَّرْتِيبِ الذِّكْرِي . وَقَوْلُهُ : (سَرْمَدَا) أَيْ : دَائِماً . وَقَوْلُهُ : (عَلَى رَسُولِ اللَّهِ مِنَ المَعْلُومِ : - أَنَّ الرَّسُولَ» أَخَصُّ مِنَ النَّبِيِّ»؛ لِأَنَّ مَعْنَى الرَّسُولِ : إِنْسَانُ أُوحِيَ إِلَيْهِ بِشَرْعِ يُعْمَلُ بِهِ وَأُمِرَ بِتَبْلِيغِهِ»، وَمَعْنَى النَّبِيِّ: «إِنْسَانُ أُوحِيَ إِلَيْهِ بِشَرْعِ يُعْمَلُ بِهِ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُؤمَرُ بِتَبْلِيغِهِ»؛ هَذَا هُوَ المَشْهُورُ . - وَقِيلَ : إِنَّهُمَا مُتَرَادِفَانِ . - وَقِيلَ غَيْرَ ذَلِكَ ، كَمَا أَوْضَحْنَاهُ فِي غَيْرِ هَذَا المَحَلِّ . قَوْلُهُ : (خَيْرِ مَنْ هَدَى أَيْ : خَيْرِ مَنْ هَدَى النَّاسَ إِلَى اللهِ تَعَالَى، وَإِذَا كَانَ خَيْرٌ مَنْ ذُكِرَ، صلى الله عليه وسلم كَانَ خَيْرَ غَيْرِهِ بِالْأَوْلَى. قَوْلُهُ : (الثَّقَاتِ) جَمْعُ: «ثِقَةٍ»؛ بِمَعْنَى : المَوْلُوقِ بِهِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (الصَّالِحِيْنَ سُبُلَ النَّجَاةِ أَي : المُتَتَّبِّعِينَ طُرُقَ النَّجَاةِ ؛ أَعْنِي : الأُمُورَ المُوَصِّلَةَ إِلَيْهَا كَـ: الأَمْرِ بِالمَعْرُوفِ وَالنَّهْيِ عَنِ المُنْكَرِ ، وَكَـ : الصَّلَاةِ وَالصَّوْمِ وَالزَّكَاةِ، .. إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ مِنْ سَائِرِ المَأمُورَاتِ وَالمَنْهِيَّاتِ . وَفِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ بِالكِنَايَةِ، فَيَكُونُ المُصَنِّفُ قَدْ شَبَّهَ النَّجَاةَ بِشَيْءٍ لَهُ سُبُل حِرِّيَّةٌ، وَطَوَى لَفْظَ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ، وَرَمَزَ إِلَيْهِ بِشَيْءٍ مِنْ لَوَازِمِهِ وَهُوَ السُّبُلُ . وَيَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ فِيهِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ تَصْرِيحِيَّةٌ، فَيَكُونُ قَدْ شَبَّهَ طُرُقَ النَّجَاةِ الَّتِي هِيَ الأُمُورُ المَذْكُورَةُ بِالسُّبُلِ الحِرِّيَّةِ ، وَاسْتَعَارَ لَفْظَ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ لِلمُشَبَّهِ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ فَالسُّلُوكُ تَرْشِيحٌ.
His saying: 'Then prayers and peace... etc.' - 'thumma' is for sequential mention. His saying: 'perpetually' meaning always. His saying: 'upon the Messenger of Allah' - it is known that 'Messenger' is more specific than 'Prophet'; because the meaning of Messenger is: a human to whom a law is revealed to be acted upon and he is commanded to convey it. The meaning of Prophet is: a human to whom a law is revealed to be acted upon even if he is not commanded to convey it. This is the well-known view. And it is said they are synonymous. And other views are mentioned, as we have explained elsewhere. His saying: 'the best of those who guided' meaning the best of those who guided people to Allah the Exalted. And if he is the best of those mentioned, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, then he is even more so the best of others. His saying: 'the trustworthy' plural of 'thiqa' meaning reliable. His saying: 'the followers of the paths of salvation' meaning those who follow the ways of salvation; I mean the means that lead to it, such as commanding good and forbidding evil, and such as prayer, fasting, zakat, etc., from all commanded and forbidden acts. In the author's words there is a metaphor by implication, so the author likened salvation to something having material paths, omitted the tenor, and alluded to it by something from its concomitants, which is 'paths'. And it is valid that there is an explicit metaphor, so he likened the ways of salvation, which are the mentioned matters, to material paths, and used the word of the vehicle for the tenor. In any case, 'following' is a reinforcement.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening of the final poetic lines of the Sullam al-Munawraq, defining key terms and explaining rhetorical figures. The author first explains that 'thumma' indicates sequential mention in the poem, and 'sarmada' means perpetual. Then he clarifies the well-known distinction between 'Rasul' (Messenger) and 'Nabi' (Prophet): a Messenger receives a law and is commanded to convey it, while a Prophet receives a law but may not be commanded to convey it. This makes 'Rasul' more specific. Alternative views (synonymy or other) are noted. The phrase 'khayr man hada' (best of those who guided) is explained as the best guide to Allah, implying by priority that the Prophet is the best of all. Then 'thiqa' is defined as reliable, and 'salikina subul al-najat' is explained as those who follow the means of salvation, such as enjoining good, forbidding evil, and acts of worship. Finally, the author analyzes the metaphor in the author's (al-Sullam's) phrasing: either a metaphor by implication (likening salvation to a physical thing with paths) or an explicit metaphor (likening the ways of salvation to physical paths). The term 'suluk' (following) is a reinforcement of the metaphorical image.

Key Terms
الرسول
النبي

D) Both are exactly the same.

Key Takeaway
The command to convey is the key differentia that makes a messenger more specific than a prophet.

The Means of Salvation (Subul al-Najat)
Author explains the meaning of 'subul al-najat' and lists the means.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the acts that constitute the paths of salvation and explain how they lead to salvation.

Core Idea
Salvation is attained through following the commands and avoiding prohibitions—the 'paths' in this metaphor.

The commentary unpacks the phrase 'salikina subul al-najat' by specifying the means: commanding good, forbidding evil, prayer, fasting, zakat, and all acts of obedience. These are the concrete ways a believer travels toward Allah's pleasure. Understanding this list helps a Muslim prioritize actions that lead to salvation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the phrase 'subul al-najat' in the text
Find the Arabic phrase 'سُبُلَ النَّجَاةِ' and note the context: it is part of the description of the Prophet's companions (الصَّالِحِيْنَ سُبُلَ النَّجَاةِ).
2
Identify the examples given
The author lists: 'الأَمْرِ بِالمَعْرُوفِ وَالنَّهْيِ عَنِ المُنْكَرِ', and 'الصَّلَاةِ وَالصَّوْمِ وَالزَّكَاةِ' and then says 'إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ مِنْ سَائِرِ المَأمُورَاتِ وَالمَنْهِيَّاتِ'. So the means include both specific acts and all commands and prohibitions.
The list is not exhaustive but representative.
3
Understand the connection to salvation
The author explicitly says these are 'الأُمُورَ المُوَصِّلَةَ إِلَيْهَا' (the means that lead to salvation). They are not just arbitrary acts but divinely ordained ways to achieve salvation.
Salvation (al-najat) here likely refers to deliverance from punishment and attainment of Paradise.
4
Recognize the comprehensive nature
The phrase 'إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ' indicates that the means encompass all commanded and forbidden acts. So any act of obedience or avoidance of sin is a path to salvation.
This aligns with the Islamic concept that every good deed is rewarded.

His saying: 'upon the Messenger of Allah' - it is known that 'Messenger' is more specific than 'Prophet'; because the meaning of Messenger is: a human to whom a law is revealed to be acted upon and he is commanded to convey it. Metaphorical Analysis of 'Subul al-Najat'
Author analyzes the metaphor in 'salikina subul al-najat'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the two types of metaphor (isti'ara bi al-kinaya and isti'ara tashrihiyya) in the phrase 'salikina subul al-najat'.

Core Idea
The author uses a double metaphor: one implicit, one explicit, both likening salvation's means to physical paths.

The commentator notes that the phrase 'salikina subul al-najat' contains a metaphor. He presents two possible analyses: either a metaphor by implication (kinaya) where the tenor (salvation) is likened to something with physical paths, or an explicit metaphor (tashrihiyya) where the means of salvation are directly called paths. This rhetorical device enriches the meaning and is a common feature in classical Arabic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two elements of the metaphor
The tenor (mushabbah) is 'the means of salvation' (al-umur al-muwasila). The vehicle (mushabbah bihi) is 'physical paths' (subul al-hissiyya). The phrase 'subul al-najat' combines them.
In Arabic rhetoric, a metaphor involves a comparison between a tenor and a vehicle.
2
Understand the first analysis (isti'ara bi al-kinaya)
In this type, the author omits the vehicle and only mentions a concomitant. Here, salvation is implicitly compared to a tangible thing that has paths, but only 'paths' is mentioned. The word 'subul' alludes to the vehicle.
This is a subtle metaphor that requires inference.
3
Understand the second analysis (isti'ara tashrihiyya)
In this explicit metaphor, the author directly uses the word 'subul' to refer to the means of salvation. The tenor (means of salvation) is directly called 'subul'. This is a more overt comparison.
The vehicle is explicitly stated as the metaphor.
4
Identify the reinforcement (tarshih)
The word 'suluk' (following) is a tarshih, a reinforcing element that extends the metaphor. It continues the image of walking on a path.
Tarshih is a rhetorical device that strengthens the metaphor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (ثُمَّ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ ... إلخ) «ثُمَّ» لِلتَّرْتِيبِ الذِّكْرِي . وَقَوْلُهُ : (سَرْمَدَا) أَيْ : دَائِماً . وَقَوْلُهُ : (عَلَى رَسُولِ اللَّهِ مِنَ المَعْلُومِ : - أَنَّ الرَّسُولَ» أَخَصُّ مِنَ النَّبِيِّ»؛ لِأَنَّ مَعْنَى الرَّسُولِ : إِنْسَانُ أُوحِيَ إِلَيْهِ بِشَرْعِ يُعْمَلُ بِهِ وَأُمِرَ بِتَبْلِيغِهِ»، وَمَعْنَى النَّبِيِّ: «إِنْسَانُ أُوحِيَ إِلَيْهِ بِشَرْعِ يُعْمَلُ بِهِ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُؤمَرُ بِتَبْلِيغِهِ»؛ هَذَا هُوَ المَشْهُورُ . - وَقِيلَ : إِنَّهُمَا مُتَرَادِفَانِ . - وَقِيلَ غَيْرَ ذَلِكَ ، كَمَا أَوْضَحْنَاهُ فِي غَيْرِ هَذَا المَحَلِّ . قَوْلُهُ : (خَيْرِ مَنْ هَدَى أَيْ : خَيْرِ مَنْ هَدَى النَّاسَ إِلَى اللهِ تَعَالَى، وَإِذَا كَانَ خَيْرٌ مَنْ ذُكِرَ، صلى الله عليه وسلم كَانَ خَيْرَ غَيْرِهِ بِالْأَوْلَى. قَوْلُهُ : (الثَّقَاتِ) جَمْعُ: «ثِقَةٍ»؛ بِمَعْنَى : المَوْلُوقِ بِهِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (الصَّالِحِيْنَ سُبُلَ النَّجَاةِ أَي : المُتَتَّبِّعِينَ طُرُقَ النَّجَاةِ ؛ أَعْنِي : الأُمُورَ المُوَصِّلَةَ إِلَيْهَا كَـ: الأَمْرِ بِالمَعْرُوفِ وَالنَّهْيِ عَنِ المُنْكَرِ ، وَكَـ : الصَّلَاةِ وَالصَّوْمِ وَالزَّكَاةِ، .. إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ مِنْ سَائِرِ المَأمُورَاتِ وَالمَنْهِيَّاتِ . وَفِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ بِالكِنَايَةِ، فَيَكُونُ المُصَنِّفُ قَدْ شَبَّهَ النَّجَاةَ بِشَيْءٍ لَهُ سُبُل حِرِّيَّةٌ، وَطَوَى لَفْظَ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ، وَرَمَزَ إِلَيْهِ بِشَيْءٍ مِنْ لَوَازِمِهِ وَهُوَ السُّبُلُ . وَيَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ فِيهِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ تَصْرِيحِيَّةٌ، فَيَكُونُ قَدْ شَبَّهَ طُرُقَ النَّجَاةِ الَّتِي هِيَ الأُمُورُ المَذْكُورَةُ بِالسُّبُلِ الحِرِّيَّةِ ، وَاسْتَعَارَ لَفْظَ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ لِلمُشَبَّهِ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ فَالسُّلُوكُ تَرْشِيحٌ.
His saying: 'Then prayers and peace... etc.' - 'thumma' is for sequential mention. His saying: 'perpetually' meaning always. His saying: 'upon the Messenger of Allah' - it is known that 'Messenger' is more specific than 'Prophet'; because the meaning of Messenger is: a human to whom a law is revealed to be acted upon and he is commanded to convey it. The meaning of Prophet is: a human to whom a law is revealed to be acted upon even if he is not commanded to convey it. This is the well-known view. And it is said they are synonymous. And other views are mentioned, as we have explained elsewhere. His saying: 'the best of those who guided' meaning the best of those who guided people to Allah the Exalted. And if he is the best of those mentioned, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, then he is even more so the best of others. His saying: 'the trustworthy' plural of 'thiqa' meaning reliable. His saying: 'the followers of the paths of salvation' meaning those who follow the ways of salvation; I mean the means that lead to it, such as commanding good and forbidding evil, and such as prayer, fasting, zakat, etc., from all commanded and forbidden acts. In the author's words there is a metaphor by implication, so the author likened salvation to something having material paths, omitted the tenor, and alluded to it by something from its concomitants, which is 'paths'. And it is valid that there is an explicit metaphor, so he likened the ways of salvation, which are the mentioned matters, to material paths, and used the word of the vehicle for the tenor. In any case, 'following' is a reinforcement.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening of the final poetic lines of the Sullam al-Munawraq, defining key terms and explaining rhetorical figures. The author first explains that 'thumma' indicates sequential mention in the poem, and 'sarmada' means perpetual. Then he clarifies the well-known distinction between 'Rasul' (Messenger) and 'Nabi' (Prophet): a Messenger receives a law and is commanded to convey it, while a Prophet receives a law but may not be commanded to convey it. This makes 'Rasul' more specific. Alternative views (synonymy or other) are noted. The phrase 'khayr man hada' (best of those who guided) is explained as the best guide to Allah, implying by priority that the Prophet is the best of all. Then 'thiqa' is defined as reliable, and 'salikina subul al-najat' is explained as those who follow the means of salvation, such as enjoining good, forbidding evil, and acts of worship. Finally, the author analyzes the metaphor in the author's (al-Sullam's) phrasing: either a metaphor by implication (likening salvation to a physical thing with paths) or an explicit metaphor (likening the ways of salvation to physical paths). The term 'suluk' (following) is a reinforcement of the metaphorical image.

استعارة
كناية
تشريحية
ترشيح

D) Kinaya is used for abstract concepts; tashrihiyya for concrete ones.

The author's metaphorical analysis shows how rhetorical figures can convey deeper meanings about salvation.

The Metonymy for Perpetuity in Arabic Logic
Author explains time clause with zodiac signs and lists their names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the metonymy (kinaya) used in the Sullam al-Munawraq for perpetuity, including its grammatical structure and purpose.

Core Idea
'As long as the sun cuts through the zodiacs' is a kinaya for 'forever'.

In the closing lines of the Sullam al-Munawraq, a poetic expression signals that a conclusion holds for all eternity. The commentator unpacks this by explaining the temporal clause and the metonymy behind it. Understanding this helps us see how logicians used Arabic rhetorical devices to express universal quantifiers over time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporal clause
Find the phrases 'ma qata'at shams al-nahar abruja' and 'wa tala'a al-badr al-munir fi al-duja' in the Arabic.
2
Analyze the particle 'ma'
Note that 'ma' is a circumstantial verbal noun (masdariyya zarfiyya) indicating time.
3
Recognize the metonymy
The expression is not literal but a kinaya for all time periods.
4
Relate to the logical argument
This shows that the claim in the poem is timeless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (مَا قَطَعَتْ شَمْسُ النَّهَارِ ... إلخ) أَيْ : مُدَّةُ قَطْعِ شَمْسِ النَّهَارِ ... إلخ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَطَلَعَ البَدْرُ ... إلخ) أَيْ : وَمُدَّهُ طُلُوعِ البَدْرِ ... إلخ، فَ «مَا » مَصْدَرِيَّةٌ ظَرْفِيَّةٌ وَالغَرَضُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ : التَّعْمِيمُ فِي جَمِيعِ الأَوْقَاتِ عَلَى طَرِيقِ الكِنَايَةِ، كَمَا هُوَ عَادَةُ العَرَبِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (أَبْرُجَا) جَمْعُ : بُرْج)» ، وَهُوَ وَإِنْ كَانَ جَمْعَ قِلَّةٍ ، لَكِنَّ المُرَادُ مِنْهُ الكَثْرَةَ؛ لِأَنَّهَا اثْنَا عَشَرَ : الحَمْلُ، وَالتَّورُ ، وَالجَوْزَاءُ، وَالسَّرَطانُ، وَالأَسَدُ ، وَالسُّنْبُلَةُ ، وَالمِيزَانُ، وَالعَقْرَبُ، وَالقَوْسُ، وَالجَدِي ، وَالدَّلْو - وَيُقَالُ لَهُ : الدَّانِيُّ (١) - ، وَالحُوتُ. وَقَدْ أَشَارَ لِذَلِكَ بَعْضُهُمْ بِقَوْلِهِ : حَمَلَ النَّوْرُ جَوْزَةَ السَّرَطَانِ وَرَعَى اللَّيْتُ سُنْبُلَ المِيزَانِ وَرَمَى عَفْرَبٌ بِقَوْسٍ لِجَدِي نَزَحَ الدَّلْوَ بِرْكَةَ الحِيتَانِ وَهَذِهِ الأَبْرُجُ هِيَ أَجْزَاءُ دَائِرَةِ الفَلَكِ النَّامِنِ؛ الَّذِي هُوَ فَلَكُ الثَّوَابِتِ وَهُوَ المُسَمَّى بـ «الكُرْسِيِّ»، وَتَوْضُيحُ ذَلِكَ : أَنَّ الحُكَمَاءَ فَسَّمُوا الفَلَكَ المَذْكُورَ اثْنَي عَشَرَ قِسْماً ، وَهِيَ الأَبْرُجُ المَذْكُورَةُ، ثُمَّ إِنَّ الشَّمْسَ لَا تُفَارِقُ مُسَامَةَ هَذِهِ الدَّائِرَةِ أَصْلاً مَعَ كَوْنِهَا فِي فَلَكِهَا الَّذِي هُوَ السَّمَاءُ الرَّابِعَةُ، فَإِذَا فَارَقَتْ بُرْجاً مِنْ تِلْكِ الأَبْرَاجِ، وَابْتَدَأَتْ فِي مُسَامَةِ مَا يَلِيهِ يُقَالُ: «قَطَعَتْ بُرْجَ كَذَا، وَحَلَّتْ فِي بُرْحِ كَذَا ، .. وَهَكَذَا». وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ المُرَادَ أَنَّهَا تَقْطَعُ ذَلِكَ بِسَيْرِهَا الذَّاتِي، وَهُوَ سَيْرُهَا إِلَى جِهَةِ المَشْرِقِ، لَا بِسَيْرِهَا العَرَضِي وَهُوَ سَيْرُهَا إِلَى جِهَةِ المَغْرِبِ، وَهُوَ الظَّاهِرُ لَنَا . وَوَجْهُ تَسْمِيَتِهِ بِذَلِكَ أَنَّهُ عَرَضٌ لَهَا (٢) مِنْ حَرَكَةِ الفَلَكِ الأَعْظَمِ (۳) ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَتَحَرَّكُ بِحَرَكَتِهِ (٤) جَمِيعُ مَا احْتَوَى عَلَيْهِ مِنَ الأَفْلَاكِ وَمَا فِيهَا مِنَ الكَوَاكِبِ. هَذَا؛ وَقَدِ اخْتُلِفَ فِي المُرَادِ بـ «البُرُوحِ فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَلَقَدْ جَعَلْنَا فِي السَّمَاءِ بُرُوجًا وَزَيَّنَهَا النظرينَ [الحجر: ١٦] عَلَى أَقْوَالٍ ؛ أَحْسَنُهَا مَا قَالَهُ عَطِيَّة مِنَ : أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِهَا قُصُورٌ فِي السَّمَاءِ (٥) . ﴾ وَقَالَ مُجَاهِدُ : المُرَادُ بِهَا النُّجُومُ العِظَامُ . وَقَالَ أَبُو إِسْحَاقَ النُّجُومُ السَّبْعَةُ الَّتِي هِيَ الكَوَاكِبُ السَّيَّارَةُ، وَهِيَ : زُحَلُ وَهُوَ فِي السَّمَاءِ
His saying: 'As long as the sun of daytime cuts through the zodiacs... etc.' meaning: the duration of the sun of daytime cutting through... etc. And his saying: 'and the bright full moon rises in the darkness...' meaning: the duration of the full moon rising... etc. So 'ma' is a circumstantial verbal noun and the purpose of that is generalization over all times by way of metonymy, as is the custom of the Arabs. His saying: 'abruja' is the plural of 'burj', and although it is a plural of paucity, the intended meaning here is abundance; because they are twelve: Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpius, Sagittarius, Capricornus, Aquarius—also called 'al-Dani'—and Pisces. Some have alluded to that by their saying: 'The Lamb carried the Walnut of the Crab, and the Lion grazed the Ear of Balance, and a Scorpion shot an Arrow at a Kid, and drew water from the Bucket in the Fishpond.' And these zodiacs are the divisions of the circle of the great sphere, which is the sphere of the fixed stars and is called 'al-Kursi'. The explanation of that is that the philosophers divided that mentioned sphere into twelve parts, and they are the mentioned zodiacs. Then the sun never leaves alignment with this circle at all, while being in its own sphere which is the fourth heaven. So when it leaves one zodiac from those zodiacs and begins in alignment with what follows it, it is said: 'It cut through such-and-such zodiac, and settled in such-and-such zodiac', and so on. Know that what is meant is that it cuts through that by its essential motion, which is its motion towards the east, not by its accidental motion which is its motion towards the west, and that is what is apparent to us. The reason for its being so named is that it is accidental to it from the motion of the greatest sphere; because by its motion all that it contains of spheres and what is in them of planets moves. This is it; and there is disagreement over what is meant by 'al-buruj' in the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And We have placed in the heaven zodiacs and adorned it for observers' [al-Hijr: 16]. Among the opinions, the best is what 'Atiyya said: that what is meant is palaces in the heaven. Mujahid said: what is meant are the great stars. Abu Ishaq said: the seven planets, which are the wandering stars, and they are: Saturn, and it is in the heaven...
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage explains the poetic expression for perpetuity in the closing lines of the Sullam al-Munawraq. The author first clarifies that the phrase 'ma qata'at shams al-nahar abruja' is a temporal clause meaning 'as long as the sun cuts through the zodiacs' and 'wa tala'a al-badr al-munir fi al-duja' means 'as long as the bright moon rises in the darkness'. The particle 'ma' is a verbal noun indicating time, and the whole expression is a metonymy for 'forever', a common Arabic rhetorical device. The commentator then focuses on the term 'abruj' (zodiacal signs), noting it is a plural of paucity but used for abundance since there are exactly twelve. He lists them: Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpius, Sagittarius, Capricornus, Aquarius (also called al-Dani), and Pisces. He even provides a mnemonic poem in Arabic that strings together the names in a whimsical narrative. Next, he gives an astronomical explanation: the zodiacs are the divisions of the sphere of fixed stars (al-Kursi). The sun does not leave the plane of the ecliptic (its apparent path through the zodiacs); its motion through them is its 'essential motion' eastward, not the apparent daily westward motion caused by the rotation of the outermost sphere. Finally, he discusses exegetical opinions on the Qur'anic term 'buruj' in Surah al-Hijr: Ibn 'Atiyya says it means palaces in heaven; Mujahid says the great stars; Abu Ishaq says the seven planets (wandering stars) including Saturn. This shows the author's integration of logic, astronomy, and tafsir.

Key Takeaway

The Twelve Zodiac Signs in Islamic Astronomy and Tafsir
Author explains time clause with zodiac signs and lists their names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the twelve zodiac signs and explain the astronomical and exegetical significance of the term 'buruj' as discussed in the commentary.

Core Idea
The zodiac signs are fixed star divisions, and 'buruj' in the Qur'an has multiple interpretations including palaces, great stars, and planets.

After explaining the temporal expression, the commentator lists the twelve zodiac signs and provides a mnemonic poem. He then explains the sun's essential motion through these signs and discusses exegetical views on the Qur'anic word 'buruj'. This shows how Islamic scholars integrated astronomy with tafsir.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the twelve zodiac signs
Use the mnemonic poem to remember the sequence.
2
Understand the astronomical context
The zodiacs are 30-degree divisions of the sphere of fixed stars (al-Kursi).
3
Distinguish solar motions
Essential motion (eastward) vs. accidental motion (westward due to outermost sphere).
4
Analyze tafsir opinions
Compare 'Atiyya (palaces), Mujahid (great stars), and Abu Ishaq (seven planets).

Source Text

قَوْلُهُ : (مَا قَطَعَتْ شَمْسُ النَّهَارِ ... إلخ) أَيْ : مُدَّةُ قَطْعِ شَمْسِ النَّهَارِ ... إلخ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَطَلَعَ البَدْرُ ... إلخ) أَيْ : وَمُدَّهُ طُلُوعِ البَدْرِ ... إلخ، فَ «مَا » مَصْدَرِيَّةٌ ظَرْفِيَّةٌ وَالغَرَضُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ : التَّعْمِيمُ فِي جَمِيعِ الأَوْقَاتِ عَلَى طَرِيقِ الكِنَايَةِ، كَمَا هُوَ عَادَةُ العَرَبِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (أَبْرُجَا) جَمْعُ : بُرْج)» ، وَهُوَ وَإِنْ كَانَ جَمْعَ قِلَّةٍ ، لَكِنَّ المُرَادُ مِنْهُ الكَثْرَةَ؛ لِأَنَّهَا اثْنَا عَشَرَ : الحَمْلُ، وَالتَّورُ ، وَالجَوْزَاءُ، وَالسَّرَطانُ، وَالأَسَدُ ، وَالسُّنْبُلَةُ ، وَالمِيزَانُ، وَالعَقْرَبُ، وَالقَوْسُ، وَالجَدِي ، وَالدَّلْو - وَيُقَالُ لَهُ : الدَّانِيُّ (١) - ، وَالحُوتُ. وَقَدْ أَشَارَ لِذَلِكَ بَعْضُهُمْ بِقَوْلِهِ : حَمَلَ النَّوْرُ جَوْزَةَ السَّرَطَانِ وَرَعَى اللَّيْتُ سُنْبُلَ المِيزَانِ وَرَمَى عَفْرَبٌ بِقَوْسٍ لِجَدِي نَزَحَ الدَّلْوَ بِرْكَةَ الحِيتَانِ وَهَذِهِ الأَبْرُجُ هِيَ أَجْزَاءُ دَائِرَةِ الفَلَكِ النَّامِنِ؛ الَّذِي هُوَ فَلَكُ الثَّوَابِتِ وَهُوَ المُسَمَّى بـ «الكُرْسِيِّ»، وَتَوْضُيحُ ذَلِكَ : أَنَّ الحُكَمَاءَ فَسَّمُوا الفَلَكَ المَذْكُورَ اثْنَي عَشَرَ قِسْماً ، وَهِيَ الأَبْرُجُ المَذْكُورَةُ، ثُمَّ إِنَّ الشَّمْسَ لَا تُفَارِقُ مُسَامَةَ هَذِهِ الدَّائِرَةِ أَصْلاً مَعَ كَوْنِهَا فِي فَلَكِهَا الَّذِي هُوَ السَّمَاءُ الرَّابِعَةُ، فَإِذَا فَارَقَتْ بُرْجاً مِنْ تِلْكِ الأَبْرَاجِ، وَابْتَدَأَتْ فِي مُسَامَةِ مَا يَلِيهِ يُقَالُ: «قَطَعَتْ بُرْجَ كَذَا، وَحَلَّتْ فِي بُرْحِ كَذَا ، .. وَهَكَذَا». وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ المُرَادَ أَنَّهَا تَقْطَعُ ذَلِكَ بِسَيْرِهَا الذَّاتِي، وَهُوَ سَيْرُهَا إِلَى جِهَةِ المَشْرِقِ، لَا بِسَيْرِهَا العَرَضِي وَهُوَ سَيْرُهَا إِلَى جِهَةِ المَغْرِبِ، وَهُوَ الظَّاهِرُ لَنَا . وَوَجْهُ تَسْمِيَتِهِ بِذَلِكَ أَنَّهُ عَرَضٌ لَهَا (٢) مِنْ حَرَكَةِ الفَلَكِ الأَعْظَمِ (۳) ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَتَحَرَّكُ بِحَرَكَتِهِ (٤) جَمِيعُ مَا احْتَوَى عَلَيْهِ مِنَ الأَفْلَاكِ وَمَا فِيهَا مِنَ الكَوَاكِبِ. هَذَا؛ وَقَدِ اخْتُلِفَ فِي المُرَادِ بـ «البُرُوحِ فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَلَقَدْ جَعَلْنَا فِي السَّمَاءِ بُرُوجًا وَزَيَّنَهَا النظرينَ [الحجر: ١٦] عَلَى أَقْوَالٍ ؛ أَحْسَنُهَا مَا قَالَهُ عَطِيَّة مِنَ : أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِهَا قُصُورٌ فِي السَّمَاءِ (٥) . ﴾ وَقَالَ مُجَاهِدُ : المُرَادُ بِهَا النُّجُومُ العِظَامُ . وَقَالَ أَبُو إِسْحَاقَ النُّجُومُ السَّبْعَةُ الَّتِي هِيَ الكَوَاكِبُ السَّيَّارَةُ، وَهِيَ : زُحَلُ وَهُوَ فِي السَّمَاءِ
His saying: 'As long as the sun of daytime cuts through the zodiacs... etc.' meaning: the duration of the sun of daytime cutting through... etc. And his saying: 'and the bright full moon rises in the darkness...' meaning: the duration of the full moon rising... etc. So 'ma' is a circumstantial verbal noun and the purpose of that is generalization over all times by way of metonymy, as is the custom of the Arabs. His saying: 'abruja' is the plural of 'burj', and although it is a plural of paucity, the intended meaning here is abundance; because they are twelve: Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpius, Sagittarius, Capricornus, Aquarius—also called 'al-Dani'—and Pisces. Some have alluded to that by their saying: 'The Lamb carried the Walnut of the Crab, and the Lion grazed the Ear of Balance, and a Scorpion shot an Arrow at a Kid, and drew water from the Bucket in the Fishpond.' And these zodiacs are the divisions of the circle of the great sphere, which is the sphere of the fixed stars and is called 'al-Kursi'. The explanation of that is that the philosophers divided that mentioned sphere into twelve parts, and they are the mentioned zodiacs. Then the sun never leaves alignment with this circle at all, while being in its own sphere which is the fourth heaven. So when it leaves one zodiac from those zodiacs and begins in alignment with what follows it, it is said: 'It cut through such-and-such zodiac, and settled in such-and-such zodiac', and so on. Know that what is meant is that it cuts through that by its essential motion, which is its motion towards the east, not by its accidental motion which is its motion towards the west, and that is what is apparent to us. The reason for its being so named is that it is accidental to it from the motion of the greatest sphere; because by its motion all that it contains of spheres and what is in them of planets moves. This is it; and there is disagreement over what is meant by 'al-buruj' in the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And We have placed in the heaven zodiacs and adorned it for observers' [al-Hijr: 16]. Among the opinions, the best is what 'Atiyya said: that what is meant is palaces in the heaven. Mujahid said: what is meant are the great stars. Abu Ishaq said: the seven planets, which are the wandering stars, and they are: Saturn, and it is in the heaven...
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage explains the poetic expression for perpetuity in the closing lines of the Sullam al-Munawraq. The author first clarifies that the phrase 'ma qata'at shams al-nahar abruja' is a temporal clause meaning 'as long as the sun cuts through the zodiacs' and 'wa tala'a al-badr al-munir fi al-duja' means 'as long as the bright moon rises in the darkness'. The particle 'ma' is a verbal noun indicating time, and the whole expression is a metonymy for 'forever', a common Arabic rhetorical device. The commentator then focuses on the term 'abruj' (zodiacal signs), noting it is a plural of paucity but used for abundance since there are exactly twelve. He lists them: Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpius, Sagittarius, Capricornus, Aquarius (also called al-Dani), and Pisces. He even provides a mnemonic poem in Arabic that strings together the names in a whimsical narrative. Next, he gives an astronomical explanation: the zodiacs are the divisions of the sphere of fixed stars (al-Kursi). The sun does not leave the plane of the ecliptic (its apparent path through the zodiacs); its motion through them is its 'essential motion' eastward, not the apparent daily westward motion caused by the rotation of the outermost sphere. Finally, he discusses exegetical opinions on the Qur'anic term 'buruj' in Surah al-Hijr: Ibn 'Atiyya says it means palaces in heaven; Mujahid says the great stars; Abu Ishaq says the seven planets (wandering stars) including Saturn. This shows the author's integration of logic, astronomy, and tafsir.

Key Terms
البُرْج
السَّيْر الذَّاتِي
الفَلَك الأَعْظَم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, which of the following is NOT an interpretation of 'buruj' in Surah al-Hijr?

B) Great stars.
C) The seven planets.
D) The twelve zodiac signs as distinct palaces.

Key Takeaway
The term 'buruj' in the Qur'an is interpreted variously, with the commentator noting the best opinion as that of 'Atiyya.

Dissecting the Definition of Praise
Commentator defines al-hamd linguistically and expands its genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentiae in the linguistic definition of al-hamd and explain why each part is necessary.

Core Idea
Genuine praise is description of beauty for its own sake, not neutral information or blame.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the linguistic definition of al-hamd (praise) as 'description with beauty upon beauty.' Each component serves a precise logical function: the genus identifies what kind of thing it is, and the two differentiae separate it from other kinds of description. Understanding this dissection is essential for grasping how Muslim scholars define key terms in theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the definition components.
Find the term being defined (al-hamd) and break the definition into its parts: 'description' (genus), 'with beauty' (first differentia), 'upon beauty' (second differentia).
2
Extract the genus (jins).
The genus is 'description' (al-waṣf). It is the broader category that includes all kinds of description: beautiful, ugly, or neutral.
The genus must be broad enough to encompass all instances but not so broad as to include unrelated things.
3
Analyze the first differentia: 'with beauty'.
This phrase excludes descriptions that are not beautiful, such as neutral descriptions (e.g., 'Zayd is a Tamimi') or ugly ones. Only descriptions that involve beauty qualify as praise.
This differentia narrows the genus to descriptions that mention beauty.
4
Analyze the second differentia: 'upon beauty'.
This phrase excludes praising something ugly due to its ugliness (which would be blame). It specifies that the beauty described must be inherent in the subject, not just any beauty.
Praise is for the beauty itself, not in exchange for ugliness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ «الحَمْدُ» لُغَةٌ هُوَ : «الوَصْفُ بِالجَمِيلِ عَلَى الجَمِيلِ، قوله : (هُوَ : الوَصْفُ) هو جنس في الحدّ شامل للوصف بالجميل، وبالقبيح، وبغيرهما. قوله: (بِالجَمِيل) مخرج للوصف بغير الجميل، وللوصف بما ليس جميلاً ولا قبيحاً ك: المقصود به مجرد الإخبار ; كقولك: «زَيْدٌ تَمِيمِيٌّ». قوله: (عَلَى الجَمِيلِ ) أي : الحسن، مخرج للثَّناء باللسان على القبيح; أي: في مقابلته، فهو ذمّ.
Then al-hamd, linguistically, is: description with beauty upon beauty. His saying: (it is description) is a genus in the definition encompassing description with beauty, with ugliness, and with other than them. His saying: (with beauty) excludes description with other than beauty, and description with what is neither beautiful nor ugly, like: the intended with it is mere informing, as in your saying: 'Zayd is a Tamimi.' His saying: (upon beauty) i.e. the good, excludes praise with the tongue upon ugliness; i.e. in return for it, so it is blame.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion (sharḥ) of the word 'al-ḥamd' (praise). The commentator first gives the lexical definition: it is 'description with beauty upon beauty'. Then he unpacks each part. 'Description' (al-waṣf) is the genus (jins) of the definition; it includes all kinds of description—beautiful, ugly, or neutral. The phrase 'with beauty' (bil-jamīl) is the first differentia that excludes descriptions that are not beautiful, and also excludes neutral descriptions that are merely informative, like 'Zayd is a Tamimi'—such statements are not praise. The phrase 'upon beauty' ('alā l-jamīl) is the second differentia that excludes praising something ugly with the tongue; praising something ugly because of its ugliness is actually blame (dhamm), not praise. Thus the definition precisely distinguishes genuine praise from mere description and from blame. The explanation uses only Arabic linguistic and Islamic conceptual examples, consistent with the tradition.

Key Terms
الحمد
الوصف
الجنس في الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The logical structure of a definition—genus plus differentiating qualities—ensures that only genuine praise is captured.

The Five Prerequisites of Praise (Ḥamd)
Author enumerates the five prerequisites of praise and explains their status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five prerequisites of praise (ḥamd) and explain why they are not definitional parts of its essence.

Core Idea
Praise has five prerequisites, but they are not parts of its definition.

In this passage, the commentator lays out the five matters upon which praise depends. He emphasizes that these are not parts (ajzāʾ) of praise in a definitional sense, but rather external prerequisites for its realization. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing definitions in logic and rhetoric.

Guided Path
1
Locate the list of five matters
Find the sentence beginning with 'وَهُوَ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى أُمُورٍ خَمْسَةٍ'. This introduces the five prerequisites.
2
Identify each of the five
List them: (1) the form (al-ṣīgha), (2) the praiser (al-ḥāmid), (3) the praised (al-maḥmūd), (4) that by which praise is given (al-maḥmūd bihī), (5) that for which praise is given (al-maḥmūd ʿalayhī).
3
Understand the non-parts status
The commentator clarifies: these are not parts of the essence of praise (e.g., the praiser cannot be a part of praise itself). They are real-world requirements.
This is a key logical point: prerequisites are not constituents.
4
Check the definitions of the fourth and fifth
The fourth is an attribute of perfection (ṣifat kamāl); the fifth is the occasion or motivator. Note the distinction between virtue (faḍīla) and benefaction (fāḍila).
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the phrase 'Al-ḥamdu lillāh'. Identify each of the five elements in this expression as applied by the commentator.
Example given in the passage: form = the words, praiser = the poet, praised = Allah, attribute = the content (praise belongs to Allah), reason = the extraction of results.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على مقدمة الناظم (عَلَى جِهَةِ التَّعْظِيمِ وَالتَّنْجِيلِ). وَهُوَ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى أُمُورٍ خَمْسَةٍ: (1 - 3) - الصّيغَةُ، وَالحَامِدُ، وَالمَحْمُودُ. وَهَذِهِ الثَّلَاثَةُ يَتَضَمَّنُهَا لَفْظُ الوَصْفُ، وَهُوَ خَاصٌ بِاللِّسَانِ. قوله: (عَلَى جِهَةِ التَّعْظِيمِ وَالتَّبْجِيلِ) هذا القيد ليس فصلاً من فصول الماهية، وإنَّما هو شرط: إما لتحقق الحمد، أو للاعتداد به. وإنما اشترط ذلك؛ لأنَّه لو خلا عن مطابقةٍ لم يكن حمداً، بل مداراة؛ كمن مدح شخصاً ببعض محاسنه غير قاصد تعظيمه، بل اتقاء له. وذكر الشَّرط في التعريف الرَّسميّ جائز، كأَنَّه خاصَّةٌ مِن خواصه، فالشرط المذكور إنَّما أتي به لإخراج ما إذا كان الوصف بالجميل على جميل، ولكن لا على جهة عد الموصوف جميلاً، بأن لم يخطر بباله تعظيمه، فإنَّه ليس بحمد. (4) - وَالرَّابِعُ: المَحْمُودُ بِهِ، وَهُوَ: صِفَةُ كَمَالٍ يُدْرِكُ العَقْلُ السَّلِيمُ حُسْنَهَا، وَالمُرَادُ: كَوْنُهُ جَمِيلاً فِي الوَاقِعِ، أَوْ عِنْدَ الحَامِدِ، أَوِ المَحْمُودِ بِزَعْمِ الحَامِدِ. (5) - وَالخَامِسُ: المَحْمُودُ عَلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ: مَا يَقَعُ الوَصْفُ الجَمِيلُ مُقَابِلَهُ أَوْ بِإِزَائِهِ، فَهُوَ كَالبَاعِثِ عَلَى الحَمْدِ. وقد بيّن بعضهم معنى الفضائل والفواضل وأتى بمفرديهما في بيتين، فقال: فَضَائِلُ: صِفَاتُ ذَاتٍ يَا فَتَى فَوَاضِلُ: صِفَاتُ فِعْلٍ قَدْ أَتَى مُفْرَدٌ لِأَوَّلِ أَتَى: «فَضِيلَه» وَالثَّانِ: «فَاضِلَة» خُذِ الوَسِيلَه قوله: (وَهُوَ يَتَوَقَّفُ) يعني: في الواقع، لا أنَّها أجزاء له فإنَّ استحالة كون الحامد والمحمود جزأين مِنَ الحمد ضروريَّة، ولا أنَّه يتوقف حصول الحمد على ذكرها في صيغته كما توهم، بل على وجودها في الواقع. قوله: (عَلَى أُمُورِ خَمْسَةٍ) بيانها هنا: أنَّ الصِّيغة لفظ: «الحَمْدُ للهِ»، والحامد: «النَّاظم»، والمحمود هو: «الله»، والمحمود به مضمون هذه الجملة؛ أي ثبوت الحمد بأسره الله تعالى، والمحمود عليه: إخراج النتائج لأرباب العقول الكاملة. قوله: (وَهُوَ صِفَةُ كَمَالٍ يشمل: الصِّفة الوجوديَّة؛ كقولنا: اللهُ تَعَالَى عَالِمٌ، والسَّلبيَّة؛ كقولنا: لَا شَريكَ لَهُ). قوله: (فِي الوَاقِعِ، أَوْ عِنْدَ الحَامِدِ، أَوِ المَحْمُودِ بِزَعْمِ الحَامِدِ) معناه: سواء كان جميلاً في الشرع، أو في العادة فقط كـ الثَّناء على نهب الأموال، وسفك الدماء ممَّا كان عند العرب محموداً. قوله: (وَهُوَ مَا يَقَعُ) ولا يُفسَّرُ بـ: السَّبب الحامل على الحمد؛ لقصوره، فإنَّ الحمد مِنَ الله تعالى يستحيل أن يكون له سبب حامل ولا يُفَسَّرُ بـ: «ما جعل الحمد بإزائه»؛ لأَنَّ القديم لا يتعلّق به الجعل. قوله: (مُقَابِلُهُ) يعني: إذا كان بسبب الفاضلة وقوله: (بِإِزَائِهِ) بسبب الفضيلة. ثم المحمود به وعليه: قد يتغايران بالذات؛ كأن يحسن إليك زيد، فتقول: زَيْدٌ شُجَاعٌ، أو عَالِمٌ، فالمحمود به: الشَّجاعة أو العلم، والمحمود عليه: الإحسان.
Discourse on the introduction of the author (with exaltation and veneration). It depends on five matters: (1-3) the form, the praiser, the praised. These three are encompassed by the word 'description', and it is specific to the tongue. His statement: 'with exaltation and veneration' — this restriction is not a differentia of the essence, but rather a condition: either for the realization of praise, or for it to be taken into account. It is stipulated because if it were devoid of matching it would not be praise, but rather flattery; like one who praises a person for some of his good qualities not intending his exaltation, but rather to avoid his harm. Mentioning a condition in a definition by description is permissible, as if it were a property of its properties. So the mentioned condition is only brought to exclude when the description with the beautiful is upon a beautiful thing, but not in the manner of deeming the described as beautiful, in that he did not have in mind his exaltation — for that is not praise. (4) The fourth: that by which praise is given, and it is an attribute of perfection that the sound intellect perceives its goodness, and the intent is that it be beautiful in reality, or according to the praiser, or according to the praised in the praiser's claim. (5) The fifth: that for which praise is given, and it is that which the beautiful description corresponds to or is in front of, so it is like the motivator for praise. And some explained the meaning of virtues (fada'il) and benefactions (fawadil) and gave their singulars in two lines: 'Virtues are attributes of essence, O youth; benefactions are attributes of act, indeed. The singular of the first is 'fadilah' and the second is 'fadilah' — take the means.' His statement: 'it depends' means: in reality, not that they are parts of it, for the impossibility of the praiser and the praised being parts of praise is necessary, nor that the occurrence of praise depends on mentioning them in its wording as is imagined, but rather on their existence in reality. His statement: 'on five matters' — their explanation here: the form is the phrase 'Praise be to Allah', the praiser is the author, the praised is Allah, that by which praise is given is the content of this sentence, i.e., the affirmation of all praise to Allah the Exalted, and that for which praise is given is the extraction of results for possessors of complete intellects. His statement: 'and it is an attribute of perfection' includes the affirmative attribute, such as our saying 'Allah the Exalted is Knowing', and the negative attribute, such as our saying 'There is no partner for Him'. His statement: 'in reality, or according to the praiser, or according to the praised in the praiser's claim' means: whether it is beautiful in the Law, or only in custom, like praise for plundering wealth and shedding blood, which was praiseworthy among the Arabs. His statement: 'and it is that which occurs' is not explained as 'the cause that motivates praise' because of its inadequacy, for praise from Allah the Exalted cannot have a motivating cause, nor is it explained as 'that which makes praise in front of it' because the eternal is not subject to making. His statement: 'corresponds to' means if it is due to a benefaction, and his statement: 'is in front of' means due to a virtue. Then that by which praise is given and that for which praise is given may differ in essence; for example, if Zayd does good to you, you say 'Zayd is brave' or 'Zayd is knowledgeable', then that by which praise is given is bravery or knowledge, and that for which praise is given is the good deed.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition and conditions of praise (ḥamd) as conceived in Islamic intellectual tradition, specifically in the context of praising Allah. The text is a commentary on an introductory line of a poem. The author explains that praise requires five things: the form (al-ṣīgha) which is the verbal expression, the praiser (al-ḥāmid), the praised (al-maḥmūd), that by which praise is given (al-maḥmūd bihī) which is an attribute of perfection, and that for which praise is given (al-maḥmūd ʿalayhī) which is the occasion or reason. These five are not parts (ajzāʾ) of praise in a definitional sense, but rather prerequisites for its actualization in reality. The author emphasizes that a key condition is that the praise must be done with exaltation and veneration (taʿẓīm wa tabjīl); without this intention, it is not praise but flattery or jest. This condition is not part of the essence of praise but a external condition. The text then elaborates on the fourth and fifth elements, explaining that the attribute of perfection can be either affirmative (like knowledge) or negative (like lack of partner), and it can be considered beautiful in reality, according to the praiser, or according to the praised as the praiser claims. The fifth element is the motivator for praise, and it can be a virtue (faḍīla) of the essence or a benefaction (fāḍila) of action. The author gives examples: if Zayd does you a favor, you might praise him for his courage (that by which) because of his favor (that for which). The text also clarifies that the condition of exaltation excludes cases where someone says something good but does not intend to glorify the person, perhaps to avoid harm. All examples in the commentary come from Islamic discourse: praising Allah with attributes, and praising someone for a virtuous trait. This is a definitional passage that sets the conceptual framework for understanding praise in logic and rhetoric.
