Metaphor Types and Grammatical Substitution in Arabic Rhetoric
Author explains the rhetorical and grammatical features of the poem's opening line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify metonymical and imaginative metaphors in Arabic texts and distinguish badal ishtimal from badal ba'd min kull.

Core Idea
Metaphors in Arabic rhetoric are classified by the nature of comparison; grammatical substitution depends on the part-whole relationship.

In this passage, the author unpacks the poetic opening line, showing how knowledge is likened to a palace. He distinguishes two metaphor types and clarifies the grammatical substitution at work. Understanding these distinctions is key to analyzing Arabic rhetorical devices and grammatical structures in classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of isti'ara
The author mentions two types: metonymical (بِالكِنَايَةِ) and imaginative (تَخْيِيلِيَّة). Note how each is derived from the comparison of knowledge to a palace. The metonymical relies on the implied palace containing secluded women; the imaginative affirms the veil, a necessary concomitant.
2
Examine the metaphor's components
The metaphor consists of knowledge (المعرفة) compared to a palace (قصر) with secluded women (خدور) and brides (عرائس). The author explains that the mention of 'seeing' is a reinforcement (ترشيح).
3
Analyze the grammatical substitution
The clause 'they saw...' (رأوا) is treated as badal ishtimal from the preceding clause. The prepositional phrase is also badal ishtimal from the preceding genitive. Understand that badal ishtimal implies an inclusive relationship.
4
Evaluate the author's rejection of alternative
The author explicitly rejects that it is badal ba'd min kull. Consider why: the relationship between the clauses is inclusive (one clause contains the meaning of the other) rather than part-whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على مقدمة الناظم - اسْتِعَارَةٌ بِالكِنَايَةِ ؛ لِتَشْبِيهِهِ المَعْرِفَةَ بِقَصْرٍ فِيهِ خُدُورٍ وَفِيهِ عَرَائِسٌ . - وَتَخْيِيلِيَّةٌ ؛ لِإِثْبَاتِهِ الخِدْرَ - الَّذِي هُوَ مِنْ لَوَازِمِ القَصْرِ - لِلْمَعْرِفَةِ، وَذِكْرُ الرُّؤيَةِ : ترشيح. - وَيَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ لَفْظُ المُخَدَّرَاتِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ تَصْرِيحِيَّةٌ ، أَطْلَقَهَا عَلَى المَعَانِي الدَّقِيقَةِ، وَقَرِينَتُهَا الإِضَافَةُ إِلَى ضَمِيرِ المَعْرِفَةِ. وَجُمْلَةُ رَأوا ... إلخ بَدَلُ اشْتِمَالٍ مِنَ الَّتِي قَبْلَهَا ، وَمِنْ الجَارَّةُ لِسَمَاءِ بِمَعْنَى ، وَمَجْرُورُهَا بَدَلُ اشْتِمَالٍ مِنَ المَجْرُورِ قَبْلَهُ، لَا بَدَلَ بَعْضٍ مِنْ كُلِّ كَمَا قِيلَ.
Discussion on the introduction of the author: - It is a metonymical metaphor, because he likens knowledge to a palace in which there are secluded women and in it are brides. - And it is an imaginative metaphor, because he affirms the veil - which is a necessary concomitant of the palace - for knowledge, and the mention of 'seeing' is a reinforcement. - And it is valid for the word 'the secluded ones' to be an explicit metaphor, he applies it to subtle meanings, and its clue is the annexation to the pronoun of knowledge. And the sentence 'they saw... etc.' is a badal ishtimal from the preceding sentence, and 'min' is a preposition for 'samaa' meaning..., and its genitive is a badal ishtimal from the preceding genitive, not a badal ba'd min kull as has been said.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening line of the poem. It first identifies two types of metaphor (isti'ara) used: metonymical (bikinnaya) and imaginative (takhiliyya). The author explains that knowledge is likened to a palace, and the mention of 'seeing' reinforces the metaphor. Then it discusses the possibility of the word 'al-mukhadarat' (the secluded ones) being an explicit metaphor for subtle meanings, with the clue being the annexation to the pronoun of knowledge. The grammatical structure is then analyzed: the clause 'they saw...' is treated as badal ishtimal (an inclusive substitution) from the preceding clause, and the prepositional phrase is also a badal ishtimal. The author emphatically rejects that it is badal ba'd min kull (part-for-whole substitution), as some have claimed. The discussion follows the order of the text, clarifying the rhetorical devices and grammatical choices.

Key Terms
استعارة (isti'ara): A metaphor; here explained as either metonymical (bikinnaya) or imaginative (takhiliyya) based on the comparison of knowledge to a palace. [Note: No significant shift; standard rhetorical term.]
بدل اشتمال (badal ishtimal): An inclusive substitution where the substitute contains the meaning of the substituted, but is not a part of it. [Note: Stable grammatical term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The author uses badal ishtimal to link clauses in a way that emphasizes inclusive relationship rather than part-whole.

The Performative Blessing: Inshā' and Omitting Salām
The commentator explains the meaning of the blessing line and addresses a potential objection about omitting peace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between khabar and inshā' in a phrase and evaluate the permissibility of omitting 'peace' from the blessing based on the commentator's analysis.

Core Idea
The blessing is a supplication, not a statement.

In this passage, the commentator explains that the phrase 'May God bless him' is outwardly a report (khabar) but functionally a performative invocation (inshā'). This distinction is crucial in Islamic linguistics and jurisprudence. He then addresses a common question: why is only 'blessing' mentioned without 'peace'? Understanding this requires insight into the balance between textual precedent and scholarly practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction between khabar and inshā'
In Arabic linguistics, khabar is a statement that can be true or false, while inshā' is an utterance that performs an action (like a promise or prayer). Find the phrase in the Arabic where the commentator says 'لفظه لفظ الخبر ومعناه الإنشاء'.
This is a standard distinction in uṣūl al-fiqh and balāgha.
2
Understand why the blessing is inshā'
The commentator says it is a supplication (du‘ā’) for mercy, seeking nearness to Allah. This is not reportive but performative. Reflect on how the meaning changes if it were simply a report.
3
Analyze the issue of omitting salām
The majority of hadith scholars dislike singling out blessings without peace because the Quran pairs them. Find the commentator's response: he cites a hadith urging blessing, and then offers two possibilities: either the poet combined them verbally but omitted 'peace' by mistake, or he considered it not disliked.
The commentator relies on scholarly precedent (al-Ujhūrī's report) to show that singling out 'peace' was common among early scholars, so singling out 'blessing' is even more acceptable.
4
Evaluate the commentator's resolution
Assess whether the commentator's reasoning is convincing. Does the precedent of singling out 'peace' justify singling out 'blessing'? Consider the strength of the hadith about miserliness and the Quranic command.
This step involves critical thinking about the use of evidence in Islamic legal reasoning.

قوله: (صَلَّى عَلَيْهِ اللهُ) لفظه لفظ الخبر ومعناه الإنشاء؛ أي: الدُّعاء له بالرحمة على وجه التَّقرُّب مِنَ المولى على الاحتياج لها. فإن قلت: كيف أفرد الصَّلاة عن السَّلام وهو مكروه عند جمهور المحدثين؛ كما نص عليه زروق في شرح الوَغْلِيسِيَّةِ؛ لاقترانهما في الآية الكريمة. الكلام على مقدمة الناظم (٢) أَتَى بِالصَّلَاةِ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم عِنْدَ ذِكْرِ اسْمِهِ الشَّرِيفِ؛ لِحَدِيثِ: «البَخِيلُ كُلُّ البَخِيلِ مَنْ ذُكِرْتُ عِنْدَهُ فَلَمْ يُصَلِّ عَلَيَّ»، وَحَدِيثِ: «رَغِمَ أَنْفُ رَجُلٍ ذُكِرْتُ عِنْدَهُ فَلَمْ يُصَلِّ عَلَيَّ» أَخْرَجَهُ التِّرْمِذِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ. وَالإِضَافَةُ فِي بَحْرِ المَعَانِي مِنْ إِضَافَةِ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ إِلَى المُشَبَّهِ؛ أَيْ: يَخُوضُ فِي المَعَانِي الَّتِي هِيَ كَالبَحْرِ فِي اتِّسَاعِ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا اتِّسَاعاً يَحْتَاجُ فِي سُلُوكِهِ إِلَى آلَةٍ، وَذِكْرُ اللُّجَجِ وَالحَوْضِ تَرْشِيحٌ لِلتَّشْبِيهِ. وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ شَبَّهَ فِي نَفْسِهِ الحِجَا الَّذِي هُوَ العَقْلُ بِالسَّفِينَةِ فِي التَّوَصُّلِ بِكُلِّ مِنْهُمَا إِلَى المُرَادِ، وَهَذِهِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ بِالكِنَايَةِ، وَذِكْرُ اللُّجَجِ وَالبَحْرِ اسْتِعَارَةٌ تَخْيِيلِيَّةٌ، وَالمَعَانِي تَجْرِيدٌ، وَهُوَ أَنْ تَذْكُرَ مَا يُلائِمُ المُشَبَّهَ. وَاللُّجَجُ جَمْعُ: «لُجَّةِ»، وَهِيَ: مُعْظَمُ المَاءِ. وَمَعْنَى البَيْتِ: تَأْبِيدُ الصَّلَاةِ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم بِدَوَامِ تَفَكَّرِ العَقْلِ فِي مَسَائِلِ العِلْمِ، وَذَلِكَ بِدَوَامِ العُلَمَاءِ، وَدَوَامُهُمْ لِقِيَامِ السَّاعَةِ. قلت: يحتمل أنَّه قد قرن بينهما لفظاً، وأسقط السَّلام خطأ، أو ذهب على أنَّه لا كراهة في ذلك؛ قال الأجهوري: شاع في كتب المتقدمين من أهل مذهبنا وغيرهم قولهم: «قال عليه السَّلام» ونحوه مقتصرين على «السَّلام»، وأخبرني الثقة أنه رآه بخط الباجي كذلك، وإذا لم يكره إفراد السَّلام فالصَّلاة أَوْلى. قوله: (وَحَدِيثِ: «رَغِمَ» ... إلخ) هذا الحديث أخرجه الترمذي عن أبي هريرة رضي الله تعالى عنه، ومعنى «رغم»: ألصق بالرُّغام؛ أي: التُّراب. قوله: (جَمْعُ: «لُجَّةٍ») أي: بضم اللام، وأما بفتحها فهو: الخصومة واختلاط الأصوات؛ قال الشاعر: [من الرجز] في لُجَةٍ أَمْسِكْ فُلاناً عَنْ فُلِ.
His statement: 'May God bless him' — its wording is indicative (reportive) but its meaning is performative (invocative); i.e., supplication for mercy upon him in a manner of seeking nearness to the Lord, with need for it. If you say: How did he single out 'blessing' from 'peace'? And that is disliked by the majority of hadith scholars, as Zarrūq stated in his commentary on al-Waghlīsiyya, due to their conjunction in the noble verse. The discourse on the poet's introduction (2): He mentioned the blessing upon the Prophet, peace be upon him, upon mentioning his noble name, because of the hadith: 'The miser, the complete miser, is he who is mentioned in my presence and does not bless me,' and the hadith: 'May his nose be rubbed in dust, a man who is mentioned in my presence and does not bless me,' recorded by al-Tirmidhi and others. The genitive construction in 'sea of meanings' is of the imagined to the imaginer; i.e., he delves into meanings that are like a sea in their vastness, each requiring an instrument to traverse. And mentioning 'depths' and 'basins' is amplification (tarshīḥ) of the simile. And it is possible that he, in himself, compared the intellect ('aql) to a ship in attaining the desired goal through each, and this is a metonymic metaphor (isti'āra bil-kīnāya), while mentioning 'depths' and 'sea' is an imaginary metaphor (isti'āra takhyīliyya), and 'meanings' is abstraction (tajrīd), which is to mention what suits the compared. And 'lujaj' is the plural of 'lujja', which is the main body of water. And the meaning of the line: the perpetuation of blessing upon the Prophet, peace be upon him, with the perpetuity of the intellect's reflection in the questions of knowledge, and that is with the perpetuity of scholars, and their perpetuity until the establishment of the Hour. I (the commentator) say: It is possible that he combined them verbally, and omitted 'peace' by mistake, or he held that there is no dislike in that; al-Ujhūrī said: It was common in the books of the early scholars of our school and others that they said 'peace be upon him' and similar, restricting themselves to 'peace', and a reliable person informed me that he saw it in al-Bājī's handwriting likewise. And if singling out 'peace' is not disliked, then 'blessing' is more appropriate. His statement: (And the hadith: 'May his nose be rubbed in dust' etc.) — this hadith was recorded by al-Tirmidhi from Abu Hurayra, may God be pleased with him, and the meaning of 'raghima' is: stuck to the dust (turāb). His statement: (plural of 'lujja') i.e., with a damma on the lām, but with a fatḥa it means 'dispute and mixing of voices'; the poet said: [from rajaz] 'In a dispute, restrain so-and-so from so-and-so.'
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharḥ) of the opening line of al-Sullam al-Murunq. The commentator explains each part of the line: 'May God bless him' is analyzed as a performative invocation (inshā'), not a report (khabar). A question arises about the individual mention of 'blessing' without 'peace', which is considered disliked by many hadith scholars because the Quran pairs them. The commentator then discusses the reason the poet included the blessing: to follow the prophetic hadiths that urge blessing upon the Prophet when his name is mentioned. Next, he delves into the rhetorical figures in the line. 'Sea of meanings' is a metaphorical genitive (idāfa tashbīhiyya), comparing meanings to a sea in vastness, requiring an instrument (like a ship) to traverse. The mention of 'depths' (lujaj) and 'basins' (ḥawḍ) is 'tarshīḥ', amplification of the simile. He also proposes another reading: the intellect (ḥijā) is implicitly compared to a ship (isti'āra bil-kīnāya), while 'depths' and 'sea' are 'isti'āra takhyīliyya' (imaginary metaphor), and 'meanings' is 'tajrīd' (abstraction), which is mentioning what suits the thing compared. The collective meaning of the line is the perpetual blessing upon the Prophet as long as the intellect reflects on knowledge, i.e., as long as scholars exist. The commentator then addresses the earlier question about omitting 'peace', suggesting he either combined them verbally and omitted 'peace' by mistake, or he considered it not disliked. He cites al-Ujhūrī's report that early scholars often used 'peace' alone, so using 'blessing' alone is even more acceptable. Finally, he notes the plural 'lujaj' and the linguistic distinction between 'lujja' (water mass) and 'luja' (dispute).

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the primary function of the phrase 'صلَّى عليه الله'?
A) It is a report (khabar) that God blessed the Prophet.
B) It is a performative supplication (inshā') for mercy.
C) It is a metaphorical expression with no real meaning.
D) It is a command to the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'May God bless him' is an act of supplication, not a mere statement; and the omission of 'peace' is justified by prophetic hadith and scholarly precedent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Analyze the basmala: 'بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم' — is it khabar or inshā'? Support your answer with the commentator's logic.
?
2. In a fiqh text, the phrase 'أنت طالق' is used. Explain why this must be inshā' and not khabar, and what would happen if it were treated as khabar.
